The Red Page
The War
Once again Boyd Cable has written the
real thing. G 'rapes of Wrath (Smith, Elder,
ss. in England) is just the sort of thing that
readers, tired of histories or novels of the
war, are waiting for; and it is done in a
way that confirms the high reputation made
by this author in the war. “My ambition,”
he explains, “was of describing what a Big
Push is like from the point of view of an
ordinary average infantry private....!
have tried to put into words merely the sort
of story that might and could be told by
thousands of our men to-day. I hope, in
fact, I have so ‘told the tale’ that such
men as I have written of may be able to put
this book in your hands and say ‘This chap-
ter just describes our crossing the open,’ or
‘That is how we were shelled,’ or ‘I felt the
same about my Blighty one.’ ” And that is
exactly what Boyd Cable, with a brilliant
realism and a convincing touch, has sincerely
done.
He. takes four men, four ordinary privates,
and tells us what they feel and know while
taking part in a Big Push. To us on-lookers,
fed up with full descriptions of the various
operations, it is amazing how little the actors
actually know of the real drama or their part
in it. “Bloomin’ Blind Man’s Buff, I calls
it. Gropin’ round after ’Uns you can’t see,
an’ gettin’ poked in the ribs without seem’
one—like Billy was,” is how one of the men
describes it. And in a casualty station: "It
was extraordinary how little they each ap-
peared to know of the fight they had taken
such an active part in, how ignorant they
were of how well or ill the action had gone
as a whole. Some talked very positively, but
were promptly questioned or contradicted by
others* just as positive; others confessed
blank ignorance of everything except that
they themselves had stayed in some ditch for
a certain number of hours, or that the bat-
talion had been held up by machine-gun fire;
or that the shelling had been ‘hell.’ ” Even
when the tired and dispirited men in the
trench had the chance of seeing an aeroplane
duel just overhead, they were not allowed to
watch it. A sharp order was passed along
the trench for every man to keep his face
down. “A trench full of white faces lookin’
up at a ’plane,” the sergeant explains, “is
as good as sending a postcard to their spotter
upstairs sayin’ the trench is occupied in
force; and 1 I don’t suppose you’re any more
anxious than I am for that ’Un to be send-
ing a wireless to his guns, and ’avin’ this
trench strafed like the last one was.” The
disgusted men never even knew who won that
fight.
*
Boyd Cable affords us more vivid pictures
of the little things that count. Here is how
a German counter-attack was met. “The
German counter-attack boiled down into a
mere matter of the rapid firing of a very hot
rifle into a dense bank of smoke and a dimly-
seen mass of men. Each man shot straight
to his front.... So each simply shot and shot
and went on shooting at full speed, glancing
over the sights and squeezing the trigger,
jerking the bolt back and up, and pulling
trigger again till the magazine was empty;
then, throwing the butt down to cram a fresh
clip of cartridges into the breech.... Each
man was an automatic machine, pumping out
so many bullets in so many seconds Ken-
tucky began to fear that his stock of cart-
ridges would not last out, began vaguely to
worry over the possibility of having to
shooting even for a minute until bp could
obtain a fresh supply. Pug was filled with
an intense irritation over the behavior of his
rifle, which dn some mysterious fashion de-
veloped a defect in the loading of the last
cartridge from each clip.... He cursed his
rifle and its parts, breech, holt and magazine
severally and distinctly, the cartridges and
the clips, the men and the machinery who had
made each, but at no time did he check the
speed of his shooting to curse.”
And here is exactly how a man dons his
gas helmet: “Through the acrid odors of the
explosives’ fumes Kentucky caught a faint
whiff of a heavy, sickly, sweet scent. In-
stantly he stopped breathing, and hastily
wrenched out the flannel helmet slung in its
special hag by his side, pulled it over his
head, and, clutching its folds tightly round
his throat with one hand, tore open his
jacket collar, stuffed the lower edge of the
flannel inside his jacket and buttoned it up
again. He lay- perfectly quiet, inhaling the
pungent odor of the impregnated flannel, and
peering upward through the eye-pieces for
any visible sign of the gas.” And all through
the homeric adventures of the next half hour
in which Kentucky did enough to win seven
VC.’s he was intensely worried because he
could not identify the peculiar scent of that
gas!
For all along that confused line deeds
were being casually done that no melo-
dramatic playwright would dare to use.
“Among the stock situations of the melo-
drama one of the most worked-to-death is
that of the beleaguered garrison at the last
gasp, and the thrilling arrival of the rescu-
ing force at the critical moment. It is so old
and threadbare now that probably no theatre
would dare stage it; but in the war the same
situation has been played again and again in
the swaying and straining lines of battle in
every variety of large and small scale. W hat
the theatre has rejected as too theatrical,
the artificial as too artificial, the real has
accepted as so much commonplace that it is
hardly remarked. Actually the battle-line is
one long series of critical situations on one
side or the other, the timely arrival, or
failure to arrive, of assistance at the critical
moment. The great difference is that in the
theatre the rescue never fails to arrive, in
war it often does.”
What makes this yarn more thrilling for
us is that the rescuers were Anzacs. And
one hopes that the incident that follows is as
true as it is characteristic. For the Anzacs,
ordered like the English to retire, happening
to see a khaki figure, followed by an officer,
actually advancing, saw nothing but the
necessity for Australia to be there, and un-
officially started an advance that resulted in
the capture of the village. The cause of
this victory was one English soldier who had
potted a German officer wearing a helmet
and wasn’t going back without retrieving
that helmet! His officer followed him, yell-
ing for him to come back. What else could
the Anzacs do, but follow?
Another vivid picture—of a casualty
station. “The work went on at top speed, as
smooth as sweet-running machinery, as fast
and efficiently as the sorting and packing of
goods in a warehouse by a well-drilled and
expert staff. It was curiously like the
handling of merchandise, if you gave your
attention to the figures (of the wounded)
passing down the stairs, moving into line up
to the tables, halting there a few minutes,
moving on again and away. The men might
have been parcels shifting one by one up to
the packers’ tables and away from them. . . .
It was quite different if attention were con-
centrated on the doctor alone and the case
he handled. That brought instant realisa-
tion of the human side, the high skill of the
swiftly-moving fingers, the perfection of
knowledge that directed them, the haste with
which a bandage was stripped off, the ten-
derness that over-rode the haste as the raw
wound was bared, the sure, unhesitating
touch that handled the wound with a maxi-
mum of speed finely adjusted ter a minimum
of hurt....”
The whole grim reality of modern warfare
is here, lit, as is inevitable, by sharp flashes
of humor, rough and simple, but intensely
moving against that hideous background.
One chapter dealing with the tanks is as
humorous as its ludicrous subject. And
there is the same simple masculine pathos
in the concluding chapters. For Boyd Cable
is too sincere a writer to conform to the
formula that always brings a hero out of the
battle unscathed, or at least with one
definite and picturesque wound. Of his
four heroes, two are killed, and the other
two, receiving Blighty wounds, are mangled
for life. And none receives a V.C.
Two Australian Novels
Let us keep our standards clear: these
two novels, it must be admitted, would
hardly merit mention in this page if they
had not been written by Australians. The
policy of this page is to note only those
oversea novels that either represent some
interesting tendency in modern fiction or
that possess outstanding qualities of style
or theme. Such novels as these two would
perforce escape notice among the many ex-
cellent novels produced every year in Eng-
land and the U.S.A. Painted Clay, by Capel
Boake, a niece of Barcroft Boake ('Aus-
tralasian Authors’ Agency, Melb.; 4s.), and
Paul Strange, by Louise Brown, an Adel-
aide woman (Hodder and Stoughton), are
admirable examples of novels of the f.a.q.
standard —that is all. But they rank fairly
high among the novels already produced by
Australians,
Painted Clay is the realistic story of a
Melbourne girl who inherits from her mother
a tendency to love passionately and too well.
But the author needlessly stresses this in-
heritance, since Helen’s one “lapse” might
have occurred to any girl, no matter how
brought up, who found herself by an acci-
dent alone for the night with the unscru-
pulous man she loved. The value of this
novel, however, does not depend on the plot.
Capel Boake paints with vivid touches the
beauty of Melbourne, and, evidently from a
first-hand knowledge, depicts the struggles
of her heroine to make a living, first in a
shop and then in an office, with interesting
and authentic glimpses of studio life. There
is one picture of a typical Australian subur-
ban home that is delightfully and convinc-
ingly done. And in her analysis of the
heroine Capel Boake does not shrink from
hurting the susceptibilities of her readers.
After her “fall” Helen thus searches her
heart.
“Did she regret it? Traditions of her
grandmothers that still lived in her, the
atmosphere of the world she lived in, told
her that she should regret. She knew, accord-
ing to their standards, she had suffered an
irreparable harm. Yet she could not feel the
sense of harm and loss that she should have
felt. If she herself could not feel that she
had been wronged, then was she? That was
the question. If she were only harmed in
the minds of other people, then how could
that affect her? It was only the knowledge
of her transgression, not the actual fact of
it, that would damn her. As it was, people
liked her and sought her society. If it were
known it would not be the fact of the sin,
but the knowledge of it, that would affect
their attitude to her.”
The characters are sufficiently well-drawn ;
though the final reconciliation between the
girl and her notorious mother rather strains
the reader’s credulity. A readable, realistic
story on a conventional model, done with an
artistic sincerity, but without much depth
of insight.
Paul Strange is still more conventional
in arrangement, its theme being the, rise of
a poor Australian country boy to high politi-
cal honors. The opening scenes on a cocky’s
selection are an attractive realistic intro-
duction to a story that too soon becomes a
mere society-political-conversational novel.
There is originality in the conception of the
boy’ parents, a waster of a father who, how-
ever, insists on himself educating his boy,
and a self-sacrificing, common, hard-work-
ing drab of a mother, whose hardly-saved
money gives her son his chance in the world.
The mother is a finely-drawn and charac-
teristic Australian type. There are, too, a
genial professor who could only have been
drawn from life, and his equally delightful
wife. There is, however, a feminine fuzzi-
ness about the other male characters, and
though Barbara is in an unobtrusive way
real, “the other woman,” upon whom the
author has spent much pains, remains
theatrical, a concocted formula common to
society novels.
The he ' becomes a leading lawyer in Mel-
bourne, and shocks his society friends by
joining the Labor party. There are some
portraits of the leaders of that party that
seem to have been based upon Tammany and
not Australian models. Yet Louise Brown
evidences a sufficient knowledge of local poli-
tics to make the strike in Melbourne real.
Melbourne “society” life is treated with a
light touch, but at too great a length for its
contribution to her 'hero’s development: too
many pages are devoted to “smart” conver-
sation. The author depends for the compli-
cations in her hero’s love affairs upon the
hackneyed device of the letter of confession
that was mislaid, and so only received on
the wedding-day. In real life the man would
have not been content to take the woman’s
silence for forgiveness. Though he had cer-
tainly taken the wise precaution not to trust
to the Melbourne post office to deliver his
letter, he could not have avoided in his sub-
sequent meetings with the girl some reference
to such a momentous communication. But
he not only makes this mistake with one
woman, but similarly misinterprets the
silence of the other. These mechanical mis-
understandings do not occur in real life.
And the theatrical climax is unworthy of
the excellent but unpretentious work in the
earlier portions of the novel.
Louise Brown does good service in
attempting to elucidate the Australian soul.
She allows her characters to discuss Aus-
tralian in an interesting but obvious fashion.
Thus one visitor does not notice very appar-
ent differences between the English and the
Australians. “The crowd in the street is
just like an English one. You Australians
are darker-skinned, of course, and you have
less color than we have. Then, though you
are not nearly so stolid, you are more
triste. I feel so much that undercurrent of
sadness here. It haunts the place; it is
everywhere—in the landscape, the people,
the Very air. * I have never seen it in a
whole nation and country before. It is
rather fascinating On the whole, I admire
your women, to look at. They are extremely
well-turned-out. and resemble French women
more than English.”
A correspondent criticises the phrase in
Jean Curlewis’s free verse, “If he was
killed.” She says it ought to be: “If he
were killed.” She also wants to know why
“this verv obvious error” is made so often
and so often allowed to pass? The answer is
that the use of the indicative instead of the
subjunctive after “if” is not an error. To
the fastidious ear the subjunctive is better;
but the growing disuse of archaic forms due
to the modern urge towards the simplification
of the language has established the indica-
tive mood after “if’ in general usage. In a
war-stricken world there is not much use
worrying about an inessential matter like
this. We say “if he was killed”; why not
write it? Especially as this simplification
breaks no immutable grammatical rule.
A reference in this page to “a littie society
of writers in Melbourne” has brought to light
the existence of The Australian Literature
Society, Royal Arcade, Melb. The reference,
however, was to The Australian Writers and
Artists’ Guild. The objects of this modest
little society are the study of Australian
literature and the encouragement of authors
in Australia; but the hon. secretary, P. C. E.
Widdop, does not mention how much it has
encouraged Australian authors. However,
we are pleased to learn that the society is
“still going strong.” He suggests that the
franchise of voters for the R.A.A. is too
narrow, since other journals “less literary”
and their contributors “less brilliant,” as he
courteously remarks, might feel aggrieved
and ignore a purely Bulletin R.A.A. But
there is no restriction upon the names of
the members proposed for Australasian
academicians, lie makes the suggestion that
the names selected by our voters may help
to focus opinion upon a possible group of
excellent literary critics, and that in the
final selection Australian literary societies
and Australian authors should have a say.
The method suggested by this page is, how-
ever, the only .practical one; and the fran-
chise, provided a sufficient number of voters
take the trouble to poll, is sufficiently wide
and sufficiently qualified to make the selected
candidates properly representative.
The “Brisbane,” R.A.N., is the first vol-
ume of a publication issued by the ship’s
company of the first cruiser built in Aus-
tralia. This number is printed in Malta,
3917. The issue celebrates “the end of a
13,000-mile journey in a brand new ship
snatched from the clutching fingers of a
thousand or so dilettante dockyardmen”!
During that voyage, we learn, the ship
“found” herself. One “spasm” begins
blithely but jumpily thus: —
Where are we going—don’t know.
When do we get.there—don’t know.
Shall we for days at a stretch in port stop,
Go to the bottom or stay on the top,
Eventually reach Plymouth or Pompey or not?
Don’t know.
Of Malta we learn that the present in-
habitants are mainly V.A.D.’s and goats,
and that the chief occupation of the Staff
Officers is to stand on the steps of the Club
and thrash their boots with a peculiar form
of cane. There are the usual naval jokes
and allusions that are -doubtless very ludi-
crous to those who understand them. The
best thing for readers of this page has been
borrowed by the wise editors, a poem en-
titled, “The Hospital Ship.” It was worth
stealing:—•
Like a feather, like foam, like a ray of the moon,
Like a lily afloat on a shining lagoon,
Like a cloud in fair weather, like mist far away
Is the white ship of Healing... .come in with the
day!
What freight does she carry? Those hosts of brave
men .
Who in forest and city, by mountain and fen
Were not deaf to the call, dared the pestilent breath,
And passed nnd mayed by the gateways of death!
Does she come from the near? Does she come from
the far? 1 ■
She comes from a land where all great hazards are!
Will she tarry awhile? Is she eager to roam?
Like a bird in the twilight she wings her way home.
Like a sweet passing face in the dusk of a dream,
Like a flake of fresh snow on the edge of a stream,
Like a shimmering star on the breast of the rrght
Is the white ship of Healing.... gone out with the
light! -
—Lorna de Lucchi. 1
Paul Jean Cels, Belgian art-connoisseur,
pertinently asks in Sydney Hekald why the.
names of Rubens, Michael Angelo and other
ancient masters are emblazoned on the out-
side of Sydney National Gallery while in-
side they are unrepresented? “What a
mockery!” he exclaims. It is a mockery
that on an Australian picture gallery no in-
dication is given on the outside of the build-
ing of the treasures it holds. Inside that
gallery are represented some of the finest
examples of Ililder, Norman Lindsay,
Streeton, Lambert and other Australian
artists who stand pre-eminent in their art
in Australia, and who can court comparison
with the best modern masters in their special
metiers. Those ancient foreign names should
be erased, since for the Gallery they are only
names, and in their stead we should see, and
some day shall see, there “emblazoned” the
Australian masters of the Australian contri-
bution to the world of art.
The last day on which entries for the
guinea prize for the list of six names, in
order, to form the foundation members of
the Republican Acadeipy of Australasian
Art, is next Saturday. The qualification for
the voter is the publication of story or verse
in The Bulletin. So far, the response has
been poor; and, in order to make the selec-
tion representative, every qualified contri-
butor is urged to vote.
What Billzac Reads.
James R. Durkin, abroad: There are a mighty lot
of men in this army who want more substantial
mental pabulum than' the English halfpenny dailies,
which in the main are simply snobbish sheets devoted
to the doings of an effete aristocracy. As for my-
self, I will undertake to read all the Triabs and
Stead Reviews anyone likes to send along; also
Contemporary or Fortnightly Reviews and others
of that stamp. I made arrangements in Australia for
a regular Bulletin, but it doesn’t often get as far
as me.

Write for Illustrated Literature
VARIOUS VERSE
The Shrine.
In my heart there is a shrine
Guarded with a jealous care,
Where my spirit takes the wine
Of a sacrament I share
With my inner thoughts alone —
Memories that have not flown.
There the best I know is kept
Sacred, safe, inviolate ;
Scent from flowers that life has swept
From my life. In holy state
There, pure, tender, fair and good,
Lives my dream of womanhood !
Envy in your heart might rise
Knew you what that vision seems;
For her eyes are not your eyes,
Tender passion in them gleams.
To my conscience I might say
That my dream resembles May.
As, without a temple grey,
1-lighway-dust mars image fair,
Dust of wedded day-by-day
Hides the soul I thought you were —
May, from whom I had to part,
Holds the shrine within my heart.
N.S.W.
HAMER.
Nocturne.
Across the desert of the sky
Passes the sun’s slow caravan,
And Night, a jaded courtesan,
With stealthy feet comes drifting by.
Above the pool of Dusk she stoops
And pauses, and with patient care
She lifts the darkness of her hair,
Gem-decked in green and yellow loops.
And while she smears her breasts and hands
With fragrant ambergris and nard,
Beneath her sandalled feet the shard
Of Day is trampled in the sands.
She steals in through the city gates;
And ’neath the shadowy minarets
Glimmer her silvern amulets
As furtive-eyed she peers and waits.
Anon the purple heights afar
Grow pale, and from the East comes
soon,
With laggard steps, a drunken Moon,
Thrust out from some sin-steeped bazaar.
She plucks his sleeve, and, passion-fanned,
Flame of desire lights up her eyes;
And reeling home across the skies
They go, hot hand enclasped in hand.
Each shadow, like a startled fawn,
For sanctuary turns to flee
As at the desert’s rim they see
The slinking jackals of the dawn.
SINAI.
G. F. E.
A Love-Lilt.
Love is a thing that will conquer still though
the sun in its course should stay. . . .
A street organ plays underneath my sill
“Oh ! where is my love to-day?”
I curse it well as I hear its strains, for its
tones on my nerves jar hard;
And my thoughts stray rudely to dim-lit lanes,
and the girl whom my follies barred. I
She was the Girl; but I did not know that
the Fates in their hidebound course
Had laid for me a long sea-tow with no
grease in a rusty hawse!
For a well-chafed life the Fates have
brought, and a love with a schooner rig
(Here the organ changed with a snarl and
a snort to a rollicking Irish jig!).
My heart is stirred as I hear its sound, and
my gouty feet strive well
To do the steps that my boyhood found, when
it danced towards n loveless Hell.
A loveless Hell —-from a misspent life and a
mother who petted and kissed,
And a woman who ought to have been my
wife, to-cherish and love....but, list!
The organ’s giving the dinkum cry, the sob-
stuff is put to flight!
It grinds out “A wandering minstrel I,”
and I reckon it’s nearly right !
For a wandering life on an endless sea with
a ship as my sweetheart true
Are the things, by God ! that are most of me
while the sky and the seas are blue!
We’ve heard a lot of the men outback, with
their swags and billies and things,
And the long, long tramp through the lone
bush-traclc —of which Harry Lawson
sings.
I don’t know Harry; but I desire that his
heart when a-tune will send
A wireless message, a mulga wire, to me on
the deep-sea end !
For he is a man of the gipsy breed, and that
breed is also in me.
So I wander on, and I do not heed, but
follow the path of the sea!
And the organ plays with a sob and a squeal,
and it mixes the tunes awry ;
Though the only tune of the lot I feel is “A
wandering minstrel I” !
But this is a song of an old-time day, a song
of a love confest,
A song of a love that was thrown away, a
song of a heart distrest!
A song of a cove that “don’t care a curse”—
a song of “don’t want to care”;
A song of better and bad and worse, a song
of the salt sea air!
But our course is laid, and we’ve got to sail;
for we're crimped by a heartless Fate,
Who flings us drunken over the rail to be
hazed by a soul-dried mate,
Whose name is Work. We must earn our
screw, though our hearts are in lotus-
land —•
But the organ-grinder (and Lawson, too)
I’m certain will understand!
N.S.W.
ALBATROSS.

publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby expressly
reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column all
contributions submitted, whether in the form of Politi-
cal, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or
Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects, and
not exceeding two columns in length, or, say, 3000
words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters
or Newspaper Clippings. All communications will be
regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. ivill be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded,
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,

but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, JUNE 21, 1917.
COCKY AMONG THE RODENTS.
On the facts prevailing when the Australian
harvest of 1915 began to ripen there can be
little doubt that the Federal Government of
that day did the right thing in establishing a
wheat pool. The consequences of not doing
so would certainly—for a time, anyhow—
have been far more disastrous than any con-
ceivable hash the Government might make of
its job. But, as The Bulletin pointed out
at the time, the really important matter was
not whether the intentions of the State and
Federal Ministries were good in theory, but
to what extent they were going to be real-
ised in practice. Judgment would in the end
have to be passed, not upon a balancing of
the hypothetical calamities avoided against
the results achieved, but upon the actual
working of the arrangement entered into for
the salvation of the national cocky. It was
clear enough that once the scheme had been
inaugurated everything would depend upon
the wash-up, and that, if Cocky had reason-
able grounds for believing himself entitled
to a dividend of 3d., it would be futile to tell
him that he ought to be gratified with some-
thing less because he had escaped getting a
still more microscopical reward for his toil.
The pool was a business undertaking entered
into for the direct purpose of marketing the
Australian harvest to the best possible ad-
vantage of the grower—of securing for him
those prices which, without intervention by
the State, would, in the language of Hughes,
“remain mere glittering baubles to mock our
plight”—the public credit being utilised in
the meantime to finance “the backbone of the
nation” by advances of cash against the pro-
duce. Well, 18 months have passed, and
there are now two pools, with another loom-
ing on the horizon. The operations so far
have involved many millions of money, and
while it is impossible, because of the close
secrecy in which the working parts of the
pool are shrouded, to fully criticise the ad-
ministration it is well within the bounds of
legitimate comment to say that the experi-
ence of the last year and a half justifies a
suspicion that the management is miserably
failing to accomplish what it set out to do.
There is an almost universal belief among the
wheat-growers that they are not getting a
fair deal. It is a belief shared in by this
paper. While Cocky is being given an occa-
sional drop or two out of the pool, other
interests are dipping their buckets in.
The wheat of two harvests has been taken
clean out of the growers’ hands, nominally
passing to the control of State Boards and
a Central Board, the latter being the
linch-pin of the outfit. This Central Board,
consisting of politicians, allegedly does the
things that matter in the way of arranging
freights and finance and sales at the other
end. The whole business is actually in the
hands of what is called an Advisory Board
consisting of agents and charterers. On
neither Board is there a representative of
the wheat-growers. Applications for the
appointment of such a representative were
for a time steadily refused. Why? This
paper cannot guess. Afterwards the Prime
Minister agreed to make an appointment
provided someone was nominated by the
farmers’ organisations in the various States.
The nomination was made. The appoint-
ment was not. Instead, there has been a
prolonged “stall” and talk about taking a
poll of the farmers and an apparent anxiety
to bluff them off until the time arrives when
it will matter nothing whether there is an
election or not. It is monstrous that this
should be so. If ever men were entitled to
representation anywhere it is in a machine
that is disposing of their goods, a transaction
which in this case is equivalent to fixing
their wages. No other class of men would
tolerate being imposed upon in this way.
The meat men have been having a very con-
siderable share in the discussion about the
price of their commodity. In the wool-sale
the pastoralists are principals. There are
some differences in the nature of these
affairs and the wheat-pool, but not one of
them supplies a reason for putting a bandage
over the wheat cocky’s eyes, giving him an
advance of 2s. Gd. and the privilege of either
selling his scrip or waiting in the dark for
additional doles.
* * * *
Millions of bushels of Australian wheat
have been sold below the London parity.
There may be good reasons for this. If
there are, the growers are entitled to know
them. Millions of bushels are being shipped
at a freight rate which looks like highway
robbery, and until some explanation is made
of these atrocious figures this paper is going
to believe that, through the blundering
or worse of the Central Board and the
Advisory Board of agents and charterers, the
Australian farmer has garnered two harvests
on which an enormous war-tax is being
levied by the British Government, and which
is yielding immense and avoidable profits to
charterers and shipowners. The result of
selling millions of tons of wheat to Britain
at 4s. 9d. per bushel when the price in
London was 10s. 3d. is this: that the ship-
owners are able, while charging freights up
to .180 s. a ton, to land wheat in England on
which, first, the British Government makes
a selling profit. Subsequently the same Gov-
ernment makes the shipping company dis-
gorge a great part of the excess profits
earned by carrying the material; and, finally,
it requires the co. to hand over a substantial
part of the remaining profit by way of
income-tax. The price the Australian grower
should have received is cut very nearly in
halves, one piece going to him and the other
to someone else. There is no getting away
from it that either because the British Gov-
ernment encourages the earning of excess
profits or because it has failed to keep
freights down, the Australian wheat-pool is
being conducted on lines which throw upon
the Australian wheat-grower the full weight
of the burden. Canada, the United States
and the Argentine are not being dealt with
in this way. The holders of wheat in those
countries and the growers in England are
being paid the prices the Australian pools
were started to get. For what purpose has
this objective been abandoned by the Com-
monwealth and the deal placed upon an in-
verted basis, yielding no benefit to the
British consumer, restricting the State and
Federal Treasuries’ revenue from income-
tax, heavily reducing the national earnings
of Australia and yielding enormous revenue
to the British Government and its partner
who owns the carrying fleet? There may be.
“patriotic” and “Imperial” reasons for all
this. But they were not mentioned in the
bond. If they exist, the Australian cocky
ought to know what they are instead of
being surreptitiously fleeced.
What is happening now is that every-
body touching Australian wheat is mak-
ing a fat profit except the poor devil
who sweated himself at the plough
and the harvester. The “purchasing
agents” on their 3*d. a bushel arrangement
are doing very well: the shipowners and the
charterers and all the rest of that particular
family are getting a rake-off of well-nigh in-
credible dimensions; the British Government
is appropriating revenue; millers, mice, civil
servants, re-baggers and everyone else are
well-nourished and prosperous. At the tail-
end of the procession comes Cocky, dis-
tractedly wondering what has hit him and
taking what is left after everybody else is
satisfied. And it is a poor, wretched share
that is left. How the majority of growers have
managed to get through on what they have
been tossed this paper has not even a remote
idea. That in nine cases out of 10 they are
just scraping along from hand to mouth is
iio less certain than that if the existing sys-
tem is pursued much longer thousands of
them will be pushed off the soil. The Federal
Parliament is now in session. The manage-
ment of the wheat-pool is of vital import-
ance to Australia. It should be the business
of the Federal Parliament to insist on the
lid being taken off —to insist on getting from
the new Government a full, precise state-
ment of what has been, and is now being,
done, and the reason why the “glittering
baubles” have been appropriated by other
people, while Cocky’s head has been kept in
a bucket. Australia’s financial position is
not such that this business can go on with-
out a fuller understanding. And the position
of the wheat-grower is not such that he can
calmly contemplate his crops being devoured
by about 17 different varieties of mice.
GREECE.
Out of the dull red haze which marks the
progress of Ancient History as it followed
the imperial banners of successive races
from victory to victory rises the mixed but
heroic tale of Hellas. Greece was small;
her affairs were merely those of a few striv-
ing parochial councils; her wars, with one
exception, were tinpot struggles over the
position of the local pump or the hymns
that should be sung in church. But there
was something vital about it all —something
that found expression in art and poetry and
philosophy unlike those of any other land.
The breeze blew freshly down from the
Olympian range in the north of Thessaly,
and that breeze got into the Greek soul. The
ancient Greek was hardly a pleasant person,
lie had no very high regard for truth ; his
sentiments of honor were often a bit twisted ;
he was intolerant of all who did not possess
his particular brand of culture, and lie had
an overwhelmingly large idea of his import-
ance in the scheme of humanity. But he
had high courage, wide-open eyes, a hue faith
in doing things and a directness and
simplicity of thought which made a certain
harmony through all his discords. How long
Greece was Greece before authentic history
began archaeologists and ethnologists are now
guessing from the remains of ancient civili-
sation in Crete; but the Greece of history
is only a short-lived dog-fight around a few
capes and mountains in the bottom right-
hand corner of Europe, trimmed with
hexameters and marble and decorated with
oratory and song. A dozen generations takes
in the full sweep of Greek story at its best,
and then follows a period of wrangling decay
until the whole thing merges in the vast
placidity of the Roman Empire. Still, there
remained something vital in Christianised
Greece which in a sense conquered that
Empire; and the organisation which held
the world in awe tipped over to the east by
the weight of Greek thought, until Con-
stantinople and not Rome became the capital
of mankind. Various things happened then
which brought no added credit to the
Hellenic race, though they included a Greek
empire, an empire partly Greek and an
empire wholly barbaric which rested on
Greek props; but the invading Turk brought
it all to a dead finish and heaped Asia and
Mahomet on everything that looked worth
preserving. Greece fell into slavery and
piracy; and remote scholars reading the
works produced during the brilliant period
of the original dog-fight lamented the fate
of the people who had overthrown Xerxes
and carved white stone into miracles of grace
and beauty. So Greece was in the mud;
and schemes for pulling her out again made
the pulses of the person who had felt the
inspiration of old Greek thought leap as at
the call of a nation to arms. For Hellenism
goes far beyond the limits of mere blood and
race, and all who have felt the Greek fire are
in some sense—perhaps the best sense—
Greek patriots. Men who were left cold by
the thought of London or Paris quickened
into strange warmth at the mere mention of
Athens; and so it came about that people
the world over plotted with what was left
of lonian and Dorian for a glorious resur-
rection.
Greece rose from the tomb in a ricketty
and truculent fashion. The dead bones clat-
tered and the unburied knife came into evi-
dence early in the proceedings. To Europe,
looking on, it seemed that Greek hated Greek
far more fervently than he hated Turk, and
that the throwing off of the Ottoman yoke
must mean a fight to a finish by about a
dozen picturesque but infuriated sorts of
bandits. For this reason the Great Powers
were induced to take a hand ; and the only
thing the statesmen of the world could think
of to help to solve the problem was the pre-
sentation to the Greek nation of a foreign
king. The first monarch sent on approval
was dethroned with popular enthusiasm;
but the second stuck with some tena-
city to the bucking throne, and at last
seemed to. have almost mastered his mount.
All the time, however, there was a feeling
that Greece was not quite what Greece
might be, and that a Dane with German
connections was not altogether the ideal
leader for a people with a history and an
ancient repute. George and his son Con-
stantine were foreigners in thought as well
as race. Had Xerxes, before his overthrow
at Platea, suggested the settlement of Attic
and Spartan rivalries by placing a Car-
thaginian prince on the throne of all Greece,
the proposal would have been received with
classic blasphemv and plain Parnassian mud.
No Greek of the days when Piiryne’s smile
was in active service would have tolerated
the outsider; and the national consent to
the establishment of a Teutonic dynasty
even in the nineteenth century was grudg-
ingly given. Greece wanted freedom, not a
petty potentate playing tricks with an
alleged Constitution; but the wisdom of
Europe, anxious to nurse an old people into
new nationality, foisted upon it an institu-
tion that was wholly opposed to Greek ideas,'
ancient and modern. The result has been
what might have been expected. The Greek
nation is still at sixes and sevens; and Con-
stantine, playing the game of his German
brother-in-law, has made the people who de-
sired freedom and didn’t get it a menace to
every free country in the world. That it did
not become more than a menace was mainly
due to a Greek who thought his nation’s
honor and decency counted for more than
mere loyalty to the foreign king. In plain
fact, the attempts of more or less well-mean-
ing outsiders to shape the affairs of a people
whose aims and ideas they could not under-
stand have first and last been a bitter
failure. Constantine has slipped off the
shaky throne and his son is attempting to
mount it: but there will be no real settle-
ment of the Greek difficulty until the
Perigees or Themistocles whom the
modern revolution throws up takes charge
with understanding, and Greek meets Greek
on a plain, democratic basis without bowing
to the overlord or keeping an eye open for
alien intrusion.
This war is being fought mainly in defence
of the rights of small peoples; and it is
reasonably to be presumed that those rights
include the hisrh nrivilesre of calling their
souls their own. Hitherto the idea of inter-
national statesmen appears to have been
that all men were much of the same kind
and that if they couldn't see things in the
same way the best tiling that could be done
was to put a charge of gunpowder under
their vision. Plainly Greek thought is not
in the least like that of the north of Europe ;
and the most Danish king would never induce
Demetrius to dream or act like a Dane.
But he is not to be despised on that account,
nor is there any decent reason for supposing
him inferior to the Dane. On the contrary,
there are elements in the make-up of the
present relatives of Leonidas which suggest
that there should be a splendid nationhood
some day in that ragged corner by the
£Egean where some memory of Athene still
floats through the popular mind. The modern
Greek is not the ancient Greek ; but, given
a decent chance, he might readily prove him-
self a far better fellow. Those Serb elements
in the people that broke to some extent the
race continuity were probably as great an
advantage to the whole as the previous
Dorian invasion from which ancient historic
Greece dated its fresh start along progressive
lines. Had Byron’s idea of the yoke of the
Turk being broken by “native swords and
native ranks” been steadily kept to the front,
affairs might have looked a great deal more
pleasant to-day. True, there might have
been a lot of throat-cutting which outsiders
could not have understood; but the person
who did the cutting would have known why,
and the end of the tumult among hardy,
hard-fighting people whose aim was liberty
would have been liberty. Without nursing
or interference “from outside, without foreign
king or foreign suggestion in the Constitu-
tion. a Greek republic might have been in
existence at the beginning of the present war
whose weight on the side of Servia would have
prevented the hectoring of that small country
by Austria and compelled the Kaiser to find
another excuse for his war. The rights of
small peoples will involve a lot of breaking-
up in Europe; and the breaking-up will be
good for Europe if it can be done without
additional fireworks. One thing seems quite
clear. When Poland and Bohemia and a few
other places get their liberty, the memory
of Constantine and his wife will prevent
out-of-work kings being offered the chance
to make nuisances of themselves by the
establishment of new centres of intrigue.
Greece has added this lesson to a lot that
she taught us in the past; but she may have
bigger and better things to say in a freer
future.
QUEENSLAND’S EXPERIENCE OF
WORKMEN’S COMPENSATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Men with bound hands; men lame and halt;
a youth with a broken finger laboriously
signing his name; a uniformed ambulance
man bandaging and unbandaging the broken
soldiers of industry; a medical officer ex-
amining the temporarily unfit; the sight of
lint and the smell of iodoform —this is the
Claims Department of the Queensland State
Insurance Office on the Workmen’s Compen-
sation side, now, to all intents, a State
monopoly. Ending April last there were paid,
since the State started its insurance business
last year, 6947 claims, totalling £71,389. No
employer may carry his own risk. Every
workman and workwoman has to be insured.
The duty of the State office is to see that the
claim, if sustained, is paid.
Some of the claims turned down and a
few of those paid are remarkable for the
ingenuity of the claimants; others are
peculiar illustrations of the vagaries of the
little blind devil of Chance. One claim was
made for the death of a man in a tragedy of
the track—a long, dry stage with one water-
bag ; the journey taken while the subject was
suffering a recovery; death on a stony rise
from thirst after rum. A horse bit a man,
and the bite cost the assurers £l2 3s. 4d. A
stage bomb exploded too early at rehearsal,
and the office paid for an eye injured by the
explosion of a gun on active service on the
stage. A girl fainted in a factory, and, while
escaping injury herself, injured another girl
by falling on her. A man’s eyes were
scorched by an electric flash from a switch-
board during a storm. A man, avoiding a
snake, fell and broke two ribs. A cook, while
sleeping, was bitten by “some insect” at a
cost of £7 in compensation—insect not pro-
duced. Sydney Smith’s review of the evi-
dence of the select committee of the House
of Lords into the conditions of child chimney-
sweeps relates that “sweepers’ cancer” from
the irritation of soot was common; and
Queensland has paid compensation in one
case of skin trouble resulting from the irrita-
tion of coal-dust. The rider of a bicycle
caught in a tram-rail; a jockey who fell in
a race; a fire-brigade man who injured him-
self while playing football as part of the
compulsory physical drill of the brigade—all
these made accepted claims. A girl employed
in a picture-show was accidentally knocked
down by a drunken man in the dark, and
received compensation; and a workman
killed by a fellow-workman who had run
amok was considered by the Chief Justice to
have suffered death as one of the risks of
his labor, and his widow was awarded £250
Damages were paid also in the case of a fire-
man injured in the row following on a
second fireman calling a third a “Russian” ;
in a case of “copper-itch.” caused by skin-
irritation from working in water containing
copper in solution; in a case of strain sus-
tained while preventing a fellow-workman’s
attempt at suicide; and the widow of a man
who had died of snake-bite, received while
attempting to kill the snake, was awarded
compensation for the loss of the bread-
winner.
When a number of soldiers attacked a
Greek’s fishshop in Brisbane a frightened
waitress jumped from a balcony and sprained
both ankles, and received compensation. A
njan whose employer had warned him of a
dangerous bull replied that he would fight
the bull. When the bull accepted the chal-
lenge the workman attacked with a crowbar,
and the bull won the toss. The injured
workman claimed against the State Insur-
ance, then withdrew the claim, and brought
suit against the owner of the bull, winning
damages.
Compensation was claimed for one case of
housemaid’s-knee, and was refused; but 140


claims of domestic servants have been paid.
There were four deaths paid for the
mont flood; one for a bite
hacked spider; one for a man who handled a
live wire. A man while eating lunch with
a fellow-workman threw a piece of iron at a
rat. He killed the rat, but the iron tangented
to the head of the rat-killer’s mate, mju »
him seriously. The Department paid.
Among the humors was the_ case oi a doj
who was thrown down and injured m t“ e
crush of the workmen to leave when the
whistle blew. And there was a large immi-
grant, 6ft. 2in. high, who claimed for in-
juries caused by being thrown down by a
calf 12 hours old. The claim was rejected,
but the immigrant laid siege to the office tor
three weeks. It will be bad luck tor the
State Insurance office if this Goliath
among calves survives the veal stage.
The work of the State Insurance Depart-
ment has been to establish lower rates and
higher benefits, with the permanent local in-
vestment of all its funds; and a late amend-
ment of the law is a fine thing in the direc-
tion of supporting the miner stricken with
diseases incidental to his calling. Among
these are miners’ phthisis; anklyostomiasis;
miner’s bent hand, bent knee or bent elbow
(a disease common to other than miners, but
in a more pleasant form), and inflammation
of the synovial lining of the wrist joint and
tendon sheaths. These are injuries far more
poignant and generally more permanent than
the injury of sudden violence. The Govern-
ment based its legislation on the fact that the
miner’s wages are the pay for miner’s labor,
and not an indemnity of the mine-owner
against disease resulting from the miner’s
calling. Wage-earners receive wages for work
and not for risks to health or bones, and in-
surance against such shocking diseases is as
much a necessity as daily bread. The com-
pensation payable is only fair to modern
minds, providing for the miner’s widow and
children and for the support of the tempo-
rarily incapacitated worker and his wife and
family. There are all sorts of safeguards to
prevent an influx to Queensland of miners
already incapacitated in other States, and
both the State and the employer are pro-
tected against abuse of the pi'ovisions of the
Act.
The Legislative Council has unfortunately
limited the operation of the Amending Act to
two years’ currency, as if all the diseases
of miners were to be stamped out in that
time; but the Government accepted the
limit, knowing that employers and employees
alike will be so satisfied in that two years as
to demand the re-enactment of the measure.
It comes into operation in July next, and is
sure to repeat the success of the State Insur-
ance Department in the field of compensation
to men injured in surface occupation.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Forty Millions for Corruption.
From the beginning of Federation until now
£10,167,215 has been handed out of the
national caslibox to the Post Office; and by
spending this sum and the whole of its
revenue the institution has managed to
struggle along. Ten millions to keep a pub-
lic monopoly standing on its legs—the per-
formance seems entitled to he regarded as
something like a world’s record of what may
be achieved on let-’er-slide principles. If the
Commonwealth had those millions now it
might find employment for the cash on a
more important enterprise. Likewise if it
had the £1.652,447 frittered away at Can-
berra the money would be useful. And there
is the little matter of £18.000.000 loaned out
of the note-issue fund to the States since the
war began, and the £10,500.000 borrowed by
the Commonwealth for the same hungry
crowd in the same period. Add together
these various lumps passed out by the
Federal authority in the interests of bloat
and muddle—the Post Office, Canberra and
the States —and you get a cheerful total of
£40,319,662. This would have met more- than
two-thirds of Australia’s war bill of the
current year. The payment of this money
to the States represents a gross outrage upon
the nation. It was money used to debauch
the community—to help blind lhe people to
their dangers and responsibilities. Accord-
to Bigjoh'n Forrest the Commonwealth
crippled itself. It did worse. It corrupted
itself and assisted the States to degradation.
The Matter of Reprisals.
While the Germans are getting all the war
they want on the Western Front and British
bird-men have all the best of it in cloud-
fighting. the baby-killing raids still take
placb, and there seems to be no sure mode
of stopping them. The fact is that just as
the patrol of the sea cannot, on account of
the extent of water to be covered, be perfect
at all places and times, so the air is too wide
and plentiful for complete observation of
murder on the wing. The reprisals attempted
by Britain do not seem to have amounted to
much; but the anger of people in London
and Manchester on this account is hardly
reasonable. To take aeroplanes away from
direct militarv duty as the Germans have
done and apply them to plain slaughter of
unarmed citizens is not the best way to win
the war. Of course, everybody feels that
Berlin ought to get a taste of its own fright-
fulness ; but that is coming in a better way.
The smashing blows which prove to all the
world that the German soldier is the inferior
of the British soldier, the German arms of
poorer quality than those of the Alfies. and
the German organisation which took 50 years
to perfect less effective than that which the
free nations called into existence in two—
these things are worth more than all the
local explosions which Fritz could gnash his
teeth over. What Berlin is being made to
feel is that, with uncounted crimes on her
head, her defenders can’t fight well enough,
her leaders can’t lead well enough and that
the Hell which is the special creation of the
Prussian city is thundering a little.nearer all
the time. That knowledge, while the hunger
bites, represents the best sort of reprisal
London can make.
The Nation and War Profits.
Tim Hip I‘ist financial statement made by
(M«l £3,000,000 in.
eluded in the estimated receipts for lJlb-l<
was to be derived from a tax on war-time
profits. The next statement of Australia s
problematical balance-sheet was made by
Poynton after the earthquake. Poynton
reckoned the national income was by the
annexation of part, of the profits going to be
swelled by £1,500.000. Forrest supervened
in March with a further statement m which
he estimated the revenue from war profits
at £BOO 000. Last week Forrest produced
an additional story about revenue and ex-
penditure. and as the financial year closes
in a couple of weeks he was down almost to
bedrock. The war profit receipts had, of
course, amounted to od., which represents a
fairly wide gap between expectation and
realisation. But Big JOHN was still optimis-
tic. He explained:—
The Bill is now prepared and will be immediately
introduced. It seems likely that the £BOO,OOO esti-
mated for 1910-17 but not received will be collected
during 1917-18, and the tax for that year during
1918-19.
It seems quite as likely that a year may be
in danger of losing itself in this labyrinth
unless someone keeps an eye on it. It
also seems reasonable to ask that the Bill
giving the Commonwealth authority to col-
lect should be produced without further
delay. The necessity for finding new sources
of revenue is urgent to the point of despera-
tion, and there is no levy which can be more
legitimately made than this; for the pile,
which has in the aggregate reached large
proportions, is mainly if not exclusively the
result of the expenditure of loan money. For
a time after the slaughter began most
trading and financial concerns seemed
to feel that a certain degree of shame
was attached to profiteering out of
war conditions. They knew. instinct-
ively—that they had no moral right
to this money—-no moral right to get rich
out of the horrors of war. The tax should
have been imposed then. That it was delayed
—if the Government had made up its mind
to collect it-—was an injustice not only to
the nation but to the persons liable to pay ;
and the longer the delay the greater the
injustice, because the greater the inconveni-
ence in the end.

The Hunt of the Submarine.
F.verytiiing seems to be upended in modern
war, and part of the general unexpectedness
is the discovery that the best way to deal
with an individual carrying on operations
under the water is to maintain another in-
dividual up in the clouds. At any_ rate, pend-
ing the invention of a mechanical shark
which will devour the submarine where it
lives, the aeroplane patrol appears to be the
most useful thing yet discovered, and it is
quite possible that if the Yankee-birds flock
to the North Sea in vast coveys it will
become dangerous for Fritz to even come
up to inspect the situation. Meanwhile,
though the latest reports about the torpedo
campaign show that the German is still very
destructive, there are features about the
performance which make it look as if John
Bull is keeping the road to the pantry
more open than it was a few months ago.
Up till March particulars of what unre-
strained frightfulness was accomplishing
was left to the imagination. Since then
reports of a sort have been made by the
Admiralty. From these it appeared that
for the next six weeks Fritz missed a lot of
shots. By the end of April he had, how-
ever, got both his eye and his hand in:
nearly twice the number of ships were
attacked and the sinkings went up, so to
speak, with a bound. The situation began
to look very bad : for it was obvious that if
the enemy continued at this rate, 102 ves-
sels of the larger class being blown up inside
three weeks, the end wouldn’t be at all
cheerful to think about. Since then more
use has apparently been made of the aero-
plane, and the record from the - time of the

But there is one blot on the figures. This
is the division of the vessels sunk into two
classes —over and under 1600 tons —and no
division of the vessels attacked. According
to the last report there was an increase from
15 to 22 in the larger vessels sunk. If these
were two- or three-thousand ton vessels, it
means much less than if they were
craft of 10 and 12 thousand tons. As the
submarine seems latterly to be devoting
most attention to the larger ship, more in-
formation of what lip was actually doing to
it would fill what is now a vacancy. And it
happens that this vacancy is at the point
where Australians have the greatest direct
interest.
The State and the Occasional Hyena.
Except upon the ground that the death
penalty should not be paid for murder —that
to hang a man is to do something futile —
there was no reason why Christian Benzing
should have been spared. The crime of
which he stood convicted was of the grossest
kind. There was no shadow of doubt about
his guilt, nor is it conceivable that if this
man had been shot red-handed in his tracks
near the torn and quivering body of the
child he so brutally did to death, there would
have been anyone to say that he did not
richly deserve the bullet. Benzing enticed
a little girl away from the companions with
whom she was playing by the roadside in
one of Sydney’s outer suburbs, took her to a
secluded spot, and there committed crimes
which are scarcely to be thought of without
seeing red. That he knew what he was
doing is beyond question. This was not one
of those offences that are committed sud-
denly by men in moments of excitement. It
was a measured and deliberate act. and the
effort to escape the consequences alone ban-
ishes the theory of irresponsibility. If any
murderer should ever have been hanged
Benzing was the man. The midnight appeal
made to Acting-Premier Fuller by a depu-
tation from the State Labor Conference
rested upon no more solid ground than a
general objection to hanging. The story
about the prisoner having been sunstruck
when an infant meant nothing, though it
illustrated the flimsy evidence on which cut-
and-dried theories on human conduct are
built by untrained minds. Against hanging
there is in very many cases room for pro-
test. Scientific research and the experience
of many countries prove this to be so. But
both research and experience can be easily
misinterpreted, and though The Bulletin
earnestly desires to see the criminal code
amended in the light of the wider knowledge
now possessed, it is unable to avoid the con-
clusion that what happened in France and
the United States have terrible significance.
The history of the social experiments in
those countries have broken into fragments
many of the theories formulated by men
horrified at the almost indiscriminate hang-
ings of their time. And until we see some
more satisfactory explanation of the increase
of murder in the first degree in France after
the abolition of the death-penalty and the
decrease of murder after the restoration
thereof, how can we escape believing what
the French Government was driven to
believe?
Wage-bonus Systems.
The observations of a man who knows some-
thing about it: —
W. W. Fohwood, of South Aus., when re-
tiring from the presidency of the Australian
Chambers of Manufactures, advocated bonuses
for workmen to encourage production. If Mr.
Norwood will permit the expression, he is
on the right track, but what made him spoil the effect
by speaking kindly of “an agreed bonus payable every
day for all extra work performed”? There is not an
authority in the manufacturing world of the United
States who is not trying to provide against men buck-
ing up for a day or so in order to earn bonus, and
then slowing-down to something under day-wages rate
—easing up, in short, for another burst.
There are four well-known wage-bonus systems in
the world, and the system of Harrington Emerson,
an American, is probably best adapted to Australia.
H. L. Gantt, however, would run a very good second,
if not level. Emerson, when called into a factory to
advise, overhauls the plant and the entire arrange-
ments from start to finish, seeking to lessen the work
of the operatives all he can. Then he takes the past
times for doing each job, allows for help to the men
by his own innovations, and fixes a standard time
for every bit of work. Anyone coming up to this
standard, with all the wrinkles Emerson’s expert
efficiency tradesmen can show him, gets 20 per cent,
added to his wages. That achievement is called 100
per cent, efficient. But if a man takes time and a
half to do the job, he reaches the point where the
bonus starts. He is “two-thirds efficient.” This,
however, means a very small bonus. But the task is
easy, and it marks the beginning of bonus-earning
capacity and gives encouragement. For 67 per cent,
efficiency the bonus is an addition of .01 per cent, to
the day-rate of pay; for 74 per cent, efficiency, 1 per
cent. ; for 90 per cent, efficiency the bonus is 10 per
cent.; for 100 per cent, efficiency, 20 per cent. After
that, all the time a man saves on a job is paid to
him in full, just as for piecework.
The great expert Gantt likes to set a hard task,
and to encourage the operatives to become skilful
enough to do it, and earn a swinging bonus. He
will show them everything known about how best to
do the job, to get all the machinery and power
working as they never worked before, to avoid
inconvenience to the men, and eradicate entirely all
vexation of good artisans by incompetent foremen,
overseers and managers. But 'when Gantt says three
hours is the proper time for a job, it has to be done
in three hours before the workman by the strict
letter gets anything over his day-rate of pay, which
in Australia would be that fixed by the industrial
courts. For doing a three-hour job in five hours a
man is paid for five hours. For doing it in three
hours and five minutes he is paid for three hours
and five minutes. But for doing it in three
hours he is paid for four hours; and if he can get it
done in two hours he would still be paid for four
hours, by time rate, thus earning double pay. As a
matter of fact, anything like three hours and 10
minutes would also be paid as four hours, or some-
thing near it, because Gantt is an enthusiast and
does everything he can to develop skill when intro-
ducing his methods in any works.
A point to note is that both Gantt and Emerson
have paid big bonuses on a month’s work which has
been less than was usually done by day-rate. Men
have bogg'ed in and earned a bonus, and then
“slowed-down.” Nowadays, to stop that, Gantt
pays an extra bonus to men who earn their ordinary
bonus every day, and Emerson adds up a man’s time
for a month, and sets that against his month’s per-
formance to arrive at the bonus due. Therefore, when
Mr. W. W. Forwood, president of the Association of
the Chambers of Manufactures of this big Australia,
suggests that the bonus should be paid every day, he
might do worse than go and look it all up in a book.
The Halsey and Rowan premium systems of bonus
can be studied by the same means, but the Rowan
is not adapted to Australian conditions, and it is
doubtful if the men would reckon that either it or
the Halsey plan would give them enough reward for
increased output.

The Worker (Sydney), in answering Joe
Cook’s chortle that the Labor movement is
dead, declares: —
At the present time what is known as the Indus-
trial Section is supreme in the Conference and on the
executive, and there is much irritation among the
minority, which includes some of the best and most
loyal men and women that ever devoted their lives in
a spirit of self-sacrifice to the well-being of the work-
ing-class. But why . not recognise that this develop-
ment was inevitable, because necessary to the salva-
tion of the movement? Why not join the Industrial-
ists? The door of membership is wide open, and
there are no prohibitive conditions.
This is a remarkable statement following, as
it does, directly upon the “unremovable” ex-
communications lately pronounced by the
P.L.L. Conference on all who honestly dif-
fered with the majority of that body on the
question of compulsory service. What the
Worker should have said was that the door
was wide open to anyone who would follow
the dictates of a small clique with blind,
dog-like fidelity, but that the hoof would be
immediately applied to hoist him for ever into
the outer darkness if he so much as hinted
an objection to certain planks which had
lately been clumsily nailed across the old
platform. Joe Cook may be doing the fool’s
rejoicing-act over the death of a thing he
wishes dead ; but the Labor movement can
have no life save as a destructive and
dangerous force until it gets rid of the notion
that coercion in matters of opinion can go
hand-in-hand with progress. To restrict
thought is the last and most damning treason
to democracy, and of that treason the P.L.L.
stands convicted. t
The water-waggoner protests too much.
Whether beer is a recruiting agent or not it
is tolerably certain that Cold Tea isn’t. The
figures of recruiting before and after the
carrying of six-o’clock closing may not be
an argument in favor of beer; but they cer-
tainly don’t show that drought stimulates
enlistment. • Now Cold Tea advances anti-
shouting as another means of filling up the
ranks. There isn’t the smallest ground for
supposing that it would do anything of the
kind. On the contrary, it seems probable
that mates more often decide to go together
to the recruiting office while sharing beer
than at any other time. The slight glow of
alcohol minimises the difficulties in the way.
This may be a bad thing or a good thing, but
while enlistments are voluntary and hap-
hazard it is something which must be taken
into account.
In The Bulletin last week a contributor
recalled that Holland, besides telling mothers
how to feed the baby, tells the parents in
certain cases how to keep the infant out. of
the world. On the heels of that suggestion
this comes from a contributor in another part
of Australia: —
In a truancy prosecution in a Melbourne court
the other day, the father came to answer for a
13-year-old girl who was not attending school. Ho
was a poor shadow suffering from that vague ill, a
“bad leg”—he said he could very seldom work, and
his looks bore out the plea. He added that his wife
was a chronic invalid, practically bedridden, and,
moreover, she had a baby. The 13-year-old was the
eldest of five children, two of whom were cripples,
and she “wasn’t strong.” It was necessary that
she should stay at home to look after the deplorable
household. The truant-officer’s statement confirmed
the father’s story, and it was decided to get an
exemption from further schooling for the 13-year-old.
The invalid wife and mother of cripples, please note,
had a baby —she will probably go on having them.
The same day, investigating a “missing girl” screech,
I discovered that the wanderer was the 22-year-old
eldest of a family of 11; she was what is known
as “weak,” and had a spell at the receiving-house.
Her father and mother are cousins and she has four
deaf-and-dumb brothers! A few days later I was
on a case where a man had been cut down from
the rafters of his outhouse. The Coroner gave it
as felo de se, but there was evidence of deceased
having been in the receiving-house a few months
previously. He left a poor, faded wisp of a 30-year-
old wife and eight children, the eldest aged 10.
She seemed quite relieved to be a widow. At the
time when the father’s conduct made the receiving-
house period necessary, the wife lodged the com-
plaint at the police-station with No. 8 a few days
old in her arms. “Don’t you think you ought to
cut this out?” the sergeant asked, indicating No. 8.
“I’ve tried,” she said, “but he won’t listen!” In
one of, I think, Baring-Gould’s books there is the
story of a Staffordshire village wholly peopled—the
population dating back hundreds of years—by two
families or tribes, the members of one of which
were all hunchbacks and of the other all so de-
formed that they went on all-fours. It was prob-
ably true —anything is possible in Merrie England—
but do we want to encourage that sort of thing here?
With any policy for the encouragement of popula-
tion there will need to be a parallel one for the
discouragement of some classes of it. The three
instances I have mentioned came under my notice
in one week in one Melbourne suburb.

The Vic. P.L.O. has adopted several beauti-
ful, dream-like proposals, one of which is—
That the freedom of the seas be secured on the
lines laid down by President Wilson in May, 1916,
where he advocated a universal association of nations
to maintain inviolate the security of the highway
of the seas for the common and unhindered use of all
nations of the world.
This cry for the freedom of the seas was
first raised in Germany, and what does it
mean? That Germany should be free to
ship an army unhindered to any part of the
coast of Britain, Ireland, Australia, America
or elsewhere? Or that Britain should have
the whole world’s permission to transport
troops to any country at war with Ger-
many? If he meant that it is one thing to
besiege a city, as the Germans besieged
Paris in 1870/ and quite a different thing to
blockade Hamburg, as the Allies are blockad-
ing it now, President Wilson talked non-
sense. But if he meant, as he did, undoubt-
edly, that the sinking of passenger ships or
neutral trading vessels and fishing boats by
submarines is a damnable offence against
civilisation and humanity which ought to
be prevented, if possible, by a universal
association of nations, he was merely utter-
ing the truth which everybody outside the
Central Powers already admits. But how
is it to be prevented? How is the freedom
of the seas to be secured any more than the
neutrality of Belgium was secured? What
means of doing it does the P.L.C. suggest?
Australia, being a large tract of land entirely
surrounded by water, is more interested in
this question than Germany ever can be. We
are not less concerned even than Britain in
arriving at a clear understanding of the
German-made term. It is therefore up to
the P.L.C. to drop large, fine-sounding
phrases and state precisely what it means
by proposing to maintain inviolate the secur-
ity of the watery highway to the land we
live in and how it proposes to do it.
The notoriously incapable N. S. Wales
State Wheat Office refused for many weeks to
admit that there was a. mice plague. When
it was impossible to overlook the pest a few
weary officers went languidly into the coun-
try and did the wrong thing. After prelimi-
nary bungling, men were put on at high
wages rebagging and restacking the wheat.
In some cases before the new stacks were
more than a few tiers high they began to
collapse again from fresh attacks by r .the
mice, which seem to prefer new bags. With-
out extraordinary supervision, indeed, it is
next to impossible to prevent mice being
thrown into the new stacks. Therefore there
is a good sporting chance of the “recon-
ditioning” of wheat becoming a perma-
nent industry until there art either no
more mice or no more wheat. There will
certainly be no coin left for the cocky—if
there is any cocky left. It would have been

How About Reinforcements for Billjim?
I*’ Russia is not actually out of business the
German has for the present, at any rate,
not much reason to be fearful about the
Bear leaping into his back yard. For the
Bear has not yet realised the terrible danger
his new-won liberties are in ; and though he
jnay wake up and yet take an effective part
in the disturbance, it is far more likely that
his land will be the home of chaos long after
the war is over. The Western Front is the
place where victory or defeat must be looked
for; and with the French armies now practi-
cally standing on the defensive waiting for
Uncle Sam. it is just plain lunacy to imag-
ine either that more men are not wanted or
that the Hindenbukg line is made of tissue-
paper. John Bull, Brother Jonathan
and the armies of France may pitch the
German out of Belgium. Unless they do so, the
German is going to start business again up
near our own Cape York. And that means
constant danger for Australia —until the war
is resumed. There is only one way whereby
Australia may help to avoid the liability of
having to stand practically at arms for the
future. That is by defeating the German so
thoroughly now that his jumping-off place at
Australia’s back door will not be restored to
him. If this war drags to an inconclusive
finish Australia may look confidently for-
ward to hard military training and unceas-
ing and burdensome defence expenditure. We
may not be able to escape it, anyway. The
best that we can do may not avail us. The
second-best certainly will not. Possibly by
Conscription during this war we may avoid
Conscription in the future. Bv loafing now,
by neglecting our national duty, we simply
invite an adjournment of the struggle, a
second instalment later on, and a hard mili-
tary rdgime in the meantime. Which is it
to be?

“Mclan” : Randolph Bedford (B.
24/5/’l7) refers to some proposal by a
syndicate to acquire the Northern Territory
and demands inquiry as to the price offered,
indicating his belief that it amounted in
value to a peanut. This seems very probable
in view of the effort of the Home and Terri-
tories Department to divest itself of large
slices of the country at peanut rates to
speculative syndicators, as set forth in the
Commonwealth Gazette of April 19.
Under that offer, any speculator, for the
magnificent sum of £lB9 13s. 6d. (one year’s
rent), can secure 42 years’ lease of 2029
square miles (1,298,560 acres) somewhere
on the Queensland border. For the first,
three years of the term the lessee has noth-
ing whatever to do, in order to hold the land,
except pay the yearly rent (which during
that time remains unchanged) and rates and
taxes, if any, and put 12 head of cattle upon
it. The Department may have intended the
provision for the providing of cattle to be
per sq. mile, but whatever its intention the
conditions of tender, as printed, provide
only as stated. The conditions contain no
restriction on sub-letting, so that the specu-
lator has a clear three years (which under
other conditions can be stretched, without
further outlay, to nearly seven) to cast
around for an opportunity to pass on his
concession at the maximum unearned profit
obtainable. There is certainly a condition
that the lessee is to ei'ect homesteads, but
nothing to compel him to start doing so
before the 42nd year of the term. Altogether
it looks like a gift distribution, and it would
be interesting to get a list of the names,
addresses and family connections of the for-
tunate lessees.
