“ J.11.M.A.”: I am afraid we have got into
the bad habit of producing or trying to pro-
duce reforms by lying about the necessity
for them; and that is a very dangerous thing
in a democratic country, because it often
loads to the reform taking a wrong direc-
tion. This is why I grieve to read entirely
false things about the recent execution of
one Wilson in Sydney. For instance: —
In Wilson’s case the drop was too short. Inci-
dentally the rope tore the mask off, leaving the face
revealed.
The twitching- of the muscles in that face was
horrible to behold. They twite hed horizontally, per-
pendicularly and in queer zig-zag patterns.
Naturally the weight of Wilson's body extended
his neck ; never, I verily believe, was a man’s neck,
even by the most fantastic freak of nature, so long
and so strangely thin.
The tightness of the knot darkened the blood in
the veins, which seemed to stand out from the flesh,
quivering, quivering.
Quivered, too, the muscles in the arms, and no
wonder ; for Nature, obviously against Wilson’s will,
was fighting for life on the unfortunate man’s behalf.
It fought to the last fraction of a gasp.
For ten long minutes this went on. Then the man
died—not hanged, as hanging is generally under-
stood, but STRANGLED —strangely, awfully and by
slow degrees.
This is fine, gruesome nnd realistic writing,
but it has the demerit of being wholly un-
true —which it is easy to understand when
you consider the fact that ’’Tt.J.C.,” who
wrote it, was not there. I was there, on
behalf of the Telegraph (Sydney 1 and
the only others present, besides the officials
and the corpse, were the representatives of
the three other Sydney dailies. Wilson
dropped about 10 feet, and it was impossible
to see his face twitching, because the mask
still covered it when ho hung at the end of
the rope. The usual spasmodic muscular
movements continued in the body for some
minutes, but nevertheless lie died at once,
and was no more strangled “strangely,
awfully and by slow degrees’’ than he was
beheaded. The evidence of the prison sur-
geon, Dr. Lee Brown, bears this out. With
the other press representatives I walked up
under the gallows and inspected the body
closely. We did not see anything of the sort
that “8..T.C.” describes so graphically. A
hanging is a horrible sight at any time, but
of the three that the writer has witnessed
Wilson’s was the least horrible.
Billjim has to do all sorts of things in
order to beat Hindenburg. One part of his
military duty recently in England and
France was to deliver the lying little sheet
which the I’hewsionists ran prior to the
general election. A Billjim who got up in
uniform and addressed a party political meet-
ing would find himself dealt with by the
regulations. That being so, some member
with a little decency left might inquire who
gave orders for Billjim in uniform to act as a
canvasser for a political party —and to do it
as part of his military duty.
George Burkett, State Recruiting-Officer
in Westralia. is up against new Governor
Ellison Macartney. On May 30 there was
a swell concert in Perth in aid of the Parker-
ville Waifs’ Home. His Ex. lent Govern
ment House ballroom —a huge and gorgeous
barn —for the occasion, and went to tin*
concert. The world and his missus (mostly
missus) were there. During the evening
Burkett blew along, and wanted to address
the crowd on recruiting. The management
demurred, and consulted the Governor, who
refused permission. Burkett’s indignation
was white-hot. He dashed into print next
day, and also wired Donald Maekinnon.
Then the letter-writing public got busy
and tussled out the ethics of the thing. But
Macartney hasn’t said a word.
Sydney is learning about the wharfies’
union that Prime Minister Hughes bossed
until the other day. Business men say they
lose £lOO 000 a year by cargo ’ullage: and
a special constable on wharf-duty has stated
on oath that wharf-laborers boycott honest
workers who won’t take loot. The gangers are
forced to reject these honest men when they
apply* as the dishonest ones won’t work
with them. Worse still, they are pushed
out of the Union. Made on oath, it is about
the most staggering indictment ever filed
against organised labor in Australia; and
it demands the immediate attention of the
Department of Labor. It ought to have the
immediate attention of the Trades Hall and
the Labor party, too. That there should
be such a scandal shows how badly the move-
ment has neglected educational work—and
that is the only work which can count in
the long run.
Let The Bulletin introduce an Anzac’s
sister to the whole mob. She was standing
outside the Melbourne Town Hall lately,
a rosy Gippsland girl, and heard Lieut. A.
Steege, recruiting officer, make a speech. It
wasn’t much.of a speech, but it was the first
recruiting appeal she had ever heard. It
went right home ; and when it was over she
walked up and said simply: “If the doctors
want any skin or fresh blood to assist in
operations on wounded soldiers, they can
take what I have. Do you think that would
be any help?” Steege felt something stick-
ing in his throat, but mafiaged to say he
would inquire.
Will Ashton, the Adelaide painter, back
in his native town after driving a Home
Service motor-car in and about London
since December, 1915, helped to buy the
landscape by Arnesby Brown lately added to
the Adelaide Gallery; but he and the other
selectors could not “fill” the standing order for
a good war-picture—as usual, they will be
produced from the imagination when it is all
over.

This journal freely forgives Cyril Maude
aud the management of Melbourne Royal for
having introduced it to the comedy-drama of
“Grumpy” under false pretences. In desir-
ing everybody, especially press-writers, to
“keep to themselves the solution of the
mystery contained in the play,” the manage-
ment is just pulling the public leg. Every-
body is set thinking beforehand of a mys-
stery; but the mystery has no reasonable
existence, and only appears to be made
possible in Act 2 because one was promised.
The next two acts are openly devoted to the
discomfiture of the culprit who is then known
to have stolen a £90,000 diamond in the
dense darkness at the end of Act 1. Some
dramas with diamond robberies in them have
a doubt running through the plot and a
thin surprise at the finish. This play ex-
cites very little doubt and no surprise at all,
but its business motto is
MUM.
Friend Grumpy is a lawyer keen, much worn and
bent with age—
A comic ancient, full of damns, who dominates the
stage.
He chuckles, grumbles, dozes, makes strange noises
with his nose,
And dodders round like one whose life is just about
to close;
He slaps his flunkeys’ faces and he pulls his nephew’s
ear,
But somehow they seem quite convinced that
Grumpy is a dear.
“GRUMPY," AT MELBOURNE ROYAL.
Cyril Maude
as Grumpy.
The man who lost
the diamond.
Ho calls them fools, but all the same they love old
Grumpy still
(Perhaps they all expect to be remembered in his
will) ;
And presently the fates conspire to aid his magic
spell;
He blossoms forth as Sherlock Holmes—but how, We
May Not Tell.
Virginia, his grandchild sweet, a flirty early peach,
Has two men who are struck on her, and she makes
eyes at each.
The one, her cousin Ernest, hopes to wed her by-
and-bye;
The other johnnie means to mash her promptly on
the sly ;
And Jarvis has the better chance to make the damsel
fond,
For someone knocks poor Ernest out and cops his
Diamond—
A brilliant of abnormal size confided to his care,
The loss whereof leaves Ernie poor and striekeri with
despair.
Old Grumpy’s catching of the thief, and how that
catch befell,
Provides the drama’s mystery whereof We May Not
Tell.
A valet and a footman tall, a Susan servant trim
Who teases long-haired Keble (no one likes the look
of him) ;
A large, dishonest Hebrew, beaming briefly on the
scene;
A doctor with a noisy voice and dark, forbidding
mien;
The doctor’s wife, a matron plump—twelve charac-
ters, all told—-
Make up the cast that radiates from Lawyer Grumpy
old.
And one of them commits the crime and suffers for
the sin.
But whether he or she be tall, or short, or fat, or
thin,
And hath the play a feeble end, or doth it finish
well ?
AVe could, we think, enlighten you, but, oh, WE MAY
NOT TELL!
In thus averting comment from the “mys-
tery” of the plot the management practically
blocks specific reference to the plot’s im-
probabilities. Still, they matter very little.
The play succeeds because it is a real enter-
tainment, containing a great part for an ex-
ceptionally humorous and forcible character
actor. In presenting an absurdly likeable old
man in a thoroughly human manner Cyril
Maude blinds the eyes of his audience to the
fact that the character is consistent only in
its theatrical inconsistencies. Grumpy de-
mands, first, sympathy for his funny senility
and physical feebleness, and then respect
for his sudden reckless valor in the field of
lurid melodrama. He assumes a natural air
of acuteness whilst doing silly things. Then
at a critical moment he plays the fool for the
sake of comedy effect, instead of taking
prompt action. He does both the improbable
and the impossible in a natural manner.
He is convincing in the same way that a
character in a Bernard Shaw comedy is con-
vincing. It is so amusing that you allow it
to wear a mask of reality rather than tear
off the disguise.
* * * #
In this case, apparently, the authors con-
ceived a somewhat unconventional old chap
of conventional stage type who might be
sufficiently humanised by the actor to be
able to carry four acts on his shoulders.
Cyril Maude did the humanising, and did
it very well. He is a capital actor, and he
made a capital speech after curtain-fall on
Saturday. The part of Grumpy’s grand-
daughter gives a good chance to a handsome
young actress who can suggest a beautiful
young nature in a flirtatious mood, and give
an impression of artless modesty suffering
its first shock. Miss Dorothy Cumming
plays the sweet girl charmingly. The pro-
mise she revealed when she was the unknown
Australian actress, playing Josephine to
Julius Knight’s Napoleon, has been more
than fulfilled. With only two or three excep-
tions, the acting is admirable all round. H.
Ranson (as the nephew), Edward Lester,
Frank Elliott and Betty Murray (Susan)
may be picked from the bunch, although the
merit of Elliott’s performance as the attrac-
tive Mr. Jarvis is mostly negative.
“Grumpy,” carefully staged, of course, may
be counted good for weeks to come.
>JC %
Penelope adored her husband. She loved
him not wisely but too clingsomely. As
hubby was a doctor he soon discovered a
patient who, like the barndoor fowl, gave her
lover a run for his money. When Penelope
found out she summoned the whole family
and announced that she was going to have a
divorce. However, her father, a celebrated
mathematician, added two and two together
and discovered that the result was four.
Applying his twelve-cylinder brain to this
problem, and incidentally disclosing a re-
markable knowledge of the sex that always
makes five the sum of two plus two, he
diagnosed hubby’s complaint to be the result
of too much wifely adoration. His remedy
was the old one, so successfully applied on
the stage and so invariably a failure in real
life —that of deliberately throwing hubby
into the other cat’s claws. The stage conse-
quence is that hubby gets sick of the other
Avoman’s cuddlesome ways and yearns to re-
turn to a wiffe grown strangely indifferent.
Working out this plot Somerset Maugham
brings to it a delightful mastery of clever
dialogue and an appreciation of high comedy.
It takes him, however, an act and a half
before he really interests his audience; but
thenceforth the play moves with a swift
logic and many surprises to a neat and con-
vincing end. The third act is a masterpiece
of dramatic construction and unsuspected
situations.

The production of “Penelope,” at Sydney
Criterion on Saturday, was thrown out of
gear by the failure of Sidney Stirling to
make the figure of the mathematical father a
dominating character: the author’s intention
was obviously to make the Professor the
pivot of the play. Graham Browne provides
another of his finished comedy characterisa-
tions, _ delightfully differentiated from his
part in “Kitty.” Leslie Victor, always reli-
able, throws himself with gusto into the
part of the sort-of-conscious snob never seen
except behind the footlights. As the charac-
ter is a pure stage convention Leslie Victor’s
overacting does not matter. Gwen Bur-

Gerald Massey arrested part of the atten-
tion of a good house at Melbourne Athe-
meum on Saturday. Gerald belongs to the
school of popular reciters who stroll over
battlefields on the arm of Kipling, short-
handing the converse of Thomas Atkins and
his kind. The artist’s programme is largely
a profusion of heroes, cemeteries and
women’s tears, but occasionally he sweeps
aside the gun-smoke to peer into the soul of
a Yarra banker or sketch the vanities of a
London bloke. Gerald Massey’s art is fair
descriptive reporting in Kiplingesque vein,
and, though never sounding the real thing,
is a presentable counterfeit in sentiment and
humor. Four appearances ending next Satur-
day night will give the reciter a chance to
expose the resources of his repertoire and
enable a supporting company of five to effec-
tively decorate his breathing spaces. Among
his supporters are a taking baritone, Tom
Minogue, and a strong-voiced songstress.
Florence Fawaz, who makes a young and
passionate thing from “Samson and Delilah”
as bloodless as a Sunday-school glee.
“Turn to the Right,” already in its ninth
week at Sydney I’alace, will pack up its
peach preserve on June 29.
The successful run of that masterpiece,
“The Story of the Rosary,” was curtailed by
the Fuller management at Sydney Grand
Opera House in order to allow students of the
Elizabethan drama an opportunity to study
a curious antique melodrama, apparently
popular some centuries ago. This composi-
tion (by a hack dramatist or somebody else
of the same name) is called “Hamlet,” and
deals in a loose, vague way with the scan-
dals of the Court of Denmark. The melo-
drama is full of hackneyed quotations and
long speeches—even the ghost is garrulous—
and the author doesn’t seem to have been
sure of his facts. In the name-part Allan
Wilkie even excels his masterly work in the
previous play. To-night will be the last
opportunity to see him and Fredetc. Watts.
This lady makes a charming Ophelia in a
play that would make a better film drama.
On Saturday night the Italian Grand Opera
Co. will return to stay a few weeks. “II
Trovatore” will be followed by nightly
changes.

“Canary Cottage” improves on acquaint-
ance—perhaps because the listener doesn’t
worry about following the,cobweb plot of the
gay insanity the second time he visits Syd-
ney Majesty’s. These shows should offer
special term to beginners.
* * *
Ada Reeve is booming as gaily as ever at
Sydney Tivoli. So far as Australia’s experi-
ence goes, she is head of the class of vaude-
ville artists who have to make real escapes
from their clamorous audiences. The
orchestra has to cover the retreat of many
other practitioners. Ada’s method is de-
lightfully quiet. First she comes on and
gives the audience a cool song-drink with
something in it; then she mixes another
insidious one a trifle stronger; so by degrees
she gets the house going properly, till it
allows her to take its collective wrist and
pump in about a quart of ragtime with a
hypodermic syringe. When that lot gets in
its deadly work the patient smiles a sweet,
drugged smile and allows the chloroform
mask to be clapped on without a kick. Louis
Nikola (of the silhouette finger-portraits) is
the most noticeable member of the support-
ing co.

Shadow drama in Sydney this week: —-
Lyceum: Australian Enid Bennett in five reels of
Triangle “Happiness.”
Crystal Palace : A capital film version of “The
Argyle Case,” the old favorite mystery-murder drama.
Hoyt’s: Nance O’Neil as the severe iron-mistress
in Margaret Deland’s novel, “The Iron Woman” ; and
Dorothy Bernard in a gorgeous love-drama, “The
Rainbow.”
At Melbourne Majesty’s the audacity of
Dondidier in kissing the Baroness Young
upon the cheek continues to be an important
factor in the success of “The Pink Lady.
There are, however, more than one factor in
the factory, and one of these fine weeks two
or three other contributing causes will be
alluded to.
“The White Chrysanthemum” lias just six
more nights to run at Melbourne King’s.
The co. moves along after Thursday, the
28th, and “Peg o’ My Heart” returns, with
her rough dawg and her rude laugh, on the
following Saturday.
Allan Wilkie is returning to Melbourne
Princess’s on Saturday, but not with any
immediate designs on the “legitimate.” The
honorable apostle of Shakspeare has earned
many ducats in Sydney by playing “The
Story of the Rosary,” and he is going to
repeat the lucrative indiscretion in Mel-
bourne. When the melodrama was played at
the Royal a year or two ago, it seemed
eminently suited to popular prices. At the
Princess’s it will get what suits it, and be
displayed to the best advantage.
The experiment of producing “Sweet
Nell” in four weekly parts was very warmly
received at Melbourne Tivoli on Saturday.
Nellie Stewart was expected, on reports from
Sydney, to justify this new form of revival,
and the picturesque embodiment of perpet-
ual young womanhood sailed right up to the
expectation. As far as the star lady and the
setting of the scene were concerned the past
seemed the present. Blakiston was again
an effective Charles the Mormon; the attend-
ant bigwigs passed inspection; Nancye
Stewart was a painstaking Lady Olivia, and
Jeffreys had all the ferocity of his most
savage predecessors in an acute form. - The
least satisfactory titled person on the job
was Sir Roger Fairfax, a very mild young
lover who seemed rather overcome by his
weight of tailoring and haberdashery. Act 2
of the gay romance begins next Saturday.
Of the various turns that make the rest of
the bill Gwen Lewis, the Yateses and the
rough-and-tumble burlesque scored most
approval. Some of the others dragged,
flagged or sagged.
In “The Conquest of Canaan,” a Hoyt
(Melbourne) picture adapted by the camera
from Booth Tarkington’s story, Joe Louden,
the hero, decides to bash his way into the
affections of a hostile community; wherefore
much of the film might be mistaken for a
first-aid demonstration by the Red Cross
Society. There are, however, some peaceful
passages wherein the Big Fist is held in the
thrall of Edith Taliaferro’s fatal beauty.
Jean Sothern is graceful in the second Hoyt
item, “The Cloud,” which features self-sacri-
fice and other curiosities.
Pretty Enid Bennett is spread over five
acts of exertion in the luce film, “The Little
Brother,” at Melbourne Majestic this week.
Enid, masquerading sweetly in masculine
garments, pursues a familiar career of Hope,
Love and Conquest, which suggests that the
Ince bump of originality is little more than
a pimple, after all. William Desmond, whom
Australia saw in “The Lion and the Mouse,”
“The Silver King ” and other plays, looks
lovely enough to be everywoman’s Secret
Fancy in a new Ince-Sterling production,
“The Last of the Ingrams.” The Majestic
also stars a humorous item. “Villa of the
Movies,” which is guaranteed to make any
bishop feel frivolous.
s': *
The “Bunyip” panto, has ended at
Adelaide Majestic, where remain Phillips,
Rene, Daisy Merritt and other of its fun-
makers in a loosely-built “revue.” The
Anderson co., which always impresses one
as being too good for the kind of play it
stages, has for the moment a monopoly of
drama at the Tivoli; “Between Two
Women” is the thrill of the week. The
Dandies at the Queen’s are coming to the
end of their season. Winsloe Hall's new
orchestra gave a concert which was shock-
ingly ill patronised, Wondergraph has a
pleasant film of a hero and heroine hand-
cuffed together and shooting themselves
asunder, West’s screens an elaboration of
the fine old Jim Bludso ballad, and the
stupendous “Intolerance” at the Royal is in
its second week of a twice-a-day season.
Four more detectives have been appointed
by Chief Sec. McLeod to act as oil on the
wave of crime now rolling over Melbourne.
Their appointment would have been more
satisfactory if it were not that, as in the
case of every other d. at headquarters, the
latest additions originally entered the police
service on the strength of their size in boots
rather than on their intellectual brilliance.
They mounted the ladder to d.-ship, no
doubt, through strict attention to business;
but the fact remains that had their feet and
other parts below the neck not been up to
certain specifications, their brains would
have had no chance.
AT POVERTY POINT.
A graceful little souvenir of the late R. S.
Smytlie, issued b.v the family to sympathis-
ing friends, explains how he came to be
known as the “Much Travelled.” Some of
his Melbourne cronies gave him the title 40
years ago, and “it acquired a wider and per-
manent popularity by its use in an early
issue of The Bulletin, which lirst fixed it
in print.” Smythe’s much-travelling had
begun nearly 20 years earlier, and a lot of
it might fairly have been called adven-
turing. As a pioneer among impresarios
R.S.S. earned his title honestly enough. In
his old age he talked jokingly of passing it
on to son Carlyle, and calling himself “The
More Travelled.” But the name that pleased
the old journalist-showman best was con-
ferred on him by another journalist whom he
toured through Australia. Archibald Forbes
termed him “The Moltke of Managers.”
“0.K.”: The two authors of “Grumpy” are
best remembered in Australia for their act-
ing in Wilson Barrett’s first production of
“The Manxman.” Horace Hodges played the
little cad who came along on a bicycle, and
Wigney Percyval was the slim seducer of
the heroine. Their first attempt at play-
writing has brought them £20,000 thus far.
Belasco and Frohman turned it down after
Cyril Maude had refused it. Then the
authors worried him again, and this time he
saw the light. Wigney Percyval, by the
way, was not a member of the second co. that
Wilson Barrett brought to Australia (1901),
his place being taken by Basil Gill, who has
since made a bit of a reputation for himself.
Gill was a picturesque young man, and, basil
being the trade name for leather, needless to
say envious rivals christened him Leather
Gills.
“Monkbarns” : Cyril Maude is the greatest
comedian we have had since Jefferson. I
rate them about equal. Yet they are very
different. Jefferson, as Hazlitt wrote of
Bannister, put every character over himself,
like a surtout, warm and comfortable. Ex-
cept in Rip, he was always the same
delightful personage, like Charley Mathews.
Maude, on the other hand, is the real charac-
ter actor. He gets into the skin. Compare
his portrait with his make-up. and it is
rather difficult to see the real man struggling
through. This may be said of him in several
characters. For his Captain Barley, in
and the Barge,” so admirably copied
mp Bob Brough, Maude spent many days in
association with the actual sort of eccen-
tricity on a canal, and even borrowed his
catchword, “Too much haffability!” Thorn-
ton told me he found his Private Secretary
in a curate, between two ladies in a London
’bus, exclaiming, “Do you know? Oh,
fancay!” I think his curate was better than
either Tree’s or Penley’s.
A Cyril Maude yarn. When Tree appeared
as Hamlet, the Second Gravedigger was
played by an old pro. named Allen. The
show was witnessed by Irving, who went
behind at the close. Tree approached him
expectantly. “Well?” he inquired. “I
thought Allen was excellent —most excel-
lent,” Irving told him.
“S.” : William Somerset Maugham crowds
a surprising quantity of home truth into his
comedy, “Penelope.” It contains something
of Wilde’s painful reflection that it is the
faithfulness of women, not their unfaithful-
ness, that is tragic. Maugham’s hero is
driven to distraction by the overweening
fondness of his wedded wife. The distraction
he seeks is in the person of a very good-look-
ing young grass-widow, who, however, to his
greater horror, develops precisely the traits
of the excessively affectionate spouse of his
bosom. This drives O’Farrell back to his
apparently cooling wife, and the tragic-
comedians end where they began, with man
and wife restored to each other’s arms, beset
by a prospect of the linked sweetness being
long drawn out precisely as before. The
wife’s common-sensible old father, a mathe-
matician, offers his daughter much sage
advice, but does not give the one true solu-
tion of the O’Farrells' difficulty, nor does it
seem to have occurred to Maugham. The
real need of such households is just a little
one. The safety-valve is baby. The trouble
is not that Mrs. O’Farrell is too loving, but
that, after four or five years of married life,
O’Farrell is still called upon to endure the
love that is rightly the husband’s, as well as
the love that is rightfully the baby’s.
Naturally he suffers from the frequent over-
doses. Maugham’s saving grace in Act 111
should have been the midwife.
“Thersites”: When war broke out very
many elderly actors and actresses fled from
England to North America—old ladies and
gentlemen with more or less fame and abil-
ity. Now they can’t get home again, and in
consequence every theatrical circuit in the
States is using some London popular actor
who can’t knit socks and can barely act
from old age. I have just met John Drew, a
famous American actor who looks 80. The
old chap was very acidy that afternoon and
snorted when asked whether lie would follow
Cyril Maude down to Australia. “Pooh,”
said Drew, “Austi-alia ain’t nothing,” and he
spat on the hotel carpet. “Say, did y’ever
hear what Nat Goodwin said about Aus-
tralia?” “You mean the American Henry
the Eighth?” says I. “Yes,” says he. “You
know Nat was a failure down there and lost
his dallars. Somebody asked him what he
thought of Sydney. ‘Gee,’ replied Nat of
The Milk White Flag, ‘that ain’t no city,
That’s Troy, Noo Yark, on a wet Sunday.’ ”
The reply is caustic because Troy is a dirty,
dilapidated factory-town all slums and mud.
Cyril Maude, who wears his burden of years
well, has been playing grandpa parts in the
U.S.A. since the war opened, varied occa-
sionally by after-dinner speeches into which
he always wedges “My cousin, you know,
General Maude of Mesopotamia.” When
old Sir George Alexander and old Sir
Charles Wyndharn have toured Australia, it
will have seen all the London favorites of
the early-Victorian era.
“Silas”: To my mind Florence Young’s
La Comtesse in “The Pink Lady” is easily
the most satisfactory thing this performer
has accomplished for years. It is only lately
that Florence has learned to act. Her
attempts in frivolous youthful characters
had always a jarring element. Her vivacity,
coquetry, canoodling and airy badinage
were always stiff and artificial. In a part
like La Comtesse she swings into place. The
thing is done neatly but with force, and the
singer begins to look like an actress and
behave accordingly, while the voice remains
quite fine enough to guarantee her even
greater popularity in a line in which she is
temperamentally and physically much more
at home. Flo’s singing of “Dondidier” is
the best example of purely comic vocalisa-
tion we have had since the great old Gilbert
and Sullivan period. By the way, how
comes it that while the neat and nice but
wholly conventional waltz strain of “The
Pink Lady” has been drummed into every
head during the past few years, we never got
a hint of the exceptionally clever and
uniquely humorous “Dondidier”?
“B. J.”: Sylvia Willes, who was born in
Melbourne 20 years ago, has just died in
England. She was a daughter of Christo-
pher Lionel Willes and a grand-niece of Sir
George O’Mahoney Willes, and was 25Ain.
short and 19-Jlb. light. It was the last-
mentioned facts that helped her to make a
fortune in the show business as “Princess
Victoria.” She had a sweet soprano, and was
billed as “The Midget Melba.” Apart from
her weight and height, she was normal. A
few years back she made very big money by
singing, playing sundry more or less musical
instruments and selling her own picture-
postcards. Later she lost most of her for-
tune. “Through mismanagement,” her
mother said.
Rosin a Palmerston, whose bright musical
turn was lately on the Fuller circuit, is the
daughter of the late Christie Palmerston, who
explored a lot of the North Queensland coun-
try and gave his name to the Palmerston
Falls, above Cairns.
Stella Power, the “little Melba” of the
Melbourne advts., inherited a fine voice from
her late mother. Mrs. Power never sang
professionally, but her sister, Josephine-
O’Brien, was a well-known figure on the-
Melbourne concert platform a few years ago.
“Pollard”: “J.F.H.” (B. 10/5/’l7) is
wrong about Daphne Pollard. She is an.
Australian, but not a member of the theatri-
cal family. She went to America with one
of the many Pollard Lilliputian cos., in which
the practice was to give some of the leads
the family handle. Daphne caught the
N’Yarkers in 1915 with her diminutive
cuteness while with the Follies, after a rather
quiet spell in vaudeville. A Maoriland con-
tingent in Blighty got the tip that she was
the daughter of Tom. and the. London
Hippodrome became a Mecca for the boys
until the error was righted. And then they
went because she was Daphne.

CALLS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When first I heard her coo-ee wing
Down from the g'reen hillside,
The lilting magpie in the spring,
Who set my early morn a-ring,
Seemed dull and lacking pride.
“Coo-ee! Coo-ee!”
A siren call it was to me—
“Coo-ee!”
At scented dawn when by the creek
The bird-choir voices vie
From wattled slope and gilded peak,
I only heard the angels speak
In Milly’s matin cry.
“Coop-coop ! Coop-coop ! ”
As homeward strung the milking troop—
“Coop-coop 1”
And now when roars the eager tram,
And clangs the hated bell,
I. drowse serenely where I am,
And do not give a single yam
For Milly’s strident yell:
“C’up-c’up! C’up-c’up!
It's seven, you lazy hound, get up !
C’up-e’up!”
VICTORIA.

Another freak for the records of. surgerj.
Some months ago at Orange East (N.SAN .)
a man had his leg amputated. Last week a

“Daru”: The law of Papua does not
allow of divorce being obtained, when
one of the parties to the marriage is
a full-blooded Papuan aboriginal. The
marriage law allows a fool white man
to tie himself to the brown woman, but the
divorce ordinance prevents any relief to
either party to these mixed and always
unfortunate wed-
dings. Evidently
the framers of the
law had the idea
that by preventing
divorce from* the
Papuan partner of
the marriage the
wedding of white
and brown would
also be prevented.
But this is turning
the thing wrong
way up. The right
way to prevent
marriage is to pre-
vent marriage, not
to stand in the way
of divorce. “Com-
bos” are not very
numerous in Pa-
pua, but one or two
gentlemen of my
acquaintance who
in their salad days
took unto them-
selves discolored
wives are now in
their sensible years
doomed to be tied
for life to women
that they long ago
lost any respect or
affection for. The
wowser would say
it is a very fitting
punishment; but a
sensible man who
sees a young fellow just past his majority
tied to an immoral black female cannot but
grieve that there is no chance of relief.

“Pajaud": The orderly-sergeant with two
defaulters awaited the arrival of the C.O.
lie appeared shortly and “’Shun!" yelled
the orderly-sergeant. The C.O. gravely-
saluted and ducked inside. “S’n’at-ease/”
snarled the orderly-sergeant. In the orderly
room the C.O. found the sergeant-major.
“Good-morning. Sawn-Majaw, any cases this
morning?” “Yes, sir, two this morning,
sir,” and he handed over the yellow forms
setting out the offences. The Major glanced
at the top one. “Ah, Redwood again! March
him in, Sawn-Majaw.” The S.M., like many
another good soldier, made a bloomer, and
marched in the wrong man, and the C.O.
failed to notice the mistake. He read out
the charge—“ No. 1547, Pte. J. B. Redwood.
You are charged with (1) absent without
leave, (2) drunk, and (3) bringing liquor
into camp. How do you plead?” The aston-
ished defaulter cleared his throat and gasped
out, “I ain’t got nothin’ ter say t’ that
charge. I ain’t” “That’ll do,” jerked
out the C.O. “You got nothing to say.
Absent! Drunk ! Bringing liquor into camp !
I’ll make an example of you. How dare you
bring liquor into camp? Don’t you know
that no one, no matter what his rank, is
allowed to bring liquor into camp?” The
prisoner here jambed his word in. “I ain’t
Redwood,” he said. “Redwood’s the cove out-
side, sir. I’m the other bloke, wot pinched
the bottle o’ whisky outer your tent last
night!’

“Chevron” :
Unto the last, whate’er that last may be—
Or day, or night;
Silence, or psalm ; defeat, or victory—
Endure and fight.
Put it in bronze for the epitaph of Lieut.
Charles Pope, a Westralian of the
Battalion, who in. obedience to orders ex-
changed his life for the Victoria Cross and
added one more ray of glory to Lagnicourt.
He and his men held a picket which they
were told to keep at all costs. On April 15
the Germans developed the tremendous at-
tack which, until General Robert Smith, of
the Fifth Australian Division, organised a
dashing counter-attack and shot the Huns
to pieces, threatened disaster indeed. Pope
and his men, cut off before the tide turned,
fired volley after vollev into the rolling waves
of grey’. But soon they were in great need
of ammunition, and it was impossible to
get it through the hordes of Germans. There
was only one thing to do, and Pope did it,
making immortal in France, as another offi-
cer of the same name made immortal in Gal-
lipoli. the name of Pope’s Post. Our
troops were a mile away—too far to aid, but
not too far to see. Battalion after battalion
of the enemy debouched upon Pope at his
post in the valley of death. Still his few
rifles spoke defiance; and while men watched
an hour went by. Half an hour more.
Then silence. What now? Our eyes were
answered by the twinkling glory of bayo-
nets. Springing to their feet and charging
down hill, on came what was left of the
piequet. Light flashed on their bayonets,
flashed again, and flashed no more. The
ocean of field-grey swallowed the little cata-
ract of khaki. When vengeance had been
made complete, the bodies of Pope and five
of his men were found together. Others
were picked up where thev had fallen, and
about that scattered ring were numbered SO
German dead.

Parson George Tait, who has tended the
Scotchbyterian flock at South Yarra (Vic.)
for many years, distinguished himself at the
recent General Assembly by talking com-
monsense about derrink. Before the Assem-
bly cussedly adopted a “deliverance” favor-
ing national prohibition, Tait protested
earnestly that prohibition was to be con-
demned as a “short cut” to a moral end.
and one which “pro-
duced greater evils
than those they were
attempting to cure.”
Tait is a scholarly per-
son, recognised among
the brethren as having
one of the keenest
minds in the church—
a reverend battler who
is prepared to shed the
last drop of ink in de-
fence of his conclu-
sions. But he stood
almost alone in mak-
ing his protest, the
seconder of the lost
amendment to the “de-
liverance” being a lay
brother. Now, with
renewed vigor and—ah
—s tre ngthened pur-
pose, the church mili-
tant will continue try-
ing to swat the fly on
its nose, while as the
blow falls the little
critter hops leisurely
across to the nearest
eyebrow.
“A.E.L.” describes an air-fight:—
A little before dusk I counted 12 of our battle
’planes go over in a bunch. From the aerodrome
on the hill opposite, the guiding flares glowed till
well after eight o’clock—pitch dark then —and at
intervals searchlights swept the heavens for the
latecomers. One, I knew, they searched for in
vain. He would never return. While those pencils
of dazzling light were trying to “pick him up,” he
lay . splashed on the brown of a muddy field.
This machine had left the bunch and, encountering ,
a German, a fight ensued. The splutter of a machine-
gun drew our eyes in the direction. There they
were, circling round each other, diving, banking,
swooping, the drone of their engines broken by spas-
modic bursts of machine-gun fire.
The two machines were of different types. W e
knew our machine. We knew the Fritz. Presently
our fellow dived steeply, and we confidently waited
for him to swoop up and catch his opponent napping.
But no! Down, down he came, until it dawned on
us that he might have been hit—tnat the fight had
resulted in a victory for the German !
While yet many thousand feet up he began a series
of magnificent spirals, coming lower and lower with
each one, until at a hundred feet from the ground
he “flattened out” for his landing. The airman had
shut off his engine, but the propeller still revolved,
though with a soft purring sound, so different from
the roar and throb of the engine in full blhst. It
was only a matter of seconds now when he’d be
racing lightly along the ground, close to us —and
we’d go over and see what was wrong. Lower,
lower, 50 feet up now, 40—but didn’t he look splen-
did! Yet ugly. Nobody would think of calling that
snub-nose thing anything but ugly, in that nose sat
the observer, with his handy little Lewis gun stick-
ing out in front. Only his head could be seen.
Behind him sat the pilot, and behind him again was
the engine, with the propeller revolving in the rear
of the main wing-span. Only 30 feet to go now.
You’re home and dried, old man. Twenty-five feet,
20—ah !
It happened in a moment. 1 saw it, yet did not
see it, it was all over so quickly. 1 looked at it
stupidly, bewildered, sickened. What a moment
before had been a living thing now lay with the
life smashed out of it, a confusion of torn fabric and
twisted wire and splintered wood, with its tail
sticking in the air and quivering with the violence
of the impact. For, from that little height, no
more than 20 feet, the machine had crashed nose-
first into the field.
It was some seconds before 1 could gather my
wits. The rest of us must have been in the same
state. Then we all suddenly made for the wreck,
arriving to see the observer crawl from underneath
his shattered cab. He was wounded in the head.
The pilot; was dead. 11c lay underneath the engine,
smashed to a pulp.
The observer told us the story. The German had
swooped down on them, and had wounded him early
in the fight. Finding himself hit shortly afterwards,
the pilot brought his machine down to within 20 feet
of the ground, under splendid control. Then he lost
consciousness, and so “went West.”

“Willie over the Water”: In close touch
with soldiering right tip to the hot edge of
the fighting line for a year now, I am further
from a definition of courage than ever. In
fact, the more I hear and see of brave men
the harder I find it to say what bravery is.
One finds men whom no one would suspect
of possessing any temperamental force doing
the most audacious things when up against
the enemy. One thing only is certain —
bravery and fear go together, even bravery
and terror. In a Red Cross tent back
here the other day, a nurse heard one
wounded Maorilander earnestly colloguing
with another who had done a splendid thing.
“What didja do it for? There was no sense
in it. It done no good. You did it, Ned,
jest to let everybody see how brave you
was.” “No.” Ned replied, quietly, “so that
nobody would guess how frightened I was.”

FOR THE SINGING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“War, from its very nature, could never produce
great poetry.” —American magazine.
Will the world sing again,
Like the birds after rain,
When the blood-storm is over?
Will the mirth of good-heart
From the years’ meadows start
As the lark from the clover?
Long the grey shadow grew
Over ways that we knew.
At the feast and love-making
The chill of a fear
Clipped the word in the ear
And the kiss in the taking.
Life ran like a stream
Through grey meadows of dream
To a hollow place under —•
Gliding on, gliding on,
From chill dusk to red dawn,
Towards the cataracts’ thunder.
No place for a song
Had the years hushed and long
With the death hammers falling,
When steel to grey steel,
With its keen-edged appeal,
Made clamorous calling.
Will the sun shine out clear
In the prime of the year
When the death-smoke is rifted ?
With pure heart of clear sight
Shall the bridal veils white
From the future be lifted?
Little graves far and cold
To the night winds have told
Of strange sleeping and waking—
O Dead that are ours,
There are garlands of flowers
And songs for the making!
In the dark ye dream well.
But the long glories swell
Where the star-lamps are swinging.
Lo, ye died for world-song,
And the Man-heart grows strong
To the ultimate singing!
x.s.w.

“0. Klin”: The reappearance of W. E.
Johnson on the Speaker’s perch in the Reps,
sent the House staff scurrying for the appur-
tenances of his office. The mace, sentenced
to three years’ imprisonment by the pre-
vious work-a-day Speaker, was released from
its dungeon amid the loud cheers of Bruce
Smith, while the old wig and gown were
relieved of their mothballs and restored to
their former official positions. Johnson is
a great stickler for Parliamentary etiquette
and is not unmindful of the, so to speak,
perks of his billet. Presumably he will
return to roost in the suite of apartments
kindly nrovided at Parliament House, and
refurnished a while back at disgusting ex-
pense—as a means, of course, of winning the
war. Clapin, Johnson’s new bodyguard on
impressive occasions, is probably the short-
est sergeant-at-arms on record. If brevity
is the soul of wit, he would make an ideal
editor for Ally Sloper. When I saw the
little fellow burdened with the mace at the
opening of Parliament the other day, I felt
like lodging a protest with the S.P.C.C.

“Copy Boy”: In their encounters with
law-breakers, Victorian John Hops are
mostly at a disadvantage, because they lack
the up-to-date appliances with which their
opponents are careful to provide themselves.
When a policeman, full of dark suspicions,
entered the Hawksburn branch of the State
Savings Bank the other night, not being
provided with a lamp or torch he had to
strike matches to find his way about. Two
matches had burned out and he was fum-
bling for a third when he was knocked
unconscious with a jemmy, and three men
who had had designs on the bank-safe
escaped. When policemen surprised
a gang of safe-breakers at Mel-
bourne Trades Hall a year or two
back they had to find their way
about the dark building by striking
matches, thus providing excellent
targets for the gang, firing from
the darkness. One constable was
killed, and it then occurred to the
Department that it would be advis-
able to purchase a few electric
torches. Twelve (12) —one (1)
dozen—were bought and are safely
housed at the Russell-street head-
quarters. Presumably the duty of
any constable about to enter a building in
which he suspects thieves are at work is to
sign a requisition for the use of one of these
and despatch it by special messenger to head-
quarters.
The big Eider Hall, at Adelaide Uni. Con-
servatorium, built for music and singing,
gives good results for both, but is a shock-
ing place to speak in, the reverberations be-
ing deadly. Various experiments with wires
and muffled hangings have been tried —it
had become really a matter of some conse-
quence now that all the biggest T niversity
functions have to be held there. But noth-
ing did much good until last week Professor
Kerr Grant was given a free hand for a
special occasion. He simply carpeted the
platform and the floor with layers of wheat-
sacks. and the result was excellent. It took
five tons of them to do the trick, though.
From an Australian surgeon in London:
We are put “on our honor” not to eat more than
so much flour, meat, sugar, etc. ; but honor seems to
be almost as scarce as potatoes. We went with a
friend a few nights ago to a well-known rendezvous
in Soho. We did not order a special dinner, nor did
we take the most expensive one submitted.
Well, we had first-class soup, a huge salmon
steak (a meal in itself), a most liberal
mixed grill (cutlet, kidney, sausage, bacon, tomato
and mushrooms), vegetables, bread and butter, pine-
apple, with coffee to finish off. We could not eat
our supply, neither could my friend, a big strapping
Billjim, over on leave and so not previously over-
fed. All around us in that crowded restaurant people
were doing themselves in the same or a much more
generous fashion. And so all day and every day, in
dozens of these places, the wants of the food hogs are
supplied ; and authority appears to take no notice of
it all. But you find the “middle-class” and “lower
middle-class” people taking very serious notice of it;
and I am convinced of this —that, quite unconsciously,
the idle or self-indulgent rich are making a whip of
scorpions for their backs. If they will not make
voluntary sacrifices to-day, they are going to be com-
pulsorily sacrificed to-morrow.

The Defence Department says there is no
truth whatever in this correspondent’s state-
ment :
I hear that our officers in Egypt are permitted
by the authorities to keep small harems; that is,
they are allotted two wives each, these wives being
chosen for them. My informant is the wife of an
officer with the Australian Imperial Force in Egypt,
and one would hardly disbelieve her word. But can
you find out whether there is any truth in the state-
ment, and, if so, whether our officers are availing
themselves of the privilege? Others who have heard
this statement would like to know definitely. It
worries me greatly.
But if it were true —officially so, that is—
ivhat a boom there would be in recruiting!

“Cherokee”: The N. S. Wales Govern-
ment’s bright idea of income-taxing the N. S.
Wales wheat cocky, not on what he has but on
what peradventure he may get from the
Wheat Pool, reminds me - of what has hap-
pened to a man in Gippsland. He was a
Fremantle wharfie, and won £4OOO in
Tatt.’s. Incited thereto by many voices
murmuring of Thrift, he rejected the spaci-
ous notion of drinking his way through
£4OOO worth of beer, and bought a farm.
All was well, and he was patting himself
on the back for being virtuous, when sud-
denly the Income Tax Department hit him
up for £9OO tax, and over £lOO fine for fail-
ure to disclose the amount of his lucky
year’s income.
The everlasting Snow case got a step fur-
ther while the High Court sat in Adelaide;
or rather, it took a step backward, and the
end looks further off than that of the war
which started it. Put in words as plain as
are possible in a tangled matter, the High
Court said that the Full Court of S. Aus.
was wrong in saying that its own Chief
Justice was wrong in a decision he had
given about certain documents used by Snow
in his metal-trading business, which docu-
ments might—or, again, might not —go to
show that he was trading, or trying to trade,
with the enemy. Sitting on himself in the
Full Court, the C.J. seemed to consider that
his own former decision was dubious. It is
now referred back to him for a kind of
third-thought. The whole case is the near-
est approach to a gold-mine that Adelaide
Iv.C.’s have encountered professionally for
years. On the “Crown” side there appears
to be rancorous feeling on the part of some-
body in a high place ; on the other side—
Snow r ’s side—there is money, backed by
Josiah Symon, who has used some remark-
ably straight talk at times.

The Age critic slates the work of local
amateurs: —■
Another instance of purposeless losses resulting*
from the evident inexperience of the shopbreakers
who have been operating in the city and suburbs
during the last few months was revealed at Carnegie
yesterday. The thieves use explosives in the most
reckless and extravagant manner. This involves
danger to themselves and risk of discovery owing' to
the great noise caused by the use of unnecessary
explosives. These facts indicate that the men are
novices at the game.
What are the business colleges doing? You
can’t even get a decent correspondence course
on the subject.
MESSINES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The biggest thing ever attempted in mining opera-
tions rent the air with air-splitting crashes.” — Cable.
Now fades the tinpot tinkle of the guns,
The lisping howitzers seem faint and small.
Like the weak thud of currants dropped from buns
Are bombs that from the soaring biplane fall.
Like rustling leaves a million rifles sigh
When the long breeze of battle pushes by.
Dig and dig deep! A railway tunnel gores
A hollow in the death-encrusted hill.
Throug'h the black mouth a patient vengeance pours
Explosive fury, fettered, curbed and still.
Above, a battle rages day and night;
Below are reefs and seams of dynamite.
Now give them warning. Pour the red bolts in!
Scourge their defences hotly night and day !
Assure them the attack will soon begin
And bid them call reserves to face the fray!
These guns are like a clamorous dinner-bell
That calls the foemen to the feast of Hell.
Behind those broken walls and ruined trees
They wait in thousands for the coming clash.
No fire can shift them from such hills as these —
They grin to watch the waste of steel and cash !...
Ready! Switch on the ruin deeply laid....
Beneath the hill our strategy is laid.
There is a noise. The world goes deaf and blind.
Something is lifting. All the sky is red.
The hill? There is no hill that eye can find.
But one vast dust-cloud rolling- overhead,
Loaded with guns and armies and wild flame,
Shrieks to the sky the terror of our name.
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.


In one of the recent Melbourne*
burglaries the criminals

PERSONAL
ITEMS
MELACHRING
George Patterson, of Melbourne, who died
last week, aged 80, was a cheery pioneer of
’•"»!. With his brother J.B. (afterwards
Premier of Victoria and K.C.M.G.) he
stepped oil the vessel after a 10-months’
voyage and tried his luck on the diggings;
out typhus drove him back to Melbourne.
Emerging from hospital with empty pockets
the youngster got a temporary job to look
after a, cab for an hour or two while the
driver went to lunch, with a hint to “talk
money” if a fare happened along. A digger
and two Flossies flung him a tenner and
demanded a drive to St. Ivilda, where there
was then one pub- He charged them another
fiver for the return trip, and then retired
from the business to pursue the dollar at
horse and cattle dealing and' mail-coach run-
ning. From the late ’GO’S he and .T.B. carried
on a Collins-street real-estate business in a
big way, being followed by two sons, one of
whom. T.A., married Melba’s sister. Another
son, that good soldier Colonel George Patter-
son, was Q.M.G. of the A.I.F. at the Gal-
lipoli landing, and died last year; while Cap-
tain Charles Patterson, of the Lancashire
Fusiliers, died of injuries at the end of 1914.
Ambrose McCarthy Patterson, another son.
lives at Honolulu, where he paints pictures.
E. H. SUGDEN, M.A., of Queen’s,
Melbourne, and one time musical critic.
Norman Marshall, who got into the ring
as sergeant-major, is now a lieut.-colonel,
and" one Melbourne man is not at all sur-
prised. That is “Jack” Davidson, editor of
Melbourne Herald, who boxed with him in
Fred Porter’s gymnasium, and was in his
corner when he won the middleweight
amateur championship of Victoria five years
ago. Norman, who is 31, and a son of the
Rev. Alexander Marshall, of Scots’ Church,
was originally No. 9G2 in ”F“ (Public
Schools) Company of the sth (Victorian)
Battalion. He won the Military Cross in
Gallipoli, where he brought into play his
accumulated experience as boxer, oarsman,
footballer and paper-mills manager.

PERCY
BROOKFIELD,
of Broken Hill.
Adelaide has discovered a brilliant young
short-story teller. lie turned up on the
wharves at Port Adelaide, a pathetic little
figure, aged 124, child of French parents,
both killed in the war, stowaway on a sail-
ing-ship from France, and turned adrift on
reaching Australia. The story went well on
the wharf. It would go well on the
“movies.” But, being very young, he had not
calculated on such close cross-examination.
Then it all came out: he was just the un-
controllable kid of a respectable local father
at suburban Kent Town, who reported that
this was his third time of running away
since Christmas, and he had better be put
in a Home and whipped. The P.M. at the
Port cut out the whipping, but prescribed
the Home till the budding novelist is 18. And
the dozens of kind p’eople who had written
in with offers of practical sympathy for the
little “refugee” suddenly lost all interest.
W. 11. Lambert, the new president of the
P.L.L. of N. S. Wales, has been sec. of the
Central Branch of the A.W.U. for the past
three years. He succeeded “Ike” Smith,
who fell foul of the executive, and he has
made good. Lambert has spent most of his 35
years Outback, and knows the wants of the
country toiler better than most men. With
Arthur Blakely, late sec. of the Western
Branch of the A.W.U., who recently won
the Darling seat in the House of Rep's.,
he represents the "new thought” in
A.W.U. councils in N. S. Wales, and is
an ardent advocate of the “one big union”
idea, lie is not a talker, but does a heap of
thinking.
Joseph Cooper, who ceased to be the old-
est inhabitant of Sorell (Tas.) the other
day, was the last man in the Commonwealth
to wear the real old English smock-frock.
He reached the Speck 70 years ago, and for
a long time was the butcher for the Port
Arthur penal settlement, being stationed at
King George’s Sound (not the W.A. one)
to kill the cattle brought from Twofold Bay
(N.S.W.). He lived 93 years.
Fred Delmer, the exchanged prisoner from
Germany who has thrown some new light
on Berlin, married a Melbourne girl, and it
was from Melbourne that he, went to Ger-
many. Prof. Marshall Hall was probably
the cause of his going. That rampageous
man. with his eternal talk of Wagner and
Nietzsche and the mythical gods they adored,
almost convinced Delmer that nothing in
art, morals or political thought was of much
account unless it had been made in Germany
or Scandinavia. Ibsen was at that time the
one non-German prophet -whom Marshall,
Hall glorified, and he induced Delmer to take
the. Norwegian for the subject of a profes-
sorial address in Melbourne not long before
his departure for Gott’s own country. The
war was an eye-opener to Fred Delmer, Pro-
fessor of English at Berlin University, and
his education was complete by the time he
was released. To a London interviewer he
said : “My heart bounded at the first sight
of the British flag. To realise the meaning
of light one must live, in darkness where the
Union Jack dare not wave.”
By the last English mail, Frank Stapley.
of the Melbourne City Council, received a
photograph of his nephew, Lieut. R. T. l.
Moon, showing a plaintively-boyish profile.
Next morning Stapley opened the paper to
find that the lad had won the Victoria Cross
for a gallant piece of work in which four
successive wounds could not stop him guard-
ing a vital flank. The boy’s father is Arthur
Moon, a National Bank inspector, and the
lad—then 20—left “a bank at Maffra on
August 21, 1914, to close Fritz’s account.
Captain Jim Newlands, one of the two
Victorians in the latest batch of V.C.’s, is
a Camperdown boy. Joining the Garrison
Artillery at Queenscliff as a youngster, he
left it*as a non-com. to enter the postal ser-
vice, later becoming a police constable in
Tasmania, lie and a sergeant brother went
unscathed through the S’African war, gain-
ing trade experience which secured for Jim
the stripes of a staff sergeant-major in the
AM.F. and an “area” in Tasmania. When
the time came to resume business he joined
the 12th Battalion, got his first star in May,
1915. and pulled down the Cross by saving
his company though thrice attacked in left
and rear with bombs, shells and rifle-fire.
He is 33. and his wife was Miss Mitchell, a
Launceston girl.
The first two King’s Medals to come to ■
N. S. Wales firemen were pinned to the
tunics of Third-officer Sydney Watson and
Fourth-officer John Francis Ford last week.
Watson has hosed every big fire in Sydney
for 31 years, and Ford, who has been “men-
tioned” for conspicuous bravery twice, has
been jumping on engines for 30 years.
Private Jurgen Christian Jensen is olie of
the latest V.C.’s. Prompted by the Viking
strain which afterwards led him to spring
on to the parapet and wave his helmet to
stop a too-eager gang of Billjims from firing
on 45 Huns whom he had bluffed into sur-
rendering, he sought adventure oil the seas
as a youngster. He sailed from Denmark
for Melbourne, where he was naturalised
on September 17, 1914, drifted to South
Aus., and was working on Murray steamers
when it struck him that Fritz had never
apologised for his theft of Schleswig-Hol-
stein. .Therefore he tramped out of Adelaide
one day, leaving all his world behind him in
a seaman’s ditty box. It awaits his return
in an Adelaide pub. He is just 25.
Two of Adelaide’s wonderfully-active
veterans died on the same day last Week.
John Gillingham. 88, Methodist minister,
hurried home after a meeting and overtaxed
his heart. Benjamin Brookman, 90, was a
printer of old standing, but was best known
of late years as father of his son George,
one of Adelaide’s experts in finance.
WHAT STRUCK HER MOST.
THE BAD MAN: “Do you know, we must
have met before — doesn’t my face seem
familiar ?”
THE TIRED GIRL : “Oh, not your face so
much as your behavior.”
Arthur S. Payne, who died at Lambton
(N.S.W.) the other day, had run a butcher-
ing business in that town for 47 years, but
was better known as a “lay doctor.” A
natural gift for diagnosis, aided by some
knowledge of drugs, led him to make some
allegedly “marvellous” cures, and for many
years past hundreds of patients from all
over the Ma State had sought his advice.
One of his sons is Ernest Payne, who gave
up the pursuit of Costs in Newcastle a few
years ago to become Registrar of N.S.W.
Arbitration Court.
DUST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the ’ome away to Southward, more 'than ’alt a
life be’ind
(So it seems to me, 'oo ponders ’ere the lot of
’uman-kind),
When we done our “snowball-nun chin’ ” pick ’em
up an’ keep along—
On the. road that runs to Sydney tip from windy
Wollongong,
We was goin’ “fours in column,” an’ the dressin'
that we made
Was the dressin’ of the species that is known as
“marmalade.”
We was seven when we started, but the roads an’
’ostelries
Brought our tally to two ’undretl, droppin’ in by
twos an’ threes ;
An’ some was sick of shovin’ skips, an’ some ’ad
jacked the plough
(An’ some won't ever .march no more, but that don’t
matter now),
An’ watchin’ through my blinkin’ lids the yellow
road-dust rise,
An’ listenin’ to the language that was born of ’eat
an’ flies,
I made a little parable of earth an’ joy an’ pain,
And in language like the Bible it kept ’unimiu’
through my brain.
“Ritsin’ dust, sinkin ’ dust,
Driftin’ on, as all things must,
Sweepin’ ’ere an', settlin’ thither, shiftin’ onward for
a space,
Never ownin’ ’abitation, ’olding no abidin’-place,
Seekin’ always, findin’ never, somethin’ flttin’ you,
could do —
Yellow dust that’s bloivin’ by ns, there are lots of
blokes like you.”
Then we sweated at the Warren, or was trimmed at
Liverpool
(Oh, every man’s a ’ero now, though once ’c played
the fool ;
’K don’t need lessons once a week, an’ payments in
advance,
’Oo’s played a ’aider, tougher game in sunny, sloppy
France).
We’d dropped the slouch we brought from ’ome, we’d
tautened at the knees,
We're lookin’ all-round neater than we’d looked in
dungarees.
We’d smartened in salutin’ (we’d started somewhat
slack— <
Oh, a eane that ’ooks you when you pass’ll bring your
memory back!).
We’d learned the best forgettin’ is forgettin’ "ow to
grouse,
An’ that eyes an’ ’ands are ’andy things to ’ave
about the ’ouse,
That a pride in your battalion is the only proper
pride,
That side-arms are for drillin’ with—ail’ other tilings
beside.
We got the sharin' ’abit strong (we’d ’ad it rather
weak),
So that by-an’-bye you’d ’ear us gently murmur (.so
to speak) :
“Livin’ dust, ’uman dust,
Do your job. an’ earn your crust!
Oh, it’s blokes that’s built of stuff like you ’oo ’alf
the world ’ave swayed,
An’ you’ll never ’ave another chance to show ’ow
you arc made!
There is somethin’ more than nothin’ in the ’ardy an’
the true,
Cap in dook the world stands waitin’! There’s a
niche for blokes like you !”
I'd. stopped a splinter, later, with my foot, an’
dropped to rest,
When a traversin’ machine-gun grazed me deep across
tlie chest;
An’ starin’ at the ceilin’, snug in Blighty an’ my cot,
I pondered 'ard why some things is an’ other things
is not ;
Why blokes are never satisfied with what would make
me sick
(It’s mostly ’ooked from other blokes ’oo couldn’t
grab so quick),
No matter if it’s land or ’ouses, skirt or fags or
notes,
It sets one ’alf the ’ole mad world at ’alf the other’s
throats.
Oh, its record isn't ’olv, nor its reputation sweet,
That yellow dust, which ain’t the dust that blows
along the street;
They scratch it up from waterways, they burrow it
from ’oles,
To sell an’ purchase yachts an’ votes an’ precious ‘
stones an’ souls)
They tie it up in little rolls, an’ love an’ cherish
t it—
An’ God ’E knows the reason why, an’ ’E won't
ever split!
Lyin’ dust, fleetin’ dust,
Partner of the moth an’ rust,
Stayin’ men an’ spoilin’ women, though Pm statin’, in
advance,
I’m not spoiled—not so you’d notice —for I never ’ad
the chance!
Yet I’ve said, my fingers itch in’, an' the outlook
mostly blue:
‘‘Leave it for the owner, Cobber! Gobi ain’t good for
blokes like you!”
In the slough of last September we was ploughin’ to
an’ fro'
In a ’orror made of sleet above an' ’ip-(lee)) bog
below ;
The silly mules that knelt to lest, the wounded men
'oo stopped,
The black mud ’eld an’ dragged them in—they died
where they ’ad dropped.
Now the roads are dry an’ dusty in the coinin’ of
the Spring,
An' the birds would all be singin’ if they ’at] a place
to sing;
But the pal we ’ad last winter, an’ the sub. we used
to trust,
With the ’alt’ of our battalion, they are dust beneath
the dust.
An’ the blokes 'oo fought at Agineourt, an’ also
Waterloo,
An’ the older scraps the padre names, are dust an’
ashes, too.
So l watch beneath my eyelids, while the laden wind,
lifts ’igh
The dust of blokes I’m kickin’ up, ’oo’ll know me by-
an’-bye.
Liftin’ dust, driftin’ dust,
Flesh of men ’oo ’ached an’ thrust,
Shoved an’ struck with pike or bay'net—did your
little dash an’ died!
Teach ns all your dyin' taught you; teach us that an’
more beside!
Hy the good we never follo.w, by the, things we fail
to do,
Fou "oo trod the road before us, make us, somc’ou),
blokes Tike you !
VICTORIA.
J. ALEX. ALLAN.

A stern chase is a long chase. This society
fell behind the procession; and now it has
hard work to catch up. The trouble is that
for many years it got very little new busi-
ness, which means that an exceptionally
large proportion of its policies are old and
therefore more likely to become claims than
would be the case had it been a progressive
office. Claims (including bonus additions
and bonuses paid in cash) were last year
equal to nearly 57 p.c. of the total income.
In the National Mutual, which is four years
older than the Colonial Mutual, the propor-
tion for the same year was only 46 p.c.
Now, claims mean more than that something
has to be paid out: they mean also that some-
thing ceases to come in. Hence, though the
society is really getting and nowadays keep-
ing a very fair number of new policies, the
total income shows for the present a poor
growth.
A better feature is the improvement in the
expense rate: —■
Expenses Compared with Total Income.
1905 .. £2l 17 10 p.c. 1911 ..£26 16 8 p.c.
1906 .. 22 8 8 „ 1912 .. 29 14 5 „
1907 .. 22 3 2 „ 1913 .. 25 5 8 „
1908 . . 20 2 10 „ 1914 . . 19 19 3 „
1909 .. 18 0 7 „ 1915 .. 14 19 7 „
1910 .. 20 16 4 „ 1916 .. 15 7 0 „
This reduction has been secured notwith-
standing that a reasonable canvass for new
business has been maintained. Last year
new-business premiums totalled £63,336. If
we assume that the renewal business cost
7 p.c. and the single-premium (annuity)
business cost 3 p.c., then this new business
was gOt at a cost of 113 p.c. of the first
year’s premiums; and though that isn’t a
cheap rate, it shows a vast improvement:—
New Premiums. Cost.
1913 .. .. £76,118 172 p.c.
1914 .. .. 64,082 153 „
1915 .. .. 51,766 125 „
1916 .. .. 63,336 113 „
In actual practice, perlians, there has not
been so great a reduction. But if it hasn’t been
in the cost of new business, then it has been
in the cost of old business. The reduction
has certainly taken place somewhere; and
bonuses must be all the better for it. There
is nowadays an annual distribution of
profit, and for the past year £79,448, equal
to about 17.2 p.c. of the premiums, is cut up.
This improvement on the figure of a few
years ago is not entirely due to the reduced
expense rate. The interest earnings are
much better than they used to be: —
1907 .. £3 16 9 p.c. 1912 .. £4 5 11 p.c.
1908 . . 4 1 1 „ 1913 . . 4 8 10,,
1909 .. 4 3 3 „ 3914 .. 4 9 10 „
1910 . . 4 3 10 „ 1915 . . 4 9 11 „
1911 .. 4 5 9 „ I 1916 . . i 10 5 „
The gross earnings for the past year were
about ss. p.c. more; but in 1915 the practice
was adopted of charging income tax against
the interest earnings, and in view of the
special character of the Federal income tax
this seems a fairer plan than to charge it
up with working exes. Part of the improved
rate is of course due to the writing down of
assets: about £175,000 of profit has been
thus appropriated in the last 10 years. This
has meant reduced bonuses while the writ-
ing down has been in progress, but prospects
are thus made brighter for those who come
in now. While the writing down has pro-
ceeded there has also been a good deal of
liquidation of old assets, and the present
distribution of funds is far better than that
'of 10 years ago:—

The public securities include £454,936 of
war loans; so the Colonial Mutual has
fairly done its duty in that respect, especi-
ally in view of its comparatively small sur-
plus of revenue in excess of claims and
exes. Actually it has put into these patriotic
loans an amount equal to the entire surplus
of revenue over outgoings since the war
started; and as these loans are free of
income tax, the society is going to escape
something presently when the war-bill has to
be paid.
When an almost entirely new manage-
ment proceeded to shake this society out of
its long sleep, it was seen that, in order to
attract the right sort of business-getters,
they must be given a chance of making com-
missions on some side-lines. So an Accident
branch was started a few years ago ; and the
next table shows what has been done: —
Accident Department.

In 1910 the society took over the Stand-
ard and claimed that the business was
worth £22,000. If you strike out that dubi-
ous item you can also strike off £22,000 from
the exes, in the following year and charged
to extension of business account. For some
years the business was horribly expensive;
and there is still a good deal of leeway to
make up; but it is bemsr made up. The
actuarial net liability on December 31 was
£196.841; so the office has still the job of
making good the difference between that and
the £110,977 shown above. And that doesn’t
mean merely putting £85.864 by out of the
surplus of revenue over outgoings; for each
year, for some time to come, the actuarial
liability will grow.

Another case where the profit is a bit more
than it was before the war; and it really 'is
time that we began to consider what this
means. The prosperity ivliich such figures
signify is either real or bogus. If it is real
ir, comes from only one thing: the supply of
foods, etc., to Britain and the Allies at
extortionate prices. If it is bogus, then we
will have to pay for it all presently. There
is certainly some genuine profit-making at
the expense of Britain and the Allies: for
our metals and our wool, for instance, we
are getting almost staggering prices. What
are we going to do about that? We cannot
refuse to take the money. We are getting
no more than others are making the Allies
pay. But that blood money should not
stay in private pockets; and in making no
serious attempt to recover the war profits
—especially the profits that have been made
out of harassed Britain and the Allies —the
Government has been guilty of something
little short of a crime. It has certainly lent
color to the story that one or two members
of the Government allow themselves to be far
too much influenced by a group of indecently
greedy capitalists. As far as this paper can
see Australia will be driven to make another
attempt to carry Conscription. But the
task of those who will have to fight for it
will be made almost appallingly difficult if
no determined steps are taken to recover war-
time profits and use them for the benefit of
the nation —the only use to which they can
honestly be put. This little bank isn’t, of
course, a bad offender: far from .it. One
wouldn’t expect it to be. That its profit
should show any increase at all, indeed, indi-
cates how widespread are the effects of the
present grossly unhealthy conditions.
There was a time when this paper shiv-
ered a little at the Tasmanian National’s
poor holding of liquid assets. The position
is much more secure nowadays. Watch how
the liquid assets have bustled along:—
1907. 1916. 1917.
Free deposits .. £310,375 £425,627 £399,072
Fixed deposits .. 245,929 317,295 332,862
Other liabilities . . 79,844 24,688 25,374
Liquid assets . . 174,252 313,678 315,209
Advances, etc. •• 630,537 714,484 707,523
Premisesandsundries 28,177 25,866 24,986
In 10 years the bank has got in £175,000
of deposits, half of them fixed ; and £141.000
of the total amount is still held liquid. This
means that most of the money which has
been put into new advances has been either
new share capital or accumulated profits.
The capital is now £195,250; 10 years ago
it was £152.040. The , little National is
more of a local bank on the American pat-
tern than any other in the Commonwealth :
its head office is in Launceston and it hasn’t
even got a branch at Hobart. Its district
is not wildly progressive, so it isn’t going to
run ahead fast. But it keeps steadily plug-
ging away; and if a 7 p.c. dividend isn’t
much to pay on a capital of £195,250 when
it is backed by nearly £90,000 of reserves,
the bank can at least justly claim to be
helping its own district as well as making
a comfortable thing for itself.

Through drought and keen competition the co.
was gasping in 1914-15; but moderate rains
made it possible to harvest the salt, while
the German submarine gave the co. a mar-
ket which Australian politicians have appar-
ently not cared whether it had or not. In
the past year the rains have been abnormal;
and that on top of the short and dear labor
supply has curtailed production. It looks
as though that may mean dear salt pre-
’scntl.v. Actually in the 12 months to .Tune
30, 1916, the flaccid, loafing State of N. S.
Wales alone imported nearly 6000 tons of
ordinary salt, in addition to over £4OOO
worth of packet salt, and Heaven knows
how much rock salt. It shouldn’t have been
necessary to fill a cubic foot of shipping
room with this product; it is a disgrace to
Australian politicians that it was necessary.
And it doesn’t say very much for Australian
enterprise either; for attempts to interest
moneyed people in schemes for recovering
salt have in pre-war years been received with
indifference. In some glad day in the future
perhaps the man with a scheme for increas-
ing this country’s output of manufactured
goods may be welcomed as a benefactor.
Hitherto he has known quite well that he
would be regarded as either a crank or a
schemer whom we have to be on our guard
against lest he get away with some of our
money.
Even with the Protection we have had it
has been possible for this co. to make some
progress. Its dividends for 10 years have
averaged just on 71 p.c., and there has been
enough profit left over to show this change:

It is only here and there that the man who
throws in to a new co-operative concern has
much money. The big man, as a rule, hasn t
much to complain about: he gets his work
done by Private Enterprise at cut prices.
In Australia, at any rate, the small man is
mostly kept small by exorbitant charges;
and even when he sets to work to escape
them —which isn’t often —he is generally
handicapped by lack of funds. Still, if he
isn’t greedy, it is astonishing how rapidly
his reserves pile up. In this case all the
capital that could be raised at the start was
£25,000. On that the co. was able to
finance the purchase of premises at Pyr-
mont. To-day, though it has been operating
only six years, and though every year it has
given a bonus to consignors, it has (after
allowing for depreciation) a third more
assets than it started with. Of course this
isn’t enough to allow it to'go into the busi-
ness of pastoral finance; and it shouldn't
attempt to, for, though very profitable for. a
co. with plenty of fixed capital, it is peril-
ous for a weak co. Certainly advances
against consignments are unavoidable; but
that’s a different matter. On March 31 the
Commonwealth (as agents for the British
Government) owed the co. £73,217 for wool,
and other people had got advances and
credits totalling £24,471. To finance that
lot the co. was leaning on its own creditors
to the extent of £96,164. But those figures
are quite exceptional. For the ordinary
purposes of the co. its own funds are now
just about sufficient: “freeholds and lease-
holds, buildings, plant and other assets”
(including £l2OO of War Loan) stand at
£42,237, while the capital is £24,142. re-
serves before ' paying the current dividend
and bonus £15.118, and depreciation fund
(not reckoned with the reserves in the table)
£3500; total £42,760. The bonus to con-
signors is 20 p.c. off the selling commission,
reducing it to 2 p.c.; 10 p.c. off the commis-
sion on sales of fat stock, reducing it to 2i
p.c. ; and in the aggregate £619 off produce-
sale commissions.

This co. has 43 years’ experience behind it;
and in that time it ought to have charted all
the snags in the stream. Twelve months
ago the directors stated their policy:—
Your Board has continued to deal conservatively
with applications for advances, preferring’ to see a
reduction in the amount advanced during the year
rather than push business with the possibility of
incurring losses owing to a quiet market as a result
of prevailing conditions consequent on the war.
During the past year they certainly were
enticed by the high rates offering to lend a
bit more than in the preceding term; still
repayments were well ahead of advances—
£ll3,744 as against £85,482. It is still. n<>
doubt, quite possible to lend money safelj
Everything depends upon the margin re-
quired, and as the present is a time when a
large margin can be asked without making
the prospective borrower jump, the chances
are that the current business is quite safe.
The worst snag in the stream paddled in
by a co. in this business is the unexpected
burst of a boom. In this case the lavish
spending of war money has given the lenders
on the old inflated values a fine chance to
get snug. The other bad snag is the sudden
withdrawal of deposits. But this co. isn’t
likely to be hurt that way, because
in the first place it lias nearly
£60,000 liquid and in temporary in-
vestments ; its advances come in, as a
rule, at the rate of well over £lOO,OOO a
year; and its total liabilities are under
£224,000. There are duller times ahead of
it for certain ; but it looks the sort of solid
old thing that will do pretty well in bad
times and be ready to do better when the
times are again not so bad.

Siiortland Islands Plantation Co.- —
This co., in the Burns-Philp stable, has had
little except trouble during the year. In
the first week a gale—the worst, of course,
for 40 years—levelled 2000 cocoanut trees
and tilted 5000 others; and though most of
them were saved, it meant a set-back to the
plantations. The labor force once got down
as low as 36; since then it has been industri-
ously built up to 130, and, “though this
number is ample for weeding and upkeep
work, the extra task of straightening up the
plantations after the gale will render it im-
possible to undertake any new work for some
months.” As to labor generally :

the Commonwealth Government by the planters of

the British Solomons, the Lieut.-Gov. of Papua lias
recently visited the Solomons to inquire into the
shortage of labor and upon the proposal to permit
British planters to. engage labor from the adjacent
German Possessions. His Ex.’s report is awaited
with great anxiety, as upon it absolutely depends
whether there can be any additional development
or whether the Estates are to be closed oft at then
present areas and all further progress stopped.
There are 1170 acres under cocoanuts and
30 acres under rubber —the latter a plot
“too small to be profitably tapped at the pre-
sent price of rubber.” The trees are from one
to seven years old and some of them came
into bearing last year. The copra yield was,
however, only about 41 tons; this year it will
be about 80 tons. So far the co. has called
up £22.820 of its total capital of £50,000, and
£20,192 has been spent on the estates, equal
to under £lB per acre, including buildings,
and reckoning the 18,000 acres of undeveloped
land as worth nothing. Two calls of £5 each
were made last year; the same will probably
be made this year.
The Germans, when they had possession,
allowed the employment of natives from Ger-
man New Guinea in their part of the Solo-
mons. The time may come when the Solomon
Islander will be a good agriculturist and
anxious to take the job; so far he isn’t. The
native of New Guinea, on the other hand,
is an excellent worker —when he works; and
he mostly is prepared to do it. Both in
Papua and the old German part the present
labor supply exceeds the demand. Papua
has, however, more than enough rich country
to employ the whole of the native popula-
tion ; and it is most improbable that permis-
sion will be given to employ any of them
outside their own island. The doubt that
appears to be in the minds of those in autho-
rity is whether a policy should be pursued
with regard to the rest of the island which
has been set aside as quite out of the ques-
tion with regard to the old British portion.
If that position is maintained the progress
of this and other cos. in the British Solomons
is going to be very slow.

This midget was formed to assist Vic. public
servants with small loans. But semi-philan-
thropists to provide the money are absurdly
scarce ; and actually the unfeeling wretches
are drawing out more than they are invest-
ing. So the share capital is only £1892
now as against £2142 in February, 1914.
