still has, its uses. There are hard-luck cases
in every big service; and it is often an enor-
mous value to these down-and-nearly-outs to
get a small loan on Christian terms. The
mistake this co. is making is in trying to
get and hold the money at p.c. and offer-
ing its own loans accordingly.
* * • •
It looks as though the Broken Hill cos.
want some plain talking to. For months
past they have been pouring out the tale that
their output was being restricted owing to
the shortage of men, and they have been
scouring Australia to get more hands. An
emissary arrived in Sydney a couple of
months ago primed with figures and begged
the Government to assist in dispatching men
to the field. The unions protested vigorously,
declaring that the cos. couldn’t find a market
for more stuff than they were producing and
that the Associated Smelters at Pt. Pirie
were already chock-a-block with concentrates
and metal. They contended that the men
were simply being taken to Broken Hill to
deliberately overload the local market. That
now looks to have been true; and if it is it
was a shameful thing for the cos. to do. At
any rate, the new men had barely arrived
at the Hill before the Associated Smelters
intimated that the cos. must send them still
less concentrates; owing to the lack of bot-
toms to remove the metals the deliveries
must be reduced by 10 p.c. And the chances
are that they will be asked to reduce* con-
signments still further. The cos. must have
known what was coming, because Associated
Smelters, Ltd., is a co-operative concern, in
which these mining cos. are. the shareholders.
They have also all of them representatives
on the Board. An unpleasant feature of the
whole business is that for some weeks past,
even while these labor reinforcements were
being sent forward, there has been a good
deal of steady selling of certain Broken Hill
stocks. Evidently somebody knew what was
coming. The N.S.W. Government can’t take
any notice of that, perhaps. But it is en-
titled to ascertain wiiether the cos. which
recently sought its assistance to get men for
Broken Hill were acquainted with the posi-
tion and prospects at Pt. Pirie. If they
were (and it was their duty to know), then
the Government should do some very plain
talking to them. No self-respecting
ment can permit itself to be used as a t
for such a purpose.
The B. H. Prop, says plainly that, while
other Broken Hill cos. may be able to pro-
duce concentrates in excess of what the
Associated Smelters can take, putting then
surplus money into these stocks, it is in
too much need of capital for the steel woiks
to do anything of that kind. The next big
outlay at the Newcastle works is for steel-
rolling plant.
* * * *
“Quidam”: “Good Australian” jprophesies
(B. 7/6/T7) a big demand for minerals dur-
ing the next few years. I wonder if this
country will wake up in time to catch the
’bus. At present the local demand for many
of the ores is almost nil. Manganese, for
instance, is practically _ unsaleable. Dia-
tomaceous earth, magnesite, etc., are in the
same boat. The ordinary man imagines,
looking at the cabled prices, that the
industrial metal-miner is at present
reaping a rich harvest. Well, in many
cases he isn’t. Take ores containing zinc,
for instance, and try to sell them. Austra-
lian works won’t buy them at all, so that,
though Hughes “solved the metal problem,”
the miner’s position is worse than formerly.
Lead is nominally at about £3O; but charges
reduce the price to about £2O. For prac-
tical purposes it doesn’t matter to the miner
whether lead is quoted at £2O when he gets
almost all of it or at £3O when he gets only
two thirds of it. Or take a complex ore
containing (say) zinc, lead, copper and sil-
ver. The smelting works that will buy the
copper and silver won’t pay for zinc or lead ;
in fact, won’t take the ore at all if over a
certain percentage of zinc is present. The
works that will pay for lead and silver
won’t pay for copper and zinc. Zinc, in
fact, is the outcast, pariah and abomination
of the local smelter (to whom alone the
miner is permitted to sell), and the for-
warder of it is penalised for its presence at
so much a unit; so that the person who goes
nearly broke buying a sheet of manufac-
tured zinc at present prices is apt to totally
miscalculate its “value” to the miner. The
habit of computing what a complex ore is
worth by taking the percentage of metals
in it and reckoning the rest by reference to
the metal quotations is apt to lead inexperh
enced people into the mud. When we can
extract all our metals and sell them after
extraction the problem will be less perplex-
ing. At present an ore is worth what one
can get for it and that, under present
arrangements, is in many cases little. As for
magnesite, infusorial earth, etc., a matchbox-
full per month appears to satisfy local re-
quirements. It is a great country, but it
wants a lot more people, a lot more work,
and a lot more commonsense.
* * * *
The old Turon goldfield (N.S.W.) is on
the last lap. Nothing is now visible on the
landscape but a procession of abandoned
shafts and heaped-up stone—the relics of
the roaring days of the ’7o’s. Even dredg-
ing has ceased, the two machines that for
years nosed up and down the picturesque
stream having been dismantled and carted
off to New Guinea. The latest event is the
removal of the plant from the old Queens-
lander mine. This show, in private hands,
has carried on for years on a big body of
low-grade stuff; but the few dwt. to the ton
have not represented enough cash. Work on
the field is now confined to fossicking, and
seme of the old dish-and-cradle hands occa-
sionally come into Sofala and flash bottles
of nice-looking alluvial gold. The township
is fast decaying, the population having come
down from 30,000 in the old times to under
300. The Hill End field, which lies 20 miles
north, is helping to keep Sofala in existence,
motor services running over the Turon daily.
Boring at the north end is urged as a
stimulant to Kalgoorlie. Tens of thousands
of pounds have been won from the reefs and
lodes in that part of the field, yet no seri-
ous attempt has been made to test them
below a few hundred feet. Dr. Maclaren
and other geologists are quoted in support
of the theory that the ore channels from
which the once great Golden Mile mines de-
rived all their wealth extend northward, and
in the Hidden Secret telluride of gold was
for a time found in fairly large quantities.
Certainly the deep ground is worth a scien-
tific test; but, judging by the work that has
been done, the prospects are not brilliant.
* * * *
Developments in the Ivanhoe (W.A.)
have reached an interesting stage. Years
ago when its main source of ore supply—
the East lode —was broken up and impover-
ished by an intrusive porphyry bar, shaft
sinking was pushed ahead and has been in
progress ever since. Dr. Maclaren recom-
mended this work after a thorough geolo-
gical overhaul of the property, and he
reckoned that the lode would free itself from
the porphyry and be its old self again at a
vertical depth of about 3400 ft. Recent
developments at 3620 ft. tend to bear out
the expert’s opinion. A crosscut is now
being extended to the lode at 3620 ft., and
should the co. find values and a width of
ore similar to those obtained before the
porphyry intrusion occurred —and there is
no geological reason why it shouldn’t—the
mine will avoid the catastrophe which has
threatened it for some years past.
The drop in Edna May’s monthly dividend
is not relished in the West; but in the cir-
cumstances the reduction seems imperative.
Equipment and development have run away
with a lot of money, and the water difficulty
has put working costs up to a figure much
higher than would otherwise have been the
case. The co. has little cash in hand and
is burdened with a substantial liability on
account of State and Federal taxation. On
the other hand, it has a fine block of ore
available between the 300 ft. and 385 ft.
levels, from which something like 30.000
tons are expected. Then there is the ore
above 300 ft. and between 385 ft. and 480 ft.
to be taken into account. The show has
been a good one, and has paid more in divi-
dends than all the other mines on the Yil-
garn field put together. Its life certainly
appeal’s to be limited, but there is still a
lot of gold to be taken out. Whether the
shares are worth their present price is
another matter.
* * * *
Oswald’s, the crack mine at Maldon
(Vic.), can yet make divs., though it has
put out a million in gold. The family no
longer lives at Maldon, and the mine is run
by a co., but the Oswalds still collect a big
swag of the profits. The stone goes about
15dwt., and most of it is broken from 1000 ft.
As the shoot can not be picked up underfoot
a winze is to go down to see whither it has
slid. This is always the bother at Maldon,
But in Oswald’s the family had the luck to
get fresh shoots before the undertaker had
to be called in. The reef at 1450 ft. needs
only a bit more color to make it very valu-
able, for there is a splendid formation 15ft.
wide. But the best prospect for a long time
has been 3dwt. Even of that, though, manager
Couehman has hopes; they’ve got costs down
pretty fine at Maldon.
* # # *
Dyason is going to sweep away old land-
marks at Bendigo, for under the amalgamation
he will not give the yields from mines indi-
vidually, though he may let it be known
where the best stone is being broken. His
first aggregate yield isn’t good, and doesn’t
look like a 17 p.c. div. Still it is early days
yet. and Dyason. has to get his crew pro-
perly stroked before exes, can be got down
and yields lifted. His development pro-
gramme also has to be unfolded. On present
appearances he may have trouble with tri-
butors, as these are out for a new agreement.
And they want such a lot, too, that even
Mines Minister Tom Livingston had to tell
them to go away. At home economies have
started: thus Dyason expects to save 21
hours a week by having only fortnightly
clean-ups—quite enough.
* * * *
Thus the Mt. Lyell Board: “The Direc-
tors have decided, after careful deliberation,
not to issue the usual report and accounts
for the half-year ended March 31, 1917. They
consider it would be advantageous to issue
accounts only once a year in future.” As
a matter of fact, until the Government does
what it should have done long ago and
announces the war-tax, no accounts are
much good. An interim div. of Is. 3d., taking
£80,574, for the half-year ending September
next, is declared payable on July 30. This is
at the same rate as the last cut-up. But note
that it is not for the half-year to the end of
March, though it is paid well before the end of
the following half. Possible taxation, uncertain
times, and the need to strengthen the finances
are the explanation offered for this little
move. In the March six months the co.
mined 145,464 tons, against 166,497 tons
for the September term. To win 3002 tons
copper, 149.8290 z. silver and 39520 z. gold
for the half-year was a feat, as that yield
of copper is only 137 tons less than for the
Sentember half-year, when 20,157 tons more
were smelted. The rich shoot on the Lyell
Blocks boundary helped to this result
solidly. Bowes Kelly says the continuous 8000
h.p. to be got from extending the Mt. Mar-
garet power scheme is to serve the first unit
of the co.’s electrolytic spelter plant. This
will eat up £70,000; so what will the new
30.000-h.p. King River sche e require? The
co. certainly does need money.
* * * •
“Thrift Week” in Melbourne was marked
by a demonstration of the use of Morwell
brown coal in the production of suction gas.
It is claimed that with a suitable type of
producer brown coal yields as good results
as charcoal and box firewood at double the
cost. On the basis of the present price
of charcoal and firewood the value of brown
coal is 28s. 9d. a ton, while it can be bought
on railway sidings for 10s. The Mines De-
partment made the experiments at its test-
ing plant, and if manufacturers can get
similar results Victoria is going to effect a
large annual economy in the cost of manu-
facture wherever power is used.
Golden. Ridge ( W.A.), finding nothing
suitable in the way of a new property, is
repaying another 2s. per share at the end of
this month, making ss. so dealt with alto-
gether. The Court sanctioned the return of
that much capital. But it is all subject to
recall.

Sydney, June 18, 1917.
My Dear Auini, —
In order to put the extinguisher on a sug-
gestion that the State should do the decent
thing now and hand Government House in
Macquarie-street back to the Common-
wealth, it has been suddenly decided to sell
Cranbrobk —the roomy mansion bought to
house State Governors years ago. Some of
the land will go with the old home. The rest
is to be cut iqi into small suburban lots.
Originally the home of a wealthy sporting
man, James White, it commands a view
which the late Lady Rawson called “the
loveliest in the world”—and she had seen
many views in her travels in the wake of
her Admiral.
Nothing doing except socks, and a few
concerts, with never a decent excuse for the
smart girl to wear her best .drapery. How-
ever, there’s the black tricorne hat still with
us. This Napoleonic millinery is now
adorned with gold cockades. You can almost
hear the band play when it comes into view.
The Conservatorium was crowded to its
keyhole on Saturday afternoon for the first
orchestral concert of the season, under
Yerbrugghen’s waving hands. Chairs, side-
ways on, had been squashed against the wall
for devotees who came early and supported
the long wait with socks and light literature.
The highbrows with reserved seats bounded
in at the last moment. The “house” man-
agement is simply dreadful. After the fid-
dlers had sawed softly in a preliminary tune-
up. and Verbrugghen at high tension had
looked round to diagnose the squeaking shoe-
leather, the Conservatorium orchestra let it
go—“ The National Anthem” raised to a
new dignity by the master-craftsman’s
orchestration. After that came the Cullens.
They were late and sidled into their seats
on velvet foot, the little man in the con-
ductor’s pen impatiently waiting till the
Deputy-Gov. had deposited his hat in safe
keeping. Beethoven, in the overture to his
only opera, “Fidelio,” a lively tinkle with
melancholy interludes like the wailing of
lost kittens, and in two massive symphonies,
kept the early-pilgrim mothers and sisters
in ecstatic silence till the finish of each item.
The Philistines who applauded when the
triumphant fiddles paused between a move-
ment got a frigid stiffening of Yerbrugghen’s
back for their pains.
The Stewart Richardsons’ daughter Mar-
garet took a plain gold ring from Neville
Laurence, of Strathfield, in St. Philip’s
Church the other day. He’s a son of solici-
tor Charles Laurence and a brother of the
other lawyer who was the .model for The
Bulletin’s Little Boy from Manly 30 years
ago. Parson Johnstone came from old St.
John’s, Parramatta, to tie the knot. Nor-
man Oakes, who belongs to a historic family
in the old town, was one of the groomsmen ;
and Arthur Gardiner also helped the best
man to remember the pocket he’d put the
ring in.
There is to be a civic lunch to wash down
the presentation to Glossop, C.B. J. C,
Watson wants the souvenir to be “something
tangible” upon which the man who dished
the Emden “and bis family can look with
pride.” Alas, the bashful hero is a bachelor!
Mrs. Brittain, the handsome wife of the
'Murkan Consul, comes from the South —
romantic background for the U.S. novelist
who wants to get away from Chewcawgo mil-
lionaires. President of the American-Aus-
tralian League of Help, the lady is putting
vim into their work for-France’s Day. A
stall in Moore-street will be under the Star-
spangled banner as a result.
Budden-Morris, the young Sydney pianist
whose recent performance at the Steinway
Hall was given under the patronage of
Queen Mother Alexandra, was a former pupil
of'Yvonne Charvin. He writes to her “with
eternal gratitude” of his brighter prospects in
London. Princess Henry has stirred up
musical bigwigs to lend their aid qnd give
the Australian pianist a push; and the
Queen Mother bought tickets for her friends
for the last concert. Eight shillings apiece,
by the way, for the best seats.
Dep.-Governor Cullen revoked his inten-
tion of living at Government House during
his term as locum tenens. So they stay on
at their flower-decked home in the hinterland
of Mosman, and, incidentally, save money.
No “official” meals will worry the mistress
of the house in that sylvan retreat.
Captain Henderson, R.N., has. after a
little delay, got away to duty on the other
side. But no passport for his indignant
sister could be screwed out of the adamant
authorities, and the lady has a right to
wonder why the Premiers’ womenkind got
through. Meanwhile Miss Henderson has
been eating the breakfast chop with the
Cullens.
A.D.C. Clifford, when lie tires of watching
the lambkins skip in the Riverina, goes on to
the G.-G.’s staff. He picks up the threads
dropped by Firth, who has returned to the
war.
General Finn's only son has recently been
promoted to a captaincy after two years’ ser-
vice. He’s in British heavy artillery, where,
even in war-time, gunners have a hard climb
for a major’s crown.
Lady Denman’s hobby—white leghorns—
has led her into other thrift excursions. A
recent article in a Loudon paper, “Economy
in the Backyard," was from her pen.
Mrs. Val Jam-
eson’s “P e t e r ’ s
Nationality” has got
into a second edition ;
and here’s a picture
of the lady, sent you.
with the hope that
her effort to rouse
the public to take
more interest in
“State” and neg-
lected children will
have great success.
First of all, she
wants Hereford
House made more
attractive. Hereford
House is a sort of
exchange, where the
“S tat e” children
awa it boarding-out.
There’s a stream passing to and fro; and
sometimes an unattractive child has to wait
many days—weeks often—before anyone will
undertake to board and mother it. In that
time the poor, unwanted little Creature has
nothing better to play in than a small, grimy
asphalt yard. “To make healthy, happy
children is national ecomony,” says Mrs.
Jameson.
France’s Day is getting into form. In a
crib six storeys above the pavement Neville
Mayman presses the buttons that will ring
out a golden tune on July 13. On the wall
is an inspiring picture of a French soldier
advancing to victory with the words, “We
Shall Get Them!” This will be sold at ss.
in a three-color print. The brooch —a charm-
ing fancy—goes for a mere 10s., and lesser
trophies can be collected for a shilling
upwards. The Australian kiddy has done
mountains of sewing for baby Francois. In
return, any youngster may buy for sixpence
a tricolor button that daddy couldn’t get
for less than twice as much.
The stone courtyard behind the Fire Bri-
gade’s Headquarters was alive with samples
of firemen’s agility the other afternoon.
They ran a ladder up to Heaven —stopped
just, this side of it—and a “saver,” having
made a goat-like ascent, looked calmly at the
universe. They shot another cobweb to tlie
top storey of an adjacent building, and
showed the goggle-eyed company how to toss
the inmates of a burning house Over a
shoulder and bring them to earth as easily
as if they were bath-towels. In an interval
Acting-Premier Fuller banded two senior
brigade officers, Sydney Watson and J. F.
Ford, the King’s Medal “for exceptional
heroism and devotion to duty.” Watson asked
his missus to pin his medal on bis coat,
and the good lady did it all smiles.
The Myrtle Meggy way at the pianoforte,
notably when she pursues the sombre
Beethoven chords, brought her a barrowload
of blooms at the Conservatorium last week.
She’s the Sydney girl who had a long spell of
training on the other side, and now instructs
the. young pianist in Macquarie-street.
Amongst Australians to lately come home
is Surgeon-colonel Sturdee, A.M.C. He is the
only brother of the Admiral of Falkland
Islands fame.
Kevin Goldrick, who first made acquaint-
ance with Fritz when he visited the Fanning
Island cable station with a machine-gun and
some blowing-up apparatus, is likely to know
him a lot more. A cable says the Australian
boy is wounded and a prisoner of war in
Westphalia, where the ham comes from.
Kevin passed through the officers’ school in
Sydney, but went away carrying a corporal’s
stripes, stars being short at the time. Mother
recently pulled up her roots in Sydney to
take her young daughter’s voice to the
Melba Conservatorium over the border.
In London a few weeks ago umbrellas
were unfurled for • the wedding of Lorna
Chadwick Osborn —a grand-daughter of the
late Robert Chadwick, of Sydney—to Squad-
ron-commander Edmonds, R.N., D. 5.0., of
Hants.
Remember Hilda Harnett, who went to
Egypt to type for the Red Cross? She
married Lieut. Abbott and recently helped
to nurse her husband in an English .hospital.
He is a whole man again and able to jump
into a Light Horse saddle.

The last mail brought us more news about
the loss of seven valuable workers for the
Australian Comforts Funds (Unlimited).
They had coffee-stalls in the Bapaume Town
Hall, and were at their stands when the
place was blown to smithereens.

£2OO to equip a massage department. Soldiers
in particular will benefit by the outlay. So
the Sydney Vagabonds are up and doing.
On July 2 they’ll produce “Mrs. Gorringe’s
Necklace” in St. James’s Hall.
The long claw of coincidence timed a
recent recital by Cyril Monk and Frank
Hutchens just as news came through that
Maurice Ravel had been wounded in France.
The point of the claw rested on the fact
that a trio by this French composer (Glad-
stone Bell at the ’cello) was the pivot of
the programme.
In “Canary Cottage” Dot Brunton wears
lots of adorable clothes as the frisky Trixie
who runs the mustard-plaster camp. She
changes her garments so often that even the
glorified ballet gown in which she attends her
stage wedding with William Greene —tall,
dark-haired young man, much attached to
a blazer and cricketing flannels —looks too
ethereal to last 10 minutes. Maud Fane,
under a flounced cloak, shows us what an
impressive thing gran’ma had in this enrlv-
Vietorian drape. The chorus froeking is
not more daring than that which drips round
a Manly beach on a sumYncr afternoon. It's
just surf.v in style, though Canary Cot is
set amidst pink mountains that might be
Neapolitan ice-cream.

When Professor
David got too rest-
less to sit down
before students’
exam, papers any
longer, he made up
his mind to get to the
F ront “somehow.”
That’s how he felt,
too, about an Ant-
arctic expedition a
few years ago.
“Don’t be a goose,”
said his wife on that
occasion. Bu t he
went all the same.
This time the Missus
didn’t try to block
the way : she saw her
only son and game
old husband off to
the war without a protest. Westralia
was sending a mining corps, and David
thought N. S. Wales might have a go at
Goliath in the same way. So, chiefly through
his initiative, a local mining-camp got into
training. Then they sailed away. For
a year very little was heard of the soldier-
geologist, except as to his thrilling espapes
in various tunnelling exploits somewhere in
France. Now comes the news that Austra-
lian sappers and miners, after 12 months’
toil with British experts underground, saw
Messines Bidge go up in flames and fury.
Here is Judith Fletcher’s photo, of the
Sydney leader of the moles.
Douglas Mawson, you remember, was one
of the old Professor’s curly-headed boys. His
mother died last week in Sydney. There’s
another son hereabouts, a doctor at Campbell-
town.
The long scarf that comes nearly to the
hem of the skirt wipes up the teacups
at four o’clock. Two damsels in black, with
yards of floating green drapery, had a busy
time keeping the verdant silk off their cake
crumbs the other afternoon. Still the
nymph-like effect is worth while— if the
wearers have willow-wand waists.
For Australia Day women tore off seal coats,
lings and brooches, and gave away every-
thing except their best hats (the only femi-
nine who can spare her‘Sunday millinery
is getting fitted for a halo), to he sold under
the hammer. They are now examining
household gods in view of France’s Day auc-
tion. Hubby may come home to sit on the
ice-chest, or carve the evening meal from the
summit of a packing-case, the missus is
getting so “worked up.” "Thousands of chil-
dren in the north of France,” said. Miss
Gurney the other day, “are livid and emaci-
ated from chronic semi-starvation during the
past awful year.” So Mrs. Subbub, at a
recent gathering iu the Town Hall < the
McMillan dame in the chair), took a secret
oath to sell anything she can lay her hands
on to swell the funds on July 13.

verse? He has been leading Dobbin on a

The War Chest Depot is getting
100.000 pairs of socks and more. .Mean-
while. they come in avalanches—2o,ooo
arrived within 24 hours. Climb a
narrow stair to the big room in Eliza-
heth-street and you get among moun-
tain-peaks of purl and plain. The casual
caller Ims to step warily, for the depot has
the bustle of a railway station. Here a
porter’s hand-trolley brings along a load of
stuff, a war lady giving the necessary im-
petus. There, cutting-out. tables have an
orchestra of snipping scissors. And at side-
tables busy women contrive —goodness knows
how—to tackle the type machine or the
handy inkpot.
The Anti-Shouting angels are winding up
miles of names on garden-hose reels. Hav-
ing caught your hare the next thing is not
to cook it to rags. So the 80,000 signatures
are being put snugly and safely to bed in
the cheapest nests inventive woman could
suggest. The last signature will go down
on June 21.

The Voluntary Workers’ Cafe (Warkon
Buildings, Eliza beth-street—-it used to be
Holland House before the new owner in-
flicted on it the name of his Bananaland
station) sets its hooks to catch special
attractions at lunch-time. This Thursday
Ada Reeve puts on cap and apron to take the
order for chop one, so there’s likely to be an
early-door queue.
There must be plenty of money left, in
spite of the twin income taxes. Velvet is a
positive obsession with ordinary womenfolk
this winter —not the shiny cotton black ’teen,
but the rich pile itself. Made with decora-
tive motifs of wide silk braid in a rich plum
shade, a frock of this kind (or in black, if
you like it better) is a good advt. for plenty
of money.
Don’t forget that gala matinee for France
in the Tivoli on June 26.
A “Tommy Cooker” concert is to happen
in tile Town Hall some time in July. Hon.
organiser Eslick and hon. concert manager
Oswald Anderson are in charge. Of course
you can guess who will collect the Cookers
at the Front.
The late Matthew Harris’s father came
to Australia about the year 1542 to inherit
a part of the big estate left by his relative,
'John Harris. John was a surgeon’s mate
who arrived with the First Fleet and subse-
quently got large grants of land,, the Ultimo
estate being one of them. Ultimo got its
name in a peculiar way. The surgeon's mate
was summoned to a court-martial, or some-
thing of the sort, and the summons gave the
date as “ Ultimo.” John made a
gibe out of this, and the story got so well
known that the name stuck.
Nelson Illingworth threatens recitals for
the winter season, beginning July 16. The
Conservatorium houses song-cycles by Schu-
bert and Schumann.
The air throbs with preparations for
France’s Day. On this Friday morn there’ll
be a meeting at the Town Hall to talk about
a lhatinee ball. Rose Lyons is hon. organiser.
Local Nightingales are in a flutter over
the news that more nurses are wanted for
British military hospitals and service abroad.
Numbers of qualified nurses are willing to
give up lucrative work here and tend the
men who fight for us. But they are hum-
bugged fearfully. You put your name down
and are accepted, and it’s months probably
before you are called up. The “calling up,”
by the Avay, means, even for the most experi-
enced woman, taking pot luck in a local
base hospital and going through an official
mill. You see, nobody is supposed to know
a thing about anything unless the Defence
Department has clone the teaching.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, June 18, 1917.
My Dear Yarrie.—
The dreary wet streets were livened up on
Thursday by the G.-G.’s procession toward
Parliament House. The potentate carried
his broom on his cocked hat and wore scarlet
and white feathers—a strange sight in these
plain khaki days. Lady Helen smiled and
bowed to both sides of the street from under
a hard little black pot hat which bloomed
with a circlet of pansies set in a row. Gent-
helps Iverr-Pearse and Conant livened their
khaki with scarlet trimmings and rode pranc-
ing motors. A fine square of mounted khaki
men went ahead and another square block of
the riders followed the petrol fumes. Postil-
lions, alas! have no place in a motor; the
gorgeous creatures must have ceased postil-
lioniug and gone into business since the war.
I think Melba sees the sun rise every
morning. Nothing happens too early for her
to attend. Her latest morning sprint was
from Coombe Cottage to the 10.30 opening
of Parliament. She brought the Lady Susan
along with her, and Willie Kelly escorted
them both to seats of honor. At half-time
Defence Pearce came along and mur-
mured a few platitudes, and Billy Hughes
was presented humbly.
Melba had a trying moment when little
Dr. Maloney began “God Save” on a fear-
fully high note and was too proud to start
again. The other members who tried to help
him out never sighted the right key either.
There was no echo of Melba’s golden notes
in the confusion.
“Nice-looking girl” was the general com-
ment when Associate Nancy Isaacs meekly
followed her father the Judge into the House.
Mrs. Isaacs looked on from the strangers’
gallery.
Prime Minister Hughes, in his new cut-
away coat, was the tailored success at the
opening of Parliament. The author of “The
Case for Labor” will now proceed to write
“The Showcase for Phewsion.*’
Actor Cyril Maude was given a welcome
at the Savage Club on Wednesday. On
behalf of the tribe, vice-president Percy
Cook (John Madden being ill) invited Maude
to come into the garden of their hearts, so
to speak, and have a drink. Unfortunately
a drink does not mean all it should to the
comedian, as some months ago he underwent
an operation which has deprived him of all
sense of taste and smell. But lie still has
a sense of humor and is an entertaining
speaker. His old-established manager, Fred
Turner, obliged with a number of stories
(only some of them have been repeated to me),
and savages Bert Bailey, Loch Fraser and
Freddie Newman contributed to the general
liveliness. Cyril Maude is an old Charter-
house boy, and was pleased to find another
in the austere person of Teddy Holroyd, a
committeeman hnd foundation member of
the club.
Cyril Maude had a bumper house for his
first night in Australia, and a satisfactory
number of late-comers were turned away.
Mrs. Billy Hughes glowed in fuchsia colors
and furs. Melba was resplendent in black
lit with diamonds; she wore a tiara of
matched diamonds set in a semi-circle, with
a diamond osprey rising nearly a foot into the
air. Lady Susan Fitzclarence, with her
hair exquisitely curled, sat in the circle,
attended by George Armstrong. The Charlie
Mitchells sat within coo-ee. Mrs. Leslie
Stuart wore a pale face and a pale ivory
dress with a streak of pink tulle across the
shoulders. She has been chief nurse to her
sick husband for many weeks. The Percy
Stuarts had a party, and George Allan was
in it. Mrs. Tommy Reynolds bunched her
fair curls into *a blue snood, and wore a
muffling fur coat. Little Mrs. Jowett, in
tucked criss-cross tulle of all-black, kept her
company. Cunningham, of the Argus, was
there with his lady in silver and lace. Editor
Schuler, of the Age, put in a rare appear-
ance. All the big theatrical guns were gath-
ered together from Sydney and Adelaide.
Hugh Ward boxed with the Tallis pair, and
Mrs. Tallis inhabited a beautiful black frock.
A south box was packed with pink—a recent
bride and her bridesmaids.
Maude made a spicy Irish speech at flower
time, and Melba threw her bouquet of wattle
and winter roses to him.
Dorothy Cumming, as the adored gran’-
daughter, blossoms in numerous lovely frocks.
She allures visitor Jarvis in silver tissue
with a turquoise sash. Then she gets ready
to motor up to “Lunnon” by wearing Robin-
Hood green with a great square collar of
sable. She repents of flirting in a fascinat-
ing frock of ivory weighted down with heavy
black seal, and she wins her diamond from
Jarvis in an early-morning n6alia6 of amber.
Amber is the color for stage undress at the
moment.
If there are any Happy Moments Day by
Day in the life of dyspeptic W. M. Hughes
he must have had one of them last Saturday
when he was about to sit down in Melba s
box to see “Grumpy.” He certainly got a
great reception from the side of the house
that could see him —a warm and sudden
burst of recognition. Certainly this time
Little Willie had two great points in his
favor. It was Melba's box and he was late
in arriving. The Queen of Song had already
warmed the spot with notoriety, as it were.
A thousand eyes were looking for somebody
else to enter, and lo! William was the One.
A mummer, under the same circs., would
have come late on purpose, and the differ-
ence between a star ac-tor and Hughes in his
favorite Win-the-War character is not so
wide tljat the Premier can escape the soft
impeachment. Anyhow, a glow of satisfac-
tion suffused his dome of intellect when the
applause came, and remained with him all
the evening. Or was it sunburn?
The G.-G. and Lady Helen had Cyril
Maude to dinner on Sunday. Melba was
there also —-she was week-ending at Govern-
ment House. Captain d’Erlanger and his
wife (Adelaide visitors) also drew in their
chairs, and jolly little Lady Doris just got
home in time from her N. S. Wales jaunt.
Every rich apostle of Thrift had her own
way of showing that she was practising as
well as preaching. The widow Staughton.
who is embarrassed with wealth, campaigned
always in the same liat, a big black umbrella
shape, of pronounced dowdiness. With this
shield she felt immune from all acid com-
ment on furs and laces. The chief topics of
the speeehmakiug dames were darning and
methods of preserving stockings and gloves.
The wretched poor were advised to darn
fresh feet into half a sock and then adji a
stocking “top.” Then the expert would go off
to a bridge gamble and bewail the fact that
her new mole silk stockings cost 1 (is. 9d.
At one of the finals, in the Austral Salon,
the most eloquent speaker was the wife of
a big tradesman. She fairly thundered about
the wisdom of paying cash.
Yet, after we have finished poking fun at
them, we’ll have to admit that there are
dozens of these women who really do know
more about economy in food —and dress too
—than the average Merely Mary does, or the
average working-man’s missus either. That
is why lots of them are rich now. But,
mostly because they would never dream of
putting it that way, they are poor Thrift
campaigners. The only Thrift campaign that
will do much good will have to be carried on
in the schools.
One desperate woman, at her wits’ end,
took the forlornly-hopeless course of writing
to the Argus to denounce the Melbourne
draper for his villainy in helping to push
priced up out of the reach of the masses.
Complainant had three grown-up daughters,
she declared, and their dresses, which for-
merly cost only six or seven guineas, were
now 12 to 15 guineas. It seems hard, of
course: but there is a harder time coming,
and the only thing for the masses to do,
apparently, will be to cut out the fur-trim-
mings and silk-linings and etceteras which
go to lift a five-guinea rig up to 15 guineas.
The Workers’ Educational Association,
which picks up crumbs of culture at Mel-
bourne Uni., is now well into its second
year. Members seek their ration of brain-
food on Saturday afternoons, when various
professors and other agitators serve up tasty
fragments of knowledge in pleasant sur-
roundings. There was a regular jamboree
last Saturday, with three hours of discus-
sion in the afternoon, followed by an 18-
penny bun-worry and an entertainment pro-
vided by Prof. Laver and his trained song-
and-piano artists, with Prof. Osborne lec-
turing about his last trip to England.
The Italian Red Crossers are to have a
tea party in the Australia on Saturday, the
last day of June. There will be some ex-
cellent., music.
All the Italian Red Cross parties are to
lead up to an Italian Button Day, billed for
July 6. Gaud Mayor Hennessy and his lady
are doing their social best and working up
a big Town Hall meeting to be held at the
end of this week. Mrs. Woolcott is organiser.
From “Rosna” in London: —
Dr. Eustace Black, son of the J. M. Blacks of
Adelaide, had an appointment in the North of Eng-
land lately. The only girl was Julia Dixon, whose
father is o.ne of the panjandrums of the Bank of
Liverpool. Dr. Black has been mending warriors
since the early days. He went over to France with
the R.A.M.C. in the first month, and had two years
of it, mostly in the trenches. Then he chucked his
commission temporarily, and medical officered a trans-
port to Australia and back. Now he is returning to
France, while his bride puts in her time nursing.
Young Bronner, son of the headmaster of the
Sturt-street .school, Adelaide, said the word at Cam-
bridge on April 7. He left Oxford to do some Hun-
chasing and was sent to an O.T.C. at Cambridge to
learn the job. Here Pauli Bartels, of Adelaide,
lately joined him—and there you are I
Melbourne has been entertaining Mr. and
Mrs. Greenwood, two young, good-looking
•English travellers who arrived here by the
So-and-so. He is an expert in drawing copper-
wire, and thes are on their way to Kembla
(X.S.W.). where the new industry is to be
set going.
Cyril Maude has been revelling in the
beauties of Coombe Cottage as Melba’s guest.
The star, by the way, was in Australia once
before; it was a health trip that time, and
he spent four months. He finds now a
good many of the old-timers whom he re-
members and who remember him.
The Tom Rutledges (Western District)
have just lost their handsome elder boy,
Lieutenant Noel. He was killed in France
last week. Younger brother Foster, who
flies for his country, married a Manifold girl
a few months back in England. Two of
Mrs. Tom’s nephews fell in action lately.
Colonel and Mrs. Stone are at the George,
St. Ivilda. He's a big gun from Burma, on
long leave, and is seeing Australia for the
first time.
Lilydale galloped very gently at the local
hunt last Wednesday, to oblige the Lady
Susan, wjio made her first appearance in the
field mounted on one of Melba’s horses. It
was the Englishwoman’s third time only on
horseback, and she stuck it almost like an
Australian. George Armstrong has been
coaching her.
Sister Adelaide’s weekly confession: —
Australia Day—proceeds for the South Aus. Sol-
diers’ Fund —is to be on July 27, and on Tuesday the
Governor presided at a meeting: to arrange prelimi-
naries. Money is to be extracted by much the same
means as last year, and as the fund’s expenditure
for the 12 months has been £30,688, there is a
tidy hole to be filled up. An idea is afoot to boom
the war loan on the same day, by selling war-savings
booklets in the streets, with two 6d. stamps already
attached as an incentive to further economies.
The Army Nurses’ Club lunched 300 ministering
angels who flew through on Thursday. Same day a
detachment of French soldiers were entertained by
the indefatigable Cheer-Uppers, and there was barely
breathing space at the railway station in the after-
noon when nurses and soldiers, wattle- and fruit-
laden, went away. Incidentally, the Adelaide Hos-
pital, 35 of whose nurses have enlisted, is asking
the Government to make a general increase in the
pay of the staff, giving first-year probationers £2O
instead of £l2 a year, second- and tfiird-year pro-
bationers £2B and £36 instead of £2O, and charge
nurses £9O instead of £BO.
The Attorney-General gave the Society for the
Prevention of Cruelty to Animals a pat at its annual
meeting, and announced his conviction that the
Society “had so educated public opinion that no
Government would care to refuse any reasonable
request for assistance.” Personally he had iai view
an amendment of the law with a view to more
humane methods of stock-branding. The Society
lias made Mrs. George Box, who has gone
from the secretaryship of the Queen Adelaide Club to
the same billet at the Quambi in Melbourne, and
who was one of the S.P.C.A.’s keenest workers, a life
member. The president, by the way, is Professor
Stirling, who appeared at this meeting carrying his
new handle —his first public appearance since it
happened.
Yardea station, north-west of Port Augusta and
one of the State’s best-known leaseholds, has
changed hands at £63,000, its new owner being
A. J. Mcßride, of the Burra.
The Conservatorium’s new violin instructor, Gerald
Walenn, arrived by the last boat. Amongst his
possessions are a wife and a Strad., the latter left
to him for his life by the late Mr. Rutson, a York-
shireman who took him in hand when he was 12 and
sent him to the Roj'al Academy of Music. At Mr.
Walenn’s death the fiddle goes to the Royal Academy.
At present it’s in England waiting till war risks
are over. A brother of Mr. Walenn’s was here with
Williamson's Opera Co. about two years ago.
A drenching night and the lowest temperature we
have had for nine years made no difference to the
attendance at Lady Galway’s lecture on Modern Lan-
guages on Tuesday evening. The issue of tickets
was stopped on Monday morning, and the Elder Hall
was packed. It was her Ex.’s first appearance after
weeks of sickness, and it was plain enough that only
pluck pulled her through. The size of the hall and its
bad acoustic properties made the mere strain of
an hour’s speaking no small thing, and about half
of the lecture was given sitting. The very gifted
lady has set a high standard for herself as a speaker,
but she quite outdistanced it this time. The Gov-
ernor and all the Uni. heads were on the platform,
a rather unique background, in this city at any rate,
for a woman speaker.
The Premier has come back from a trip to the
Murray with glowing accounts of the irrigation land
available for soldiers, and of the willingness of
present settlers to form themselves into repatriation
committees. Included in the Government’s scheme
is a canning factory, to take surplus products and
provide light employment for returned men unfit for
heavy work.
Dr. H. C. Rennie, who was invalided home about
nine months ago, after nearly a year with the
R.A.M.C., is to be medical superintendent at the
Bedford Park sanatorium for consumptive soldiers.
His father is one of the University staff; grand-dad,
a fine old Scot, was for a generation Auditor-General
of N. S. Wales.
Tenor Lois and his pretty wife, who cut
themselves adrift from the Gonsalez Opera
Co. and stayed on here, are full of extrava-
gant praise of Mel-boourne. Lois is being
rushed with engagements, and now Fuller is
waving a contract at him. As for Mrs. Lois,
sht? has been supplied for life with jewellery
by the music worshippers.

Daughter Daisy sang from Louise, and

“Wart”: Was introduced to a fine collec-
tion of fantods during; a stay at the local hospital last
Christmas—known to the nurses as “jinijam" week.
There was a cocky who had a herd of cattle pastur-
ing- in the ward. The nurses would leave the door
open and Pole}', or Strawberry, would be always
getting out. There were two bloques who saw Un-
common variety of blue devils, with pink tails, sit-
ting on the bottom- of the bed, and “lookin’ at me
with green eyes.” There was one chap who held
long conversations with his wife’s ghost. She gener-
ally appeared wearing a pair of sealskin wings. Then
there was a carrier who used to drive his team all
night. He would pick a time about two o’clock in
the morning to get his waggon tangled in a gate, or to
have a bolt. Very few patients remained asleep
after he had harnessed up. Another man, a bailor,
called the nurse “stewardess” and the doctor “skip-
per.” He confided to me that it was the first ship
lie ever had been on which was built of bricks.
“Te Whare” : At' least three of our birds
include cat-calls among their notes, viz, the satin
bower-bird, the cat-bird and the falcon-shrike. I
have never heard the latter two utter anything but
sounds between the growling and meowing of a eat,
but the bower-bird is a good mimic (second only to
the lyre-bird) and lias many strange notes. Both
cat-bird and falcon-shrike are uncommon. The for-
mer, though a member of the bower-bird family
(akin to the starlings), does not build a bower;
its rough nest resembles a collection of leaves and
bark deposited accidentally in the fork of a tree.
Its two cream-colored eggs, spotted and marked with
brown, are prized by collectors. Occasionally, but
rarely, one finds the falcon-shrike, strikingly attired
in bright yellow vest, greenish-grey coat, and cocka-
too-like crest of black and white, working over the
trunks of trees in open-timbered country and coastal
scrubs, in quest of insects. The nest is a duplicate
or 1 that of the white-shafted fantail, or “cranky fan,”
only about three times as large, and is formed of
fine bark covered with grey cobweb and shaped like
a wineglass with the foot broken off.
“Montalex” ; “Bromide” (B. sl/o/’l7 ) is
right in saying that the potency of snake-venom
cannot be judged by the rapidity with which it
knocks a man over. 1 should have made it clear
that, in all estimates of relative potency, identical
injection-conditions are assumed. It is within my
own experience that, when the snake-fang pene-
trates a vein, the action of the venom is greatly
accelerated. I once—out of scientific curiosity—
allowed a bullant to sting me in one of the super-
ficial veins on the bark of the hand. I knew to
some extent what to expect, but I hadn’t thought it
would take me a week to get clear of the effects of
that infinitesimal drop of formic acid, placed directly
into the circulation. As for potassium-permanganate,
I have always advocated its use, though of course
it is only an antidote to the extent that it is a
decompositive.
* * * *
“Country Ped”: This is my epistle to the
mouse-haunted. Build a mouse-proof chaff-house of
corrugated iron. Put some freshly-cut chaff in it.
Carelessly leave a long gangway of wood leaning
against the wall and touching a wall plate. Go to
bed. In the morning get a trough of water, place,
it close against the door, and enlist all hands as
assistant-murderers to stand round the trough. Then
open tiie door and commence the drive to the sea.
For tallying purposes remember that 800 drowned
mice go to a kerosene tin. The inventor of this sys-
tem filled 20 kerosene tins the first time, and three
next time—say 21,000 mice in two nights!
“H.V.E.” : Governor Phillip’s crowd are to
some extent responsible for our somewhat tangled and
non-illuminative bird-nomenclature. Yet these early
(and in many instances, unwilling) arrivals struck
some bird-titles which have stuck, and are likely to
stick. For instance, magpie, robin, wagtail, blue-
wren, curlew, eagle-hawk, and blue crane for the
piping crow-shrike, a red-breasted
bird of the genus Petroica but no
relative of the European robin,
black - and - white (why not
“pied”?) fantail, superb warbler,
stone-plover, wedge-tailed eagle,
and heron have come to stay.
“Peewit,” however, has given way
to magpie-lark, and “Rosehill
parrakeet” has become “rosella.”
Most of the birds first mentioned
were common about Sydney, and
quickly attracted attention. Half
a century later Gould came along,
and after only two years’ sojourn
in Australia made about 600 Aus-
tralian birds known (or better
known) to the world. Gouid was
careful to give every name, abo-
riginal and other, by which many
birds were already known. Since
his time, however, the bird-men
generally—after commandeering a
large supply of adjectives—have
tacked them, hyphenated, on to
our birds so liberally that few
people have time to call any par-
ticular bird by its right name.
“Musketoon ' : Knock all those splinter-
cutters on the head and listen to this. At his Kil-
larney property, near Armidale (jST.S.W.), George
Nott felled and cut up a Queensland hoop-pine tree.
When on its feet the shrub measured 90ft. to the
first limb, and it produced over 10,000 feet of
sawn timber.

“Miner” : Re tables of weights and meas-
ures. Bill, the timber-getter, was telling his mates
about the comet he had seen the night before. Some-
one chipped in to ask where it was. Bill said he
reckoned it was about 15 and a half axe-handles
from the moon when he saw it.

“Eddyson”: Johnnie Dodd was grave-
digger and caretaker of the cemetery at Yam.
Johnnie, who was quite 70, had adopted the ceme-
tery. It became his hobby. He devoted his life to*
it. He repaired the fence, excluded the goats,
painted the sign afresh every year, mended all paths
and railings, and had always a grave ready for the
next-comer. Furthermore, he invariably kept the
“next'’ in his mind’s eye. Yam was at this stage in
a state of hopeless decadence. Its mining had almost
entirely failed it some years earlier, and now there
were few men left but those who were too old to
drift. -The cemetery, as Johnnie’s private inheri-
tance, alone retained the comely order that had dis-
tinguished its public institutions when Yam owned
a council of its own and Johnnie Dodd was its
mayor. Now, Johnnie was a little, hard, old, wire-
haired terrier of a man pottering all day long in his
cemetery, where his wife’s grave was, supported
mainly by contributions from sons abroad in the
world, but exacting his inexorable fee of five pounds
for every grave before deceased was permitted to enter
into occupation. In these later days Dodd had
mapped out his grounds, ticking, off a space for every
likely person in the district. These were lined out/
with mathematical precision, the initials of the per-
son with the pre-emptive right being nicely inscribed
in the centre with white quartz pebbles. This idea
was not very popular, but Johnnie knew very
definitely “which was who’s,” and when one graVe
was filled set confidently to work preparing the next
in succession according to a private list he had
drawn up, and which was stowed away in the
old cowhide bunk under his bed. The existence of
this list was well known, and the ancients of Yam
had a superstitious faith in its accuracy. Johnnie
himself believed in it implicitly. When he got to
calling in on any particular old identity and in-
quiring very kindly after his health, it .was known
beyond argument who was next. So accurate had
Johnnie’s judgment been that none of the old
people doubted. The man or woman chosen accepted
the inevitable, which frame of mind, no doubt,
accounted to a large extent for Dodd’s success as a
prognosticator. It had come to old Dad Wybrow’s
turn. Coming out of the cemetery after the inter-
ment of John Cochran, Johnnie had plucked
Wybrow’s sleeve and said: “ ’N’. 'ow’s this yer
wintry weather a-playin’ up with you, ’Enrv?” Dad
Wybrow stopped and stared at Johnnie. “This
wintry weather’s findirt’ me pertickler well!” he
snorted. “I’ll be at the buryin’ of your darned ole
carcase, blarst yer eyes!” He hobbled off a few
paces, turned back and shook his stick. “Sec if I
don’t, blarst yer eyes!” said he. Johnnie only
scratched his head and grinned. “Durnecl hard
a-dyin’, them Methody men,” said he. “Durned
unwiHin' fer Glory.” On a later occasion Dad met
him in a much pleasanter manner. “Good day to
you, ole Dodd,” said he. “Up seein’ ’ow I’m
a-dyin’, are yeh? Well, I’m fit ex a fiddle, ’n’ til
ex a fiddle I’ll stick. You mod a mistook this time,
Johnnie. You look yer list up again, man. Put
yer glasses on, Johnnie, ’r ax someone what’s better
at leadin’ nor you are.” Johnnie went from this
meeting greatly upset. He actually did fish out the
cowdiide trunk, and examine the list again. He sat
peering at that list for an hour, his toothless old
mouth set in speechless consternation. A decline
began from that day. Everybody noticed it. One
morning three weeks later they found him lying in
the half-dugout grave of his wife, on which long ago
he’ had set his own initials in white quartz. He
died within 24 hours. Only when the grave was

THE ABSENT JACK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The swagmen never troubled him
While they had strength to live;
They knew his fame, and it was grim
Ile’d give not, nor forgive.
.Caretaker at the Ten-mile Tank,
He’d “tackle ane or baith” —
A big man and an ugly man,
And dour was Mucklewraith.
The clouds had formed a lurid bank
That told of death and drought;
The drover camped beside the tank"
And turned his horses out.
The pallid moon was rising higher—
The long, hot day was gone—
The drover made a “bit er fire,”
And put his quart-pot on.
The old man came out to inquire
What private tanks were dry ;
The drover said (a little dryer) :
“They all are. So am 1....
And you are Mister Mucklewraith -
I’ve heard of you Outback!”
And then he added, in good faith:
“1 worked with your son .lack.”
“So you knew Jack?” the old man gnarled
In his suspicious way
(His form was tall, and gaunt and gnarled;
His beard was iron-grey).
“Yes, 1 knew Jack,” the drover said —
“I shore with him awhile.”
The old man stood and bent his head,
And watched the quart-pot “bile.”
“We’re shorter grub,” the old man said;
The goodwife’s gone for more;
The ration team is at the shed—
She’s drove in to the store.”
The drover never said a word ;
The whole thing was a bore—
With variations he had heard
The selfsame yarn before.
“So you knew Jack?” the old man sighed
(His mood was softened now).
“I did,” the droving man replied—
“ For six weeks, anyhow.”
lie pushed the coals up with his foot,
Not anxious to begin.
He took the quart-pot off and put
The tea and sugar in.
“So you knew Jack ?” The old man woke
As from a reverie.
The drover said “I never spoke,”
A tired man was lie.
He twirled the quart-pot once—just so—
To give the tea a stir;
He pressed some coals down with his toe
And placed the quart-pot there.
The old man might have “fiad a shot”
At football long ago— .
He raised his foot and kicked the pot
A dozen yards or so.
“That there’s the missus cornin’ back—
Come to the house,” he cried;
“No man that worked with my son Jack
Shall bile his tea outside!”
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.

“Main Shaft”: Is the little wil-warbler,
alias rock-robin, the only bird which prefers to build
his nest in dark places? I have recently been
examining some mines opened by tunnels in the
side of a gorge and abandoned for many years. All
along the drives, for upwards of 200 feet and in
total darkness, the loosely-constructed, somewhat
pear-shaped nests of the birds were suspended from
the “back/’ and their owners often emerged from
very unexpected places to question the intruder’s
business. The few old hands who remained in the
neighborhood told me the little brown fellows were
regular visitors at crib-time, no matter how far
underground the working places were. I have since
found the rock-warbler quite tame and sociable; a
pair oven attempted to build a nest in my office.
“Norseman”: Abdul Wade’s Wangantana
station, Wanaaring (N.S.W.), has the biggest num-
ber of camels congregated in one locality in Aus-
tralia, The tally is 700. Abdul has been hoosh-
taing camels ever since Australia has had any to
hooshta. When the railway got to Broken Hill he
sent out long strings from there; and he had been
working from the Bourkc side long before that.
Later, he tried to introduce (he untidy beast to
North Queensland ; but that was a failure.
* * * *
“Bibb”: Anent snake-bito cures, an alleged
remedy, current in the ’so's, was known as “Under-
wood’s Mixture,” and was concocted in Tassy. Under-
wood, seeing a' “stump lizard,” bitten by a deadly
snake, hurry away and chew the leaves and stalks of
a certain plant, made up his decoction frtfm the herb
selected by the lizard. Morrissey’s antidote is a
dark-brown fluid alleged by some people to
be compounded from the bulbs of the cun-
gevoi lily ( Colocasia macrorrlnza), which is a native
of N. S. Wales and Queensland. The aboriginals
father the young bulbs of this plant, dry them for
a time in hot ashes, pound them into a sort of flour,
and finally fashion a cake, which is then carefully
baked. While fresh the bulbs of the cungevoi lily
contain an acrid principle, which turns the skin red
if applied to it. The juice of these bulbs is
said to be an effective remedy for the sting of the
giant nettle, or nettle-tree ( La-portea gigas), common
in gullies on South Coast, N.S.W., and in Queens-
land; also for mosquito and other insect bites. The
term “cungevoi,” of course, has no connection with
the lowly marine animal of the same name which
is succulent bait for groper, blackfish, morwong and
many other fishes.

DAIRY FARMER, J.P.: “Y' say he insulted you. What did he call you?”

“Wandi”: A prolific whelping-place of
dingoes is the dunes of the Diamantina, parts of
which are honeycombed with holes, for the dingoes
burrow in the sand much the same as the wombat.
Often the roof of a burrow is merely a shell of com-
pressed sand, and a horse stepping on .to it falls
through with a plunge. Where burrowed ground is
indicated no horse bred in the country can be
induced to cross it. The horse behaves similarly
in the matter of rabbit warrens. Between August
and November pups are dug out in hundreds, as
many as 40 scalps being obtained in a day by a
couple of scalpers. Most of the dingoes that have
been lately worrying the sheep-man in the north-
west of N. S. Wales are said to hail from this great
breeding-ground. They follow the water-courses down
after good rains.
“TeW.”: Both cattle and sheep relish the
leaves and seed capsules of the native hop shrubs
(dononcea ), particularly in bad seasons. I have
seen jumbucks, given plenty of room, keep in good
condition on the vegetable in a season when scarcely
a blade of grass was available. More important,
however, is the fact that the capsules make a ser-
viceable yeast and light beer of good quality. Inci-
dentally, a palatable beer can be brewed from
common nettles, while the tender tops of the young
plants are reputed not only a good substitute for
spinach, but a blood purifier which the boil-plagued
bushman may use in place of his nauseous decoction
of brook lime (hyssop) and Epsom salts.
* * * *
“Yarrum”: Will sheep-dogs kill sheep ? A
few years ago, at Moruya (N.S.W.), a butcher
claimed £2O from a sheep-dog owner for jumbucks
destroyed. Two alleged experts assured the Court
that sheep-dogs wouldn’t molest sheep. Case dis-
missed. Later on, at Mittagong station (Riverina),
I saw manager Sissons shutting a slut up at night.
Asked why, he said, “The bally thing might get
among the sheep, and kill some.” I told him that
sheep-dogs wouldn’t kill sheep. He laughed.
“Silas Snell”: When Willard's Variety
Entertainers went to pieces at Bullfoot, the Pasco
Brothers decided that, after • all, there was more
money in graft than art, and accepted billets at the
puddlers by Pea Creek. Six men took over the big
slab barn at Creswell's, and batched there, Tim
Cronan among .the rest. Tim was a bearable man
but for two serious defects, a proneness to inebria-
tion and a passion for theological argument. At
some time in some way he had picked up certain
quaint ideas bearing upon the theory of reincarna-
tion, and he would harp on the subject till all was
blue. This set’ the boys meditating revenge for
long boredom, and the administration of an effective
lesson. One Saturday night Tim came home from
Jonathan's at The Fork, speechless, pitched on to
his bunk, and was dead to tire world. Then the
Pasco Bros, fished out their old trunk, and got to
work. They shaved Tim clean, they carefully wal-
nutted his complexion, gave his eyebrows an upward
lift, and almondised his eyes. They shaved his
head, leaving only a patch of natural wool, into
which a long pigtail was woven. Then Tim was
dressed in a Chow suit of blue dungarees, Chow
slippers were put on his feet, and ho was carried to
the hut of Sue Kit, at the camp of Chinese fossickers
on Brandy Flat, and left there. Creswell had
that day engaged Sue Kit as cook. To Sue Kit on
Sunday morning the conspirators"presented Cronan's
best suit and Cronan’s hat and boots, per-
suading him to don them at once. The
awakening of Tim Cronan in the Chow camp
on Sunday morning was a stirring event. Tim
came out of Sue Kit’s hut into the sunlight, liter-
ally walking around himself in his bewilderment,
looking down at his clothes, handling the offensive
pigtail, and feeling his whiskevless face in pathetic
perplexity. He was still somewhat under the
influence of Jonathan’s benzine, but sober enough to
appreciate the fact that a great calamity had come
into his life. The Chows, w T ell primed by Harry
Pasco, sat about in the sunshine, babbling amiably.
Tim advanced to these. “Say,” said he timorously,
“would you moind telling me who I yam ?” Kip
Fit answered him in eloquent Chinese. “But who
am I?” asked Tim impatiently. “Wha’ for?”
answered Kip Fit. “You Sue Kit all li. You go
makee dlain,” and Kip Fit gave Tim a kick. “Wliat's
that?” gasped Cronan in a small, awful voice. Kip
Fit offered him a shovel. “Go makee dlain,” he
said, and kicked Tim again. Meekly, utterly
bewildered, Tim took the shovel, and started to dig.
Suddenly he uttered a yell of mortal anguish, and
ran for the creek. On his knees he gazed woefully
at his reflection in a clear pool. Then with an even
more poignant yell he started up and made for home.
By the way he met Hide. “Have yeh seen Tim
Cronan?” said he. Hide shook his head. “Me no
see him long time, Sue,” he said. Off went Tim
again. At, Creswell’s he sighted Sue Kit in the
garden, Sue Kit in his clothes and hat, and he

LOGAN’S PLACE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If you follow the track from Delannit
You will come to a fork in the way,
And the path to the right will unfold to your sight
All the glory of sand-dunes and granite,
Set out at the foot of a bay.
But if you are tired of the splendor
Of shimmering waters a-race,
Let your feet wander back to the fork in the track,
And along the left path that is slender
And rough, you will, find Logan’s place.
The fields are still harrowed and waiting
The wheat that will never be'sown,
While last season’s hay has long since turned to grey.
The ground-larks are building and mating
In grass that will never be mown.
In the background a little, rough shanty,
Unfinished and most up to date,
Stands sad and alone in its plaster and stone,
And this, in his spare time, most scanty,
Young Logan was building for Kate. '
Having just read the top verses over,
I’ll admit they’re not up to much.
I intended to shunt Logan off to the Front,
To be drowned in a troopship near Dover,
And end with a sad sort of touch.
And Kate was to stand by a slip rail
Each night, with a tear in her eye,
And gaze to the west with a sob in her br?ast;
Or convulsively clutch an old milk-pail,
And murmur: “Oh, how could you die!”
But it seems, after fresh recollection,
It ends in a much brighter way.
Logan s aunt, it appears, whom he’d not seen for
years,
Pegged out, just to show her affection—
Left Logan a hundred a day.
SOUTH AUSTRALIA.
GEORGE R. HAMBIDGE.

Edouard de Reszke, whom lvultur has just
killed in Poland, was at an agricultural col-
lege in Silesia before he had serious thoughts
of the opera stage. In ’7O lie appeared as
the King in “Aida,” with Verdi conducting,
and thereafter he never looked at a hayfork
until he had saved enough to make a country
home in his unlucky native land. As it hap-
pened. it was right in the path of the gun-
music.
“Monkbarns”: Almost (50 years ago Bob
Lawrence and his wife, just-dead Carry
George, were on the high tide of favor at
Ballarat. He was a rival of Dick Cox and
Stuart O’Brien, and with them would only
be displaced by stars like Brooke, 11. N.
Warner, Stark or Clarence Holt. Lawrence
was thought fine as Raphael, in “The Marble
Heart,” with Mary Provost. Other princi-
pal ladies of the hour included Kate O Reilly
and Kate Warde, sister to Con Warde,
whose son is a Victorian M.L.A. Carry
George, at Ballarat, was prime favorite in
juveniles, and as comedienne she made
“Bachelors’ Buttons” a delightful farce. The
Lawrences hardly got a proper show in Mel-
bourne. He was Joseph Surface, in “School
for Scandal,” at the opening of the Royal
Amphitheatre (late Astley’s, and ultimately
Princess Theatre, so named by Alexander
Henderson). Towards the end of Barry
Sullivan’s time in Melbourne, Lawrence had
an engagement with him; and both Law-
rence and his wife were prominent in
Madame Celeste’s season at Coppin’s Ilay-
market.
Sid Witton, brother of the Witton who
found trouble in 1900 with Morant (“The
Breaker”) during the Boer War, received
an inquisitive bullet into his system while
doing his share of the Big Job. The missile
entered his right breast, passed his lung,
missed his heart by a decimal, slipped round
by his left lung, and embedded itself in the
flesh close to his left ribs. It should have
killed him; but it left him with enough life
to successfully run a picture-show in Kor-
rumburra (Vic.).
“Fax” in gentle protest: —
Theatrical criticism in the Melbourne dailies
grows unconsciously humorous alike in censure and
in praise. An Argus notice of a farcical comedy at
the Royal started off with this pompous piffle:
“When an author is content to allow his play to go
into the world entitled ‘A Little Bit of Fluff lie
marks his ambitions fairly clearly.” But if the
critic were asked to explain why a popular
slang term for a feather-headed girl should not serve
as a title he would be cornered. In H. A. Jones’s
play, “The Bauble Shop,” a Prime Minister is
warned, that “It isn’t immorality that ruins a man,
but one of those damned silly things that all of us
do and only one man gets found out in.” What’s the
matter, then, with “A Little Bit of Fluff” as a
title for a story of a clergyman’s undoing? Inci-
dentally, it is certain that when Cyril Maude opens
at the Royal the Argus will not scoff at the author
who allowed his play to go forth into the world
entitled “Grumpy.” So much for the humors of
censure. Now for the absurdities of praise. Mel-
bourne Herald picked out two gems of melody from
“The White Chrysanthemum.” “At its best,” it said,
“in items such as ‘This House We’ve Come to Search*
and ‘Three Bad Boys,’ Mr. Howard Talbot’s music
has much of the sprightliness of Sullivan’s comic
ditties” (as though ditties were music and Sullivan
wrote ’em). Also the Age picked the same two
musical numbers as having “something of the snappy
Gilbert and Sullivan atmosphere about them.” Well,
perhaps they had; but the music was not by Howard
Talbot. They were interpolated item?. Barry
Lupino faked up the words, and Keers, musical con-
ductor at Melbourne King’s, supplied the melodies.
It is always dangerous to credit the nominal composer
of a musical comedy with any particular melody that
is sung in it.
“Silas”: I defy anyone to ring-in as curi-
ous and unexpected a theatre as my friend
Ayrton struck at Miners, when doing the
Vic. “smalls” with a packhorse, a portman-
teau of films of the vintage of 1915, and
other appliances necessary for projection m
a district where-electric juice is not always
on tap. There had been a kind of sports
meeting at Miners, and it seemed probable
to Ayrton that £2O could be picked up here.
But what of a hall? The countryside boasted
no such thing. There wasn’t a barn in sight
that would hold £3. The sawmill cavern
came as a godsend. It was shown to Ayrton
as the Eighth Wonder of the World. Here
an old buried bed of sawdust had been
burnt out (it was 18 months burning),
caking the soil above into a roof a foot and
a half thick and as hard as brick. Ayrton
put three men on, filled these non-licensed
premises with seats of palings, slabs, logs,
any old thing (kerosene-case front seats,
25.), brought off his show, and got away with
£22 11s.! ..
Niagara and Falls, a man-and-wife
comedy team lately on the Tivoli circuit,
went soberly to court in Sydney last week
to recover £2OO for alleged breach of contract
from Harry Rickards Tivoli Theatres, Ltd.
Their grievance was that the management
had “laid them off” too much. The friend-
liness of opposing counsel moved Justice
Sly to suggest, before evidence had been
entered upon, that the matter of excessive
and unremunerative rest might be peaceab
settled, and -so it was. Verdict by consent
for plaintiffs for £lOO and a shilling.
THE CHORUS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Fair damsels, dancing in the gleams
Oi limelight when the show begins,
Your curious creed demands, it seems,
A full confession of your shins.
You skip about or block the view
In 'many kinds of quaint undress.
[ wonder for how long could you
Take little off to put on less!
. To educate, as I suppose,
The blades who take you out to sup,
You range your figures all in rows,
And languidly they sum you up.
You are indeed a novel type.
I wonder, as you cross the scene,
If Eve had hated apples ripe,
Mv dears, where would you all have been .

Business, Bobbery, etc.
“Do Oriel”: Apparently all country barb-
ers run a side-line. Mostly it is billiards;
but 1 have been shaved by razorists who
knew useful trades. In Fingal (Tas.).the
bootmaker obliged; in Omeo (Vic.) the pub-
lican sold me a long b'eer and then adjourned
to the saloon and removed my whiskers; in
Lowood (Q.) I was shaved by a man who
made a whistling noise with his lips the
while —he was a groom in liis spare moments.
But I hand the coin to the artist at Cowwar
(Vic.) who conducts a blacksmithing busi-
ness and heats horseshoes and shaving-
water in the one operation.

“Ex-Dairyman”: I can beat “Double
Il.’s” prices for a cow. At Matthews and
Young’s sale on the Bellinger River
(N.S.W.) at the latter end of last year a
goddess brought £lOl. At that sale a dairy-
man paid £l5 for a one-week-old calf.

“Te Babarantiko”: Before me lie a few
pieces of rock —pink-and-yellow conglomer-
ate, crumbling readily under finger pressure ;
some of it dark-grey and brownish, much
harder rock, yet easily disintegrated. Upon
such stone as this has been built prosperity
for a few whites and a greater number of
Indian coolies, and opulence and sloth for a
decadent native population. All in regions
where, less than 40 years ago, the first white
settler planted his corn with rifle slung
over his shoulder, and where human bodies,
bled white to make them tender for the bak-
ing ovens, were dragged past his front gate.
It is such rock as this that has made the
smiling flats of the Fiji sugar country. From
the foothills right to the tops of the
lofty mountains the grey and yellow of the
outcropping formation has a curiously
cracked and corroded appearance. Dig the
ferrule of your stick into it and the surface
falls apart as easily, almost, as coagulated
ashes. Look down upon the vast squares of
pale-green fields, a belt of varying width
from the upper foothills to the mangrove-
fringed, swampy beaches where scanty clust-
ers of sickly palms wave. Look up and down
the coast as far as eye can see. Then look
upwards to the tops of the great hills, dotted
at intervals with grotesque, stunted screw-
pine, up to the fantastic finger-like outcrops
on the mountain summits, and you see how
the hills have perished and are still perish-
ing to form that rich country on the one-time
coral flats below. The process has been
rapid owing to the remarkable absence of
vegetation other than rank grass and sparse
pandanus on the mountains of the western
and northern portions of Viti Levu. Begin-
ning east of Sigatoka and extending west and
north, this feature is peculiar, to the Fiji
sugar country, and makes a striking contrast
to the verdure-clad hills and mountains in
the south-eastern part of the island. The
decomposing rock seems to allow nothing but
the vegetation mentioned to take root. Yet
when washed down on the coral flats and
mixed with decaying grasses and the fecund
vegetation there, it becomes the best sugar-
growing soil known.
* * » »
“Bad Australian”: Some time ago a crowd
of Yanks bought up a tract of land in Para-
guay, stocked it with cattle and imported a
Wild West show from Texas or Arizona to
do the herding. Then they sat down to
watch themselves grow rich. But they had
hardly got through their first box of juicy
Cubanos before they found that some kind of
a “bug” stung their animals, so that small
abscesses appeared on the neck and shoulder
or any spot out of reach of tail or tongue.
Very soon these abscesses began to run, and
were found to contain a hard, bristly grub.
They discovered that there was no known
remedy, except to seal the borehole with
gum. The cattlemen did this, and the “bug”
died and was sloughed off. Next, the awful
Paraguayan blowfly got busy, and their last
case was much worse than the first. The
Paraguayan fly is so bad that if calves and
foals are not attended to within 24 hours of
birth and the vulnerable parts kept dressed
with an insecticide, there is little chance of
them surviving. The smallest open wound
on an animal becomes a horrible mass in a
few hours. When the Wild West show got
busy rounding-up and treating the animals
affected by the uru, it also discovered cases
of carbuncle, tick and foot-and-mouth dis-
ease, as well as a kind of rickets. About
this time the men from the North saw why
fat beef is such a rarity in Paraguay.
* * * *
“Bunyah”: I agree with all “Lou’s” re-
marks re the minor villainies of gardeners
(B. 17/5/T7). I knew one who, while he
bossed a four-aci'e plot for Mr. Fatman,
induced a local shopkeeper to open a seeds-
and-plants department, and kept it sup-
plied with stock raised in Fatman’s
garden. As occasion arose he bought back
the plants he required, and Mr. Fatman paid
for them. Fatman noticed it at last, and
the sack was administered. His successor,
a canny Scot, cut the middleman out alto-
gether;' but that was the only difference
between them.
Fish-dealers in Melbourne complain that
there is now scarcely any sale for lobsters.
Yet some people are still saying that we
don’t take the war seriously.
* * * *
“Billy T.”: Melbourne and Sydney publi-
cans have agreed that, the price of barrels
having risen, the glass must fall. “How-
ever,” says the Argus on behalf of the trade,
“the difference will not be more than a
mouthful.” That recalls the story of the
Australian ex-M.P. to whom came Randolph
Bedford in a bar. “Hello, old man ! said
Bedford. “Had your drink?” “Only a
mouthful,” was the reply. “I’ve had a
quart, too,” said Bedford.
* * * *
“Sonear & Yett”: After trying for a fort-
night, I managed to reach the barber’s
yesterday. I had been feeling as woolly as
a prize merino, and, when the shearing was
over, I told the operator how long I had
been waiting and how good 1 felt. “That s
right,” lie said ruefully, tossing his own
mane and sweeping my fallen fleece out of
the way. “I been trying to get a cut
ineself for three weeks. I’ll have to wait for
me next afternoon off, though. You're next,
sir.”

“Bad Australian” from the spot: —
In Paraguay the butcher, baker and kindred robbers
bave no delivery carts, so the housekeeper attends
the market, which usually occupies the plaza or
central square.
With the exception of the meat stalls, which are
run by men, all the business is done by women,
generally the oldest, ugliest and most garrulous. The
butcher, armed with a saw and a machete, dumps
the carcase on a bench and, starting at the heels,
saws and slashes it into gobs about a couple of
pounds weight until he cuts out at the horns. He
never attempts to joint the carcase. The meat is
all sold at the same price, but the fat, which is
almost as rare on Paraguayan beef as on kangaroo
meat, brings a few cents more.
Pumpkins, melons, cabbages and such are sold in the
same way as the meat. The old ladies squat on their
hapnehes 'with their stock-in-trade spread on the
ground before them. Said stock usually consists of
a few handsful of mandioca starch or meal, half a
dozen mandioca cakes, a stick or two of raw man-
dioca and sugar-cane, a pound or two of peas, beans,
peppercorns, raw coffee-beans and the peculiar pink-
ish-colored hand-dressed native rice. Add to these a
few mate pots made from small calabashes, a bunch
or two of “medicinal’’ plants, five or six hand-
kneaded balls of chocolate made of crudely-ground
cocoa beans, mandioca starch and molasses, an egg
or two, and a few bundles of native cigars. Multiply
the lot by 500 and the Paraguayan market is com-
plete. The whole outfit, apart from the beef, is worth
possibly £l5 or £2O. The native cigars, which are
small and mild, sell at the equivalent of sevenpence
English for a bundle of 100.
The only persons who really buy anything are the
foreigners, and I have known three earnest German
housewives to clean out the whole market.
The Paraguayan woman does most of the transport
of the country, carrying everything on her head. Give
her a 20ft. plank, an empty cask or a beer bottle,
she claps it on her head, whatever it may be, lights
another cigar, takes a fresh grip on the baby and
trots off.
* * * *
Horace Plunkett started out in ’B9 to tell
Pat that what he needed was less politics
and more business. Horace addressed 50meet-
ings before a society could be got going, and
it took two years’ hard talking and much
cash (Plunkett’s cash) before a second was
formed. To-day there are nearly 1000
societies, • through which the “distressful
counthry” sells the Sassenach the best part
of £30,000,000 worth of products every year.
It’s up to the Australian wheat cocky to
shoulder the fumbling politician and greedy
boodler out of the road and start doing
likewise.
* * * *
“Foe”: One of the “indefatigable secre-
tary” type turned up recently in a country
town, gave an exhibition of indefatigability
at a couple of small Red Cross functions, and
then disappeared, leaving a vacancy where
the cashbox should have been. It’s a mean
trick; but, for Heaven’s sake, ask the be-
diamonded charity hostess and her daughter,
the furred flapper, who “give” a Red Cross
shivoo and then use up more than half the
proceeds in refreshments and frills, not to
have any spasms over it.

“G.D.M.” (from ’Frisco) : Dr. Win. N. Bur-
ton, gasoline (petrol) authority of the Stan-
dard Oil Co. of Indiana and'inventor of the
Burton process of refining oil, predicts that
the gasoline (petrol) stocks of the U.S.A.
will be exhausted within a year unless meas-
ures are speedily taken to conserve them. He
contends that the production for this year
will be 2,500,000,000 gallons, of which
2,000.000,000 will be for home consumption
and 500,000,000 gallons for export. What
price petrol in Australia in 1919 —especially
if that country goes on doing hardly a thihg
to develop its own oil resources?
* * * *
“Rustler”; The Bendigo trams have
started a parcel-delivery system. On pay-
ment of threepence the co. undertakes to
deliver a package at any place along the
route. But occasionally the conductor for-
gets, and then the parcel has a long-distance
record that shames the thruppence.
