remarked upon it as a singular thing that
clergymen frequently feel “called” to the
most comfortable positions :
PETER’S LUCK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The worst-used man 1 ever knew
Was Peter Queen, of Sandy Blight.
Ilia wife, an indurated shrew,
Was on his heel from morn to night.
And if the old man’s tale was true,
Hung to his ear the whole night through.
1 asked: “Why did you marry Kate?”
lie sighed, and one big tear he shed.
“ 'Twas back in eighteen-fifty-eight,
She came to me be chance,” he said.
“The fact is at the mentioned date
1 won her in a rattle, mate.
“ ’Twas on the Fly at set of sun
I struck the house of Jimmy Bonn.
The cami> had rounded up for fun —
A raffle and a dance was on.
The tickets, 1 was told, would run
To half a quid, and.l took one.
“Kate was as tough as she is now,”
He sighed and groaned. -Said I: “Then why
D’ye take the lady on?” llis brow
lie scratched. “The truth is,l
AVas much taken in. 1 thought as how
The rattle was for just a cow!” •

THE experience of the great body of Hupmobile owners
backs up the widespread idea that motor-car economy
and the Hupmobile go hand in hand.
Real economy is not merely low operating cost, but low
maintenance costs —and there the Hupmobile leads. It
keeps out of the repair shop. It stays in commission, with
the fewest delays and lay-ups.
And that means quality in the car.
SO Hupmobile owners are satisfied. They expected
economy when they bought. They get what they'
expected.
And satisfied owners are conceded to be a pretty valuable
asset for any motor-car nianufacturer.
Our records prove that more than fifty per cent, of our yearly
output is sold to former Hupmobile owners who find our car
best for the cost.

Hupmobile Models: 5-seater, 7-seater, 2-seater, sedan, and 5-seater and 2-seater with detachable winter tops. Motor: Four-cylinder, 95 m/m bore, 140 m/m stroke (32in. x s Jin.).

lined. Fong wheel base (119 in. on 2- and 5-seater, 134 in. on 7-seater). Tyres 875 x 105 m/m or 34in. x 4in. on 2- and 5-seater, 920 x 120 m/m or 35in. x 4Jin. on 7-seater. Electric
Startin'*- and lighting; ventilating, rain vision screen; one-man hood; quick-acting side curtains; door curtain carriers; deep upholstery; speedometer; ammeter; robe rail, foot
rail and carpet in tonneau; non-skid tyres on rear; five demountable rims; tyre carrier, pump, jack and full set of tools. Magneto ignition, wire wheels, special colors, khaki
hood and seat covers at small additional cost over list price of car.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The short and simple story of what lias
happened during the year to the Common-
wealth cashbox:
A surplus of £ 3,000,000 \vas carried forward from
1916. Ordinary revenue (partly estimated) amounted
to £ 32,576,300. This shows £35,075,300 avail-
aide. Expenditure (also partly estimated) ran to
£35,448,414. The surplus to start again with is
thus £120,886.
Which means that the Commonwealth will
have to set about securing an additional
£2,57.‘>,114 to make good the shortage of
revenue (or in the alternative spend tlfat
much less), and five or six more millions for
interest. By the time that lot has been
dredged out of the taxpayers' pocket, the
public will begin to understand what
economy means.
E. .T. Holloway, president of the Central
Executive of the Victorian Political Labor
Council, has tried to. explain in a printed
statement why organised Labor opposes Con-
scription :—•
The Labor movement all the world over has been
opposed to Conscription. It feels that under it
civil law would Tx^jiupplanted. by military law, and
with the death of civil law all the ethics, morals
and liberty that civil law represents would cease to
exist.
That is quite true. Ask Belgium. It opposed
Conscription. Germany didn’t. So Bel-
gium’s civil law has been supplanted by
German military law, and all the ethics,
morals and liberty of Belgium have departed.
Is that what the Australian Labor move-
ment aims at?
WILLING TO BE INSTRUCTED.
OFFICER: “Two-up seems to me hardly the
right thing for the Holy Land. Couldn't
you think of some better way of passing
the time?”
TROOPER: “ We're stuck, sir; but if you could
lay us on ter it I'd be willin’ ter make
one ter risk a few bob on it.”
Premier Holman is telling London that
he is there to see about aeroplanes. He
means kites.
It is time we made up our minds about it
one way or the other. Either strikes are
lawful and the recognised thing or they are
not. At present the lawbreaker has all the
best of it; for he gets a bit both ways. This
is about the last thing the law can afford to
•tolerate. And things are getting worse
instead of better. Within the last few days
two big union amalgamations have been
effected. In one case 11 bodies have formed
the Mercantile Employees’ Union of Aus-
tralia, at the instance of the Storemen and
Packers; in the other the Broken Hill miners
have thrown in with an amalgamation cover-
ing practically all underground workers in
Australia, bar politicians. The latter body
is openly and frankly militant, and boasts
that it can lay up every big coal and metal
mine in Australia within 24 hours. Let us
get rid of the current humbug. If we
either will not or can not prevent strikes
and lock-outs, then let us stop pretending to.
That merely penalises the person, employer
or employee who trusts to the law.

( “Send the hulks to sea !’’ is the latest cry.
Hut the responsible Navigation Department
should be indicted if it permits so much as
one coffin-ship to leave an Australian port.
We have gone quite far enough already. The
other day the little Meeiriderrv started
round the coast after being two years idle
•at Melbourne. She soon sprang a leak and
had to put back. Next she waddled in to
I'alen in distress and short of coal. By this
time it looked as if her name should be
Mcandavy. _ Yet that uncertain traveller is
a mere infant compared with some of the
old stagers which they talk of combing out
of the backwaters.
Anapsthctie administered by the Annus: —
. The French found thrift extremely useful to them
helping; them to pay their war indemnity after
IS/1.. \X o do not contemplate paying war indemni-
ties, but while there is a war of unprecedented eost-
lniess being- waged we cannot afford to waste
anything at home.
If defeat is not averted, it will not he for
ns to say whether we shall pay indemnities
or not. The nation which exacted an un-
paralleled indemnity from. France after 1871
is the nation that will dictate terms to us
it we are beaten. The sooner, therefore, we
consider the pleasantness (or otherwise) of
paying a war indemnity, the better.

oraph says ( 14/6/Y7), “This was a natural

to the tricksters!
The war borrowings of the Commonwealth
havq amounted to £128.-816,814, and the
expenditure is given thus;—
1914- . .. £ 14,471,118
1915- . .. 37,423,568
1916- .. '51,207,434
Total (three years) .. £103,102,120
But the £01,207,484 does not include
£32,000,000 due to J. Bull for maintaining
Australian troops in the field, and now being
liquidated at the rate of £2,000,000 a month.
On the borrowings and debts incurred to
date the Commonwealth is astern to the
extent of the difference between £l2B 816 SI 4
and £185,102,120; yet judging by the way
money is being flung about one would reason-
ably think that John Forrest had just found
Ivalgoorlie and made the Federal Govern-
ment a present of it.
The Phewsion didn't worry about morals
or decency when it was snaring the soldier’s
vote any more than it did when it was out

The Hun's poison-gas is good stuff—if imita-
tion is any proof of it.
. Tlxe A'ic. People’s Party
proposes to move for the re-
duction of some of the existing
Protective duties. It has- an
idea that the farmer doesn’t
want any population in Aus-
tralia—that it will always be
best for him to have his
market 12.000 miles away.
In case of war it is a speci-
ally great advantage to have
only a foreign market. For
one tiling, it prevents the
farmer getting more than one-
third the world’s-market price
for his crop. If he got the
whole price he would probably
die of apoplexy. Also, the
market being remote, the
grain can’t be shifted in case
of war; and that gives the
mice a chance. They would
starve otherwise, poor things.
Altogether, tlmn. it seems a
most suitable time for the
People’s Party to talk to the
farmer of (Ik 1 advantages of
keeping people away from this
country.
Just as well to remember
that Germany never yet talked
“no indemnities or annexa-
tions.” The anti-conscrip-
tionist who says we would be
as well off under German rule
might think better of it if the
Gorman rule began with a thumping cash
levy on the conquered race.
Last, week, under the Federal Arbitration
law, the members .of the Glass Bottle
Makers’ Union were fined £5 apiece for
striking and continuing to strike. Com-
pare that with the treatment of the coal-
miners when they struck, and the only con-
clusion possible is that when up against a
poor, weak little crowd the law is a lion, but
a mouse when it faces a strong, militant
army of workers. In those circumstances
who pan wonder at the steady disappear-
ance of the little craft unions and the rapid
growth of the industrialist section V
The N. S. Wales Government which col-
lected a small super-tax on incomes two
years ago and increased it last year,
announces that the larger tax will be im-
posed again for the current year. 'A super-
tax is essentially a special impost to meet
special needs. But the N. S. Wales Govern-
ment has no special needs that are in any
sense legitimate. It pays no part of the cost
of the war. On the contrary, it benefits very
largely by the war; for manufacturing and
trading profits have been increased by the
expenditure of millions of war loan and the
purchase of millions of pounds’ worth of our
wool, etc., at war prices. In view of this,
bad reasonable economy been practised, the
State, should have been able, not. merely to
do without a super-tax, but even to reduce
the amount of the ordinary tax. Sneaked
through under cover of the war,, the super-
tax really pays in part for the gross extrava-
gance of the'date Labor Government and the
scarcely more unrestrained, extravagance of
the present Government. The other day it
was announced that a business-man of high
standing would be commissioned to report
on the public service. What is far more
important is that three business-men of high
standing should be commissioned to report
on the . whole financial position and recent
and present conduct of the State.
The Vic. Presbyterian Assembly has been
hearing something about the manner of life
of some members of the congregation. A
woman with eight children lets rooms: she
has 10 rooms and 31 tenants. In another
place a father, mother, son (14) and daugh-
ter (12) live in one room. The trouble with
these places is that they are not slums that
can be demolished by the municipal authority.
In most cases the people are immigrants
who have brought their bad habits to a
country where a high level of wages is
officially insisted upon for the express pur-
pose of making it unnecessary for people to
herd in this fashion. That throws an obli-
gation upon Health authorities to see that
they don’t do it; and it ought to be the duty
of Labor Departments to see that Health
Departments don’t neglect this job. For the
offence of these people is. not merely against
health: it is against the whole industrial
policy of the Commonwealth.
From a front-rank Australian doctor
working in London : —-
If only it were possible to leg'-bite on the subject
of war babies for the next few rears, what a boon
it would be—what a great thing for our country I
We lift our hands in horror it such a sane sugges-
tion; jet here in London v.e are shutting our eyes
to the fact that part of our manhood is being ruined
beyond possibility of repair by tin- great diseased
multitude of women from other nations, driven from
their own lands by a wise Govei mnent desirous of
keeping its army free iron' disease! God help Aus-
tralia if it palters with the necessity of detaining
and treating every returned man who has this
plague.
What happened last week in the matter
of Christian Benzing reflected little credit
ou anybody, llenzing was to be hanged for
a most abominable outrage, and the hanging
was fixed for Thursday morning. Half, an
hour before the time he was told that the
execution was postponed. Further inquiries
were made, and (lie hanging duly took place
on Saturday. This was the very refinement
of torture and it was all due to the mid-
night arrival of a deputation from the Trades
Hall at the Acting-Premier’s home with a
rumor that Benzing wasn’t sane enough to
be responsible for his actions. That the
deputation should have moved then without
more evidence than it had was had enough ;
that the Government should not have fully
satisfied itself on the point beforehand was
worse. The inevitable comment now is:
,r Why, .this Government will even hang a
man without being sure that lie was respon-
sible for his crime!” If it wanted to play
into the hands of its friends the I.WAY. it
couldn’t have behaved better.
The recent police canvass of persons
entitled to vote at Victorian elections dis-
closes the solemn fact that while metropoli-
tan electors increased by 47.000 since the
previous canvass was taken a few years ago.
country electors added only 5000 to their
number. Melbourne, in fact, now has
430,769 electors, while the rest of the State,
including Ballarat, Bendigo. Geelong, etc.,
shelters but 889,070! And the position will
grow steadily worse unless we make life less
unendurable for the man on the land and the
country dweller in general. At present we
are busy squeezing him dry to make things
pleasant for city parasites. And Govern-
ments are the principal instruments employed
in doing it.
Under the Premiership of Peacock, the
mining man. there seems to be always a
danger of Victoria suffering further devasta-
tion from the dredging fiends. .lust now they
are applying for a lease of hundreds of acres
near Wangaratta, and there is a loophole in
the Act, of course, through which the dis-
trusted Sludge Abatement Board can push a
BACK-OF-THE-LINES COURTESIES.

permission of the proposed outrage. Vic-
toria is not so rich in watercourses that
dredging ought to be permitted anywhere,
especially as the financial results are gener-

men were employed in producing £30,000 in

quarter of a million.
IT IS THE LEADERS WHO BETRAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We who have watched with hopeful eyes
To see our laud emerge in light,"
And stand in glory 'gainst the skies
To lead all other lands aright,
\\ hat have we seen but sudden fire,
And one man marking out the way,
Who seemed a moment to aspire,
Then trailed hfs banner in the mire?
it is the leaders who betray.
They come to us with shining brows,
Their hearts attuned to one true song,
Their tongues all eloquent to rouse
And sweep the lighting line along;
"llut ere the clearer course is traced,
In pride and dalliance they stray;
’I hey see soft luxury, and taste
Of sugared ease, and die abased,
It is the leaders who betray.
\Ve held them in our hearts a while;
We give them of our strength elate;
llut Circe hath a cunning smile,
Her eyes are dusk and passionate.
So, though the dawn flare rosily,
Comes not for us the radiant day.
A Judas must there ever he
For our fair Christ Democracy.
It is the leaders who betray!
VICTORIA.
E. DYSON.

COMSTOCK’S “DEAD-SHOT” WORM
PELLETS.
A SAFE, SURE, AND RELIABLE SPECIFIC FOR
THE ERADICATION OF WORMS IN CHILDREN
AND ADULTS.
The general symptoms of worms are a,
tendency to pick the nose and a sensation of
itching in the anus. Other symptoms are
nervous affections, such as fretfulness,- irri-
tation of temper, wakefulness or disposition
to excessive sleep, disturbed sleep, and grind-
ing the teeth, sudden starting out of sleep as
if from fright, dizziness, spasmodic move-
ments of the eyelids, dilated pupils, per-
verted vision, temporary blindness and deaf-
ness. General convulsions are not infre-
quent in children, and symptoms resembling
water in the head have been ascribed to
worms, and have ceased on their expulsion.
The long round or stomach worm is the
commonest kind of worm from which man-
kind suffers, and is of a light, transparent
color, resembling the ordinary ground worm,
except that the extremities are more sharply
pointed than the ground worm.
Young people are more subject to these
worms than middle-aged or elderly people.
They live in the bowels, and sometimes make
their way into the stomach and throat, caus-
ing sickness, vomiting and choking. Gener-
ally one or two would be the most a person
would be afflicted with, but sometimes a
much larger number is met with. The length
varies from four to eight inches, but young
ones an inch to two inches long are also
found. The first thing to do is to rid the
system of the worms, and Comstock s Dead
Shot” Worm Pellets, taken according to
directions, are an effective remedy for the
thorough eradication of the long round 01
stomach worm —the common enemy of the
child, the youth, the middle-aged and aged.
A light, nourishing diet, out-of-door exercise,
and, if possible, a change of air, will greatly
improve the general health.
Threadworms or pinworms are of very
common occurrence in children. 3 hey are
very small, from one-sixth of an inch to lialf-
an-ineli in length, and resemble cotton
thread—they are sometimes called seat
worms, and reside in the lower bowels. 3. he
chief symptom of this worm is severe local
itching, restlessnesp and irritability, and a
frequent desire to urinate, picking at the
nose;. loss of appetite and anaemia are also
observed. To get rid of the worms and their
eggs their nests also must be removed, and
that requires a specific remedy. Comstock’s
“Dead Shot” Worm Pellets, taken according
to directions, on an empty stomach, followed
by a dose of opening medicine, will succeed
where others fail.
Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets are
a purely vegetable medicinal preparation for
the eradication of worms in children and adults.
Prom chemists and storekeepers, 3s. Gd. per
packet, or Dost paid by The W. II. Comstock
Co., Ltd., 23 Lang St., Church Hill, Sydney.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The good money that has been fooled away
and the good horses that have been ruined by the
knocking-about most of the 2-year-olds get in N. S.
Wales ought to be measured up by the A.J.C. and
other racing clubs concerned. Many of these young
liqrses are raced week after week, and most of the
others every fortnight or so. The juveniles which
came on early in the Spring were kept hard at it
until they were played out. Then another crop
developed, and these are being kept busy until next
birthday arrives. Heaven knows hotv many will
survive and be of any use, but it is 100 to 1 that
a fair proportion are not going to be heard of except
among the “also ran” brigade. Lately this page
saw two or three of the earlier hard-worked lot. The
chaSe after the big money on offer for young ’uns
lias left them in a state of wreck. And it is easy
to pick out others running now that have been
galloped to a standstill. The idea that the rich
prizes hung up for 2-year-olds is good for breeders
is a delusion held only by those who cannot see
beyond .the end of their noses. That it is bad for
the Australian horse is beyond doubt. The business
badly wants an overhaul.
Warwick Faun punters collapsed in their
seats when Canzone rushed past the judge’s box in
advance of five other runners in the Flying on
Saturday. The reason for all this palpitation was
that Canzone had come to be regarded purely as a
stayer and not much chop at that, seeing that a
year had elapsed since his number was hoisted in
first place. As Canzone, an imported prad, was the
outsider of the small party, on Saturday, Abrahams
kilts flapped about in merriment as the public
disgustedly flung away their useless tickets. When
Canzone first arrived from the Cold Country he was
looked upon as a sprinter, and solidly backed two or
three times in six-furlong events at Rundwick. But
he may do better that way in his old age. Anyway,
at Mooreficld a couple of weeks ago he was close up
to the place-getters in a field of a dozen when the
end of a short journey was reached.
, * * * *' '
For some unknown reason there lias been
a boom in Sydney on Black Earl, and in the last
three starts he has been a solid favorite. This was
probably due to the nag’s forward running at
the last but one Warwick Farm meeting, when, after
being bumped about, he arrived close to a winner.
When produced at llandwick in novice class he was
made favorite, led into the straight, was caught, and
subsided like a hunted dog. Next time he appeared
at a near-country meeting, and the same thing
happened. On Saturday he was favorite for the
Warwick Farm Stakes, and never looked a winner.
Besides losing, the only thing Black Earl has so far
done happened at Menangle about a year ago, when
lie won a maiden event. There must have been
something wrong with the brute that day.
* * * tc-
It was up to “Jumbo” Barnett, one-time
owner of A.J.C. Derby winner Cetigne, to have a
change of luck ; so his colt Lambourne annexed the
Warwick Farm Stakes on Saturday, winning by
three lengths. Lambourne is by the defunct sire
Grafton, and prior to Saturday had been a poor in-
vestment —-be cost 600 guineas as a yearling. How-
ever, the people behind him deposited solid wads
with Ike at a nice price this time, and are no
doubt sanguine that the horse will win some more in
future. Lambourne is out of an imported mare,
Shuffle, and ran in the A.J.C. Derby won by Kilbov.
He was well up for a mile and a quarter, but then
took up a position at the tail. In the Novice Han-
dicap won by Sobrina at Randwick recently Lam-
bourne also decorated the wrong end.
* * * #
Punters have been pleased to hear that
Spurn is pot going to race again. For a year she
was under a cloud; since emerging she has proved
horribly expensive, having won only the Railway
Stakes at Rosehill in March. She is beautifully bred
and goes to the mysterious job of producing winners
with the o-reat advantage of not having been exces-
sively raced.
» * * *
“Five Bob” : My friend the small book-
maker complains of a racecourse joke of “Baron”
Brown’s. When my friend is trying to catch the silver
minnows, up comes the financial whale covered with
coal-mines, asks the price of a horse and pretends
to be disgusted when the small penciller respectfully
declines to bet in hundreds, pleading in extenuation
that he isn’t the great Moses Loudodds or the
immense Patrick Yellbag. Apparently even book-
makers have their secret sorrows.
* * * *
The Sydney pony Haut Special was asked
to give an imitation of a. Clydesdale at Morpeth
(N.S.W.) on Saturday. First he captured the hiv-
ing Handicap, three furlongs, with 14? t. 71b. in the
saddle. Later in the afternoon, with lewt. Sor.
14lb. aboard, he secured the Morpeth Handicap, also
three furlongs. The little beast of burden started at
6 to 4 on in the first dash and 5 to 4 on in the
second. It was a day of mighty tests. Three of the
alleged races were over three furlongs, and another
over one less. Other events were “bridle” races.
Incidentally, this misuse of dumb animals was called
“sport.”
“Creh Egdur” : Clapator’s three wins (B.
24/5/’l7) in one day at Mudgee (N.S.W.) has a
competitor in British Colors. At Cobar (N.S.W.)
on Easter Monday last B.C. won the opening (lOst.
91b.), Club Handicap (lOst. 121 b.) and Farewell
(list. 41b.). His best price through the day was
5 to 1 “on.” The other two races went to a local
crack, Euchre Billy, owiiig to the favorite bolting
round the course four times, thus giving Bill about
four miles start.
“Chal”: The spieler lmd a Greek in
hand. Prior to the last race the trusting Dago
handed over his quid for investment on River Prince,
which moke duly arrived a winner. The crook
assumed a despondent attitude. “Bad luck again,
he said, “another third.” Just then the placed
numbers flew up and the Greek became excitable.
“My horse win ! My horse win ! See —number tight.”
The spieler turned on him witheringly. “My Gawd,”
he said, “don’t you know that much 1 They’re the
scratchin’s for the next meetin’!”
“Fax” : It says much for the astuteness of
citizen John Wren, whose fortunes were founded by
a private tote and established by proprietary pony-
meetings, that he gets so much advertisement for his
benefactions in Melbourne. A little bit of roofing
shelter for recruiting orators, aguinst. the front of
the Town Hall, is labelled “Erected by John Wren,
•Esq.,” which is a fine ad. for- the money. Just now
John is figuring in connection with the latest of
St. Vincent Hospital devices for raising the wind.
The Hospital is selling shilling tickets which differ
from ordinary bits of numbered paper in having
miniature copies of famous putures printed thereon.
Wren promises £lOOO to the person holding a certain
selected miniature picture—in other words, a certain
numbered ticket —to be selected on the sweep sys-
tem]. And if half a million tickets be sold at a
shilling each he will give £5OOO to the holder of
the lucky number. The chances are that John
Wren, Esq., will get through with a donation of
£lOOO, of course. It is all in the way of chanty,
but it disreminds one of the philanthropy which
doesn’t let its left hand know what its right hand
doeth.
» * * *
“Musketoon” • Mow about adopting my
patent tote, which 1 have invented at "Forbes
(N.S.W.)? Get sufficient kerosene-tins and label
each with the name of a starter. The investor puts
his cash in the tin bearing the name of the horse
he fancies, and when the race is won, the money is
pooled and a dividend declared. The contraption is
simple und cheap, and can be installed for about
one (1) quid. I give every bush dub the right to
use this machine free of royalty. If the A.J.C. or
suburban clul s take it on 1 want to be put in charge.
P.S. —The A.J.C. should use 400 gal. tanks instead
of oil-cans, in order to accomrfiodate any defaulting
tellers who might arrive with a few thousand for a
single race.

“Billjimmy” : The Desert Column Spring
Race Meeting was held at Rafa a few days before the
Battle of Gaza. Great affair. There were three
brass and one pipe (Scotties’) bands in action; large
field in each event, 20 the smallest. Programme and
results:—•
Rafa Cun: Burrawang, 1; Khedive, 2; Piastre, 3.
Syrian Derby: El Arish, 1; Desert Chief, 2;
Bidgee, 3.
Anzac Steeplechase: Tarawera, 1; Abdul, 2;
Dryazell, 3.
Promised Land Handicap: Ginger Mick, 1 ;
Huglieselier, 2; The Prophet, 3.
Sinai Grand National: The Banjo, 1; Minesabeer,
2 ; Tipperary, 3.
Jerusalem Scurry: Y’emma, 1; Te Kooti, 2;
Clancy, 3.
There were several minor events as well. Austra-
lian mokes accounted for five races, English for two
and a Maorilander for one. Among riders and horses
were several that had faced the barrier at Rand-
wick. Some have since weighed in for the Eternity
Stakes. May the sands rest lightly on them!
* * * *
“Black Wattle”: Not all the baby bonuses
go into the jeweller’s till. A Victorian wife and
mother recently sued the local produce dealer for the
recovery of two racehorses which he had collared in
payment of an account contracted in her husband’s
name. The missus said that the boss never really
owned the nags, as they were purchased by her
with the proceeds of a wedding present and three
baby bonuses.
* * * *
That accomplished exponent of Bash, Mr.
Eddie McGoorty, emerged from retirement on Satur-
day and had a scrap at Sydney Stadium. The other
party was that hefty young Australian, Mr. Albert
Lloyd. As they are a pair of good, solid punchers
the crowd saw something worth while. McGoorty
owns too much meat these days to be as formidable
as he was in the long ago, but he is still a very
harmful American citizen. Probably he reckoned
that Lloyd, who had been tastefully trimmed by Dave
Smith a few weeks before, was easy. It turned out
that Lloyd was something else. He brought more
intelligence to bear on McGoorty than he did on
David, and hit with a greater emphasis. The Ameri-
can was never particularly good as an in-fighter, and
Lloyd frequently obliged him with what he didn’t
want. In return, McGoorty handed out heavy bom-
bardment to the body, and also made occasional
efforts to connect up with the old reliable swing, but
failed. There was no question about the result; not
only was the American heavily punished, but he was
clearly outpointed. Nevertheless, McGoorty took
Scctt s verdict for Lloyd very badly. He hasn't got
into the habit of losing well, though, no doubt, if
the winner had treated Smith as disrespectfully, the
verdict would have been easier to swallow.
Flyweights had the limelight at Brisbane
on Saturday. Mr. William Woods came up from
Sydney, and was presented with a ferocious punch
oil the- ncse by Mr. James Taylor. At times it looked
as if Willie’s boxing would make a difference, but
the little Bananalander so ardently prosecuted the
attack that Willie’s boxing didn’t amount to a row
of pins. The referee did the visitor a favor in the
thirteenth round by postponing the slaughter. Jimmy
Taylor begins to look as if he is flyweight champion
because he deserves to be.
» * * *
A seldom-met-witli record got the k.o. at
Hobart (Tas.) the other day when Davey and Berris-
ford gQt through an 18in. log' with double-handed
saw in 22 4-ssec. L. Mclntosh and E. Higgins, of
Hornsby (N.S.W.), did the previous best at Sydney
Show in 1915; and in winning the N. S. Wales
championship a couple of months ago E. Higgins
and E. Harrington pushed the saw through as much
wood in 25Asec.

Last time it was the champion boxer who
was dodging; this time it is the billiardist —-Mel-
bourne Inman, champion of the world. Melb. ap-
pealed last week against military service to an
English Board, and the chairman, in adjourning the
case for further medical evidence, said nastily, “Don’t
talk to me of international champions 1”
* •» * *
The end of N. S. Wales Rugby League’s
first round on Saturday left Balmain without defeat
in seven matches. In points Balmain has piled up
115 against 19; and only three times has an enemy
crossed its border. At present, therefore, Balmain
seems on the way to add another to the premiership
successes of the two previous seasons, in the last
two matches, against Glebe and South Sydney, all
the points for Balmain (18) were scored by R.
Miller, a hustling, broad-shouldered forward with a
(lever boot.
» * * *
“The ’limp”: The Premiership Competi-
tion Shield presented by Norwood (S.A.) Club to
the N.S.W. Australian Game League to go to any
team winning it three times (not necessarily in
succession) lias been bagged twice by the Sydney
Club. Paddington has done ditto, and other clubs
have each won once. At the last meeting of the
League, on a motion that the Shield be not further
played for until after the war, the Sydney Club’s
delegates voted affirmatively. Paddington voted in
the negative, and the division tied. Then the cast-
ing vote of the president decided that the competi-
tion should go on. Sydney Club has many players
at the Front, including several who enlisted since
last season’s competition. Paddington has not lost
any men (by enlisting) since last season. Further,
the team which presented the Shield, the Norwoods
—in fact, all of the teams in S. Aus. —are out of
competition football, till after the war. In deciding
to play on as usual, the Australian Game in Sydney
seems determined to commit suicide. .You can’t
have much respect for a crowd which persists in run-
ning a competition which it is really more disgraceful
to win than to lose.
* * * *
Rnbbo (N.S.W.) had a pleasant afternoon
recentlv, when two football matches yielded a
casualty-list of thirteen.
* * * *
H. D. L. Woods, dead at Sydney, was a
fine sportsman. He pulled on a jersey in the old
Wallaroo Rugby team —which included at times
Senator “Jupp” Gardiner, Stan Wickham and Sam
Spragg—and was a sterling forward. Both at cricket
and golf Woods did useful toil for Manlv (Sydney),
and as a delegate to N.S.W. Cricket Association and
in other ways gave up many spare hours to athletics.
11.D.L. was a brother of Sammy, who, while at
Cambridge, played ewht matches with Percv McDon-
nell's 1888 Australian XI. Two years later he took
all 10 wickets for 69 in a match Cambridge v. C. I.
Thornton’s XI.
* * * *
Having tried in vain to enter the artil-
lery, George W. Morris, the youngster who is Vic-
toria’s champion distance swimmer, has enlisted in
the infantry, in order to join his brother at the
Front. George W. has started in 52 races, and, has
been unplaced only nine times. His record includes
first in mile arid half-mile championships, first in the
earliest three-mile race down the Yarra, apd second-
fastest time on each subsequent occasion that this
event was swum. He is sec. of Albert Park Swim-
ming Club.
* * * *
Lieut.-Col. Lachlan Wilson, recently deco-
rated with the French War Cross, is growing old in
the war business. He exchanged cards with Kruger
in S'Africa; ard since August, 1914, he has been
happiest where the fighting was thickest. Just on a
quarter of a century ago, in Brisbane (Q.),.he pulled
an oar with the Commercial Club, and learned foot-
ball at the Grammar School. Brother “Bob” Wilson
(also a great athlete) is warming Fritz, up with the
artillery.
* * * *
Lpnce-Cornoral T. J\ B. Kenny, lately
awarded the V.C., is an Australian bantam of
6ft. 2in. He used to lead Christian Brothers’ College,
Waverley, at cricket and Rugby’.
* * * *
Sergeant J. W. Whittle, the latest Tas-
manian V.C., is a hefty sport. He weighs about
14st., mostly muscle, and before he started for
France, via Gallipoli, he won the amateur heavy-
weight boxing championship of the Speck. He still
holds it, for they don’t do any amateur boxing down
there now.

R. G. Piffgott (East Sydney) made a haul
in the challenge match against St. Ignatius on
Saturday, winning: the 100yds. (10 4-ssec.), 220yds.
(2545ec.), and 90yds. hurdles (Hisec.).. He also
finished second in the 880vds. (2min. 26 l-ssec.).
Piggott arrived first in the East Sydney club cham-
pionsliip sprints this season; and in last years
440vds. Dunn chamniorshio filled second place to
J. J. Walshe (now in khaki) in 53 3-ssec. But,
despite Piggott, St. Ignatius won the challenge with
40 noints against East Sydney’s 32. The rest were
as slow as snails.
When Les Darcy's body is brought back to
Australia it will cot be the first time an athlete s
remains have been carried from oversea for burial in
his home land. About 15 years ago Charlie Cavili,
second eldest of the world-famed family of swimmers,
was drowned at Stockton (U.S.A.) while performing
an under-water feat he had successfully accomplished
many times previously. Cavili in this stunt had
been accustomed to use a bell which had previously
been lowered into the water charged with oxygen,
lie used to retreat into this for several minutes
while the watchers above marvelled at his remarkable
lung-power. On the last day Cavili stayed in the
bell too long, became exhausted and was asphyxiated.
His body was brought back to Sydney.

The Lamington Golf Cup (Bananaland),
competed for in a knock-out tournament, was won
this year by Bartley Fahey, a member of the Ancient
House, and P. .1. Symes. The last stroke at the
last hole settled it. The Hon. Bartley is a mere
fledgling of SI, who simply won't have it that the
Bananaland climate is unfit for white men. _
“Me-Tax”: My two young Australians (I
live at Casino, N.S.W.) went forth with one .22-
calibre miniature rifle between them. The resulting
bag was: One black duck, two black moor-hens, three
pigeons, one heron (or blue crane), 12 rosella parrots
and a crow (scalp worth sixpence). Total victims,
20; cartridges used, 55. Incidentally, ever tried
blue crane as a table bird? Roasted, it would pass
for young chicken.
* * * *
“Merrigal” : Forties (N.S.W.) has a freak
in 17-year-old “Curly” Brewer, who prefers a rifle
to a shot-gun when potting feathered game. Using
a 22-calibre Winchester, loaded with its smallest
pellet, he top-scored at a recent duck-shooting excur-
sion, securing several of his birds when they were
well on the wing. Later I saw him with the same
weapon stop 17 out of 20 bronzewing pigeons, distant
from 00 to 90 paces, and be didn’t seem to think
much of the performance. His elder brother is snip-
ing Germans.
* * * *
“Peg-Log”: I've adorned the two-up
schools around Manly mentioned by “W.J.H.” (B.
7/0/'l7), but thev were tame affairs compared with
the games that flourished on the site of the Little
Theatre, Sydney, a few years ago. Hands up the
mob that remembers the old bloque who sat at the
side of the main ring, with his trousers tucked up
to his knees, and reckoned himself the mascot of
anyone who happened to be winning a few pounds !
He used to present his bill this way: “ 'Ow arc yeh,
Brownie?” “Never better in me life.” “I'm not
arskin’ about y’r bleedin’ ’ealfh I ’Ow about my
rake-off?” It was an understood thing that the
old finger should get something out of every m&n’s
winnings. They were gentlemen in that school. A
player could knock off when he liked, even if he
were winning 50 or 70 pounds. In that event,
however, no one else was allowed to leave the joint
till 10 minutes had passed. This gave the luck-
child time to tear up Castlereagh-street for a tram
before the toreadors swooped out.
“Trot”: Anyone ever hear of an individual
being charged with cruelty to a fish? Well, it hap-
pened not long since at Colombo, where three of
the population were fined. They had conveyed a
7cwt. sunfish to the markets in a live condition,
bleeding copiously. Their yarn was that as they
did not have with them large enough cutlery to slay
the catch, there was nothing for it but to convey
the animal to the cutlery. As the beak wouldn’t
accept this as an excuse, it seems as if those who
go fishing for half-ton cod in the Lachlan had better
arm themselves with a cutlass in case of emergency.

Why hook Old?
You need not use hair dye to conceal grey hair.
Grey hair adds years to anyone’s appear-
ance. The objections to dyeing the hair are
too numerous and too obvious to mention.
Few people are aware that a simple, old-
fashioned recipe, that can he made up at
home, will quickly turn grey hair back to a
natural color without injuring the hair in
any way. One has only to get two ounces
of concentrate of tammalite from the chemist
and mix it with three ounces of bay rum.
With a small sponge apply the lotion to the
hair for a few nights and the desired change
will have been neatly accomplished. This
simple formula has given great satisfac-
tion for generations to those in possession of
it. —A pvt. [3

COSMETICS can never really help a poor
complexion ; often they are positively
harmful. The sensible, rational way is to
actually remove the thin veil of stifling,
half-dead scarf skin from the face, and give
the fresh, vigorous and beautiful young skin
underneath a chance to show itself and to
breathe.
This is best done in a very simple way,
b.y merely applying mercolised wax at night,
like cold cream, and washing it off in the
morning. It absorbs- the disfiguring cuticle
gradually and. harmlessly, leaving a brilliant
natural complexion. Of course, this also
takes with it all such facial blemishes as
red blotches, tan, moth patches, sallowness,
liver spots, etc. As a freckle remover and
general complexion beautifier this old-
fashioned remedy is unequalled.

BLACKHEADS, oily skins and enlarged
pores usually go together, but can be
nstantly corrected by a unique new process.
tablet of stymol, obtained from the chem-
st’s is dropped in a tumbler of hot water,

Secrets of Beauty
The Cream of Modern Beauty Advice
By “MIMOSA.”
When the effervescence has subsided the face
is bathed with the stymol-charged water and

BEAUTIFUL hair adds immensely to
the personal magnetism 'of both men
and women. Actresses and smart women
are ever on the look-out for any harmless
thing that will increase the natural beauty
of their hair. The latest method is to use
pure stallax as a shampoo on account of
the peculiarly glossy, fluffy and wavy effect
which it leaves. As stallax has never been
used much for this purpose it comes to the
chemist only in sealed original packages,
enough for twenty-five or thirty shampoos.
A teaspoonful of the fragrant stallax gran-
ules, dissolved in a cup of hot water, is more
than sufficient for each shampoo. It is very
beneficial and stimulating to the hair, apart
from its beautifying effect.
IT is astonishing the number of -women
who suffer from the humiliating afflic-
tion of superfluous hair, and I am sorry to
say that through ignorance or wrong treat-
ment the majority of them have made matters
considerably worse by causing the hairs to
grow more quickly and stronger than before.
It only requires a little thought to realise
the folly of it all. Removing the surface
growth for a few days may give temporary
relief, but in order to obtain permanent
results the hair roots must be killed. Caustic
depilatories are worse than useless, and on
no account should the hairs be pulled out.
Apart from the electric needle, which is a
costly and painful process, any chemist will
tell you that the best home treatment is
pure powdered pheminol, which has a de-
vitalising effect upon the hair roots, in addi-
tion to which it removes all trace of the
objectionable hair in a few seconds, and the
recommended treatment gradually, but
surely, dissolves out the hair follicles, which
is the only method of permanently ridding
oneself of this terrible disfigurement. [6

An old soldier who may have met Private
Stanley Ortheris in the flesh—for both
fought at Ahmed liheyl—is Captain W. N.
Holmes, now ending his days at Scottsdale
(Tas.). lie has named the place Dilkhoosha
(“Heart’s Delight”), after one of the palaces
so fiercely fought for in the Indian Mutiny.
The old boy, who is 85, retired in 1880 on a
pension, augmented by a grant for distin-
guished services. Ho was 30 year's with the
King’s Royal Rifles; Kaffir War, 1851-3
(medal) ; Mutiny, 1858 (medal) ; China,
1860 (medal with two clasps) ; Afghanistan,
1878-80 (medal with clasp, and bronze
star). He helped to drive the mutinous
Sepoys out of the .Tudispore jungles; saw the
fall of the Taku forts and Pekin, and the
burning of the Chinese Emperor’s palace;
marched with Roberts from Kabul to Kanda-
har. and witnessed the surrender of the
stronghold. He was a fellow officer with
Lord Gormanston, who renewed the old com-
radeship when he was Governor of Tas. 20
years ago.
“Chink” takes off his hat: —
A gilt-edged family history closed when .T. 0.
Simson passed out'at Claremont (W.A.) a few days
ago, at 75. His father was the pioneer squatter of
the Maryborough (Vic.) district, which in the
beginning figured as Sirnson’s Ranges. He and two
brothers came from the Monaro and spread their
mantle over a wide area. One of the brothers was
drowned while trying to save an employee from the
flooded Bet Bet Creek ; Donald Campbell Simson died
soon after; and his two sons, John Coghill Campbell
and David Dalgety, were sent to Europe to “finish
off” by their uncle, Hector Norman Simson. The
boys loomed large in Vic., society when they re-
turned, more so as .T.C. brought with him as his
wife the Baronne de Reibeld, daughter of a Bavarian
baron. In the early ’fiO’s the passing of the two
big, handsome lads made Melbourne crane its neck.
But their ideas of living and entertaining, together
with their attachment to the turf, started them on
the way downhill. The slump had well begun when,
in ’74, the Baroness Marie died at the age of 34,
as the result of an accident with her four-in-hand
when from races at Majorca. The star
of the Simsons ~set after that. D.D. drifted to Mel-
bourne, and finished up 10 years ago as a labor agent
m a small wav at Kirk's bazaar; J.C. married again,
pottered about arguing with Maryborough trades-
people for a while, and finally went West, where he
lived from, hand to mouth until the end came in a
retreat for the aged. . Bjit always he was the same
in spirft the same’ lofty. Simson who scattered
sovereigns from the'. Mclvor .Hotel balcony, followed
bis packs of hounds 1 over the countryside, and backed
his string of horses.
Captain Norman Brearley. D. 5.0., M.C.,
who made a name in the air above France, is
back in Perth on leave for six months with
a pair of bullet-punctured lungs. The Huns
nearly finished him,last time he interviewed
them.. He fell witlf 1 his vehicle into a shell-
hole in No Man’s Land, where he lay
wounded for hours. He is voting and very
modest, and before the war was with Hoskins
and Co., engineers. Now he gets special
compensation in his engagement to a daugh-
ter of Sydney Stubbs, M.L.A., erstwhile
Mayor of Perth,
Some loquacious patriots have lopsided
ideas of loyalty and duty. Ruth, a .Mel-
bourne Baptist parson, who never tires of
saving the Empire by abusing the Labor
party,, was asked by the Director-General of
Recruiting to address a meeting." He replied
that he was in favor of Conscription, pro-
ceeded to remark that “racing is still per-
mitted to make wastrels of men of military
age and fitness, Stadium fights are' continued
and disloyalty fostered.” and ended by re-
fusing the request. Whatever may be urged
against the voluntary system, it is the ofily
method of raising troops in Australia at
present, and will continue to be until after
another vote of the people. Men like Tudor
and Maloney—who oppose Conscription—are
always ready to address recruiting meetings
(although even this wins them the' dislike of
some of their supporters) ; yet they are con-
tinually being accused of “disloyalty.” while
men of the Ruth type, who adopt the atti-
tude that the war must be won only by the
methods they favor, have their partisan
blither featured approvingly by the “loyal-
ist” press. ' .

“Fryisan”: Re “Hussar’s” statement (B.
31/5/T7) that his father, known as Muktah
Pasha, was the originator of the jinriksha
in Japan. In 1884 I travelled from Yoko-
hama to British Columbia with the Rev.
Gcble, an American missionary, who in-
formed me that lie introduced the vehicle to
Japan, as his wife was an invalid, and there
were no other means of conveyance.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I'm four feet high,' ami soon must be
Four feet across. There is no place
In all this world for me.
I cannot take a good man’s knee
With any kind of dignity.
I have no shape, no comely grace.
A dame I had. We're sundered far.
Increasing ounces mar
My hopes. We utter strangers are
Since I outgrew his motor-car !
Caught out last eve in heavy rain,
I heard a jesting wretch who said:,
“Hi, Miss, that gamp won’t do.
It is no fit. It pinches you!”
But keener was the biting pain
When I was nursing William’s head
At Auntie’s dance. He reached a kiss;
And then to sear iny bliss
I heard: “Call up another, Miss.
’Twill take two men to cuddle this!”
A butt and scoff, a heart I own
As big and tender as my check.
In secret 1 adore
Of handsome men at least a score;
But never one a trace has shown
Of love for me. E’en if he seek
A better half, no single gent.
Will show himself content
To take me, though I represent
Not half but eighty-five per cent.!
VICTORIA.
F. A.

IMPORTANT.—LOOK TO YOUR TEETH.
There are no teeth like those furnished by Nature. For service, comfort and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be compared with a natural set.
. Sensible people will accordingly try to preserve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap and water are useful, but somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttlefish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifically prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free 1/3. Free sample posted. ROCKE. TOMPSITT & CO., Melbourne.

“Caroline”: Yon can say what you like
about Japs, but—well, how about this? _ I
lived for some time in a Westralian hotel
where a Jap cook was employed, and a very
good cook he was. One dinner-time I Was
told that he had been “fired.” Why? “Well,
y’ know,” explained the waitress, “water is
half a crown a hundred here and Surni
wouldn’t wash up in the'water the baby had
its bath in!”
“J. 8.” on the newest coronet: —
Waldorf Astor is one of the Birthday viscounts.
When lie showed sig'ns, 20 years ago, of settling in
England, American papers spelt it “Walled
orf,” to suggest the difficulties he was going to strike
while pushing into English society. However, he
and his M.P. son have got in with both feet. The
Astors bought the Pali, Mall Gazette and the
Observer (the leading London Sunday paper) and
turned J. L. Garvin, loose on them 10 years back
to preach Protection, prophesy a German attack on
England, and advocate Conscription. It was a better
policy for England than most Englishmen were advo-
cating, anyway. Young Astor, M.P., was ever ready
to accommodate philanthropic meetings at his house.
I was onee turned on to “secretary” a committee
of this kind, and while two footmen were placing
blotting-pads and fixings around the library table,
previous to the first meeting, there floated in a ray
of loveliness, gowned and hatted as angel girls might
hope to be hereafter. She chatted about the par-
ticular bit of human effort this committee was on
to, told the footmen we’d want tea, and vanished.
This was young Astor’s missus, who was a hand-
some widow in Baltimore, with rafts of money,
when he met her. Before he became active in war
work, Astor, minor, now a major, was an authority
on tuberculosis and an enthusiast for establishing
sanatoria for treating the poor.


“Window Dressing'."
WE had known for quite a long while that we were showing a clean pair of heels
in the matter of bonuses to our old friend the Australian Mutual I rovident
Society. But we never could get definite enough proof.
On the suggestion of the late General Manager, Mr. Richard Teeee (the ( it,\
Mutual wishes Mr. Teece every happiness in his well-earned retirement), in July. 191 _.
it was proposed to institute for a period of five years a . comparison of our respective
bonuses.
Apparently the results of the first year did not please Mr. Teece. because he issued
a leaflet entitled, “True Comparisons of Bonuses.”
Therein Mr. Teece let himself go as follows:
“A comparison restricted to the bonuses allotted during the first year or two only
of the existence of a policy is a device in some offices in an endeavor to attract busi-
ness.... Window-dressing is a well-known business practice for the purpose of attracting
the indiscriminate buyer....
“We might reasonably ask the question whether the first bonus in the case of the
City Mutual is not of use primarily for advertising purposes.”
Thus spoke the wise and sapient Mr. Teece over three years ago.
Well, we have been waiting for three long years to have a look at the goods in
the cases inside. We have been endeavoring during that period to get some authoritative
statement of the A.M.P. bonuses all round.
Now and again we did get an occasional peep at the bonus certificate of some
policy-holder, but it was always with fear and trembling it was shown us. a finger to
the lips, a half-scared look over the shoulder and a significant “Hush! Hush!”
But everything comes to him who knows how to wait.
In the 68th Annual Report of the A.M.P., submitted to the members a few days
ago, there will be found an authentic and official statement of the bonuses payable for
1917.
We print them below. Underneath are the City Mutual Bonuses corresponding in
every particular. Let that be thoroughly understood.
Now, we have no feeling for our esteemed old contemporary other than a friendly
one, but this pagan worship of 40 years, this A.M.P. idolatry, must cease. Life
Assurance and the A.M.P. Society have been practically synonymous terms much too
long.
Talk about “Britain and the Bible,” they are not in it with “Australia and the
Why, we have known old friends come to us for a loan, or for a position for their
relatives, or for some other favor, and when we have casually suggested the wisdom of a
life policy for the protection of their family they have enthusiastically applauded our
advice in something like the following terms: —
“By Jove! That’s good advice. I ought to have had a policy on my life long ago.
I shall attend to the matter at once. I will see the A.M.P. Agent to-morrow morning.”
Well, this sort of thing must stop. The Australian public is not more stupid than
it is given credit for, and it will begin to learn slowly but surely that there is another
pebble on the beach. Not as big a pebble, perhaps, but it is never the size of the
pebble that counts —it is the gold in it.
The City Mutual has absolutely safely invested funds and reserves of over a million
and a quarter sterling, which, goodness knows, are ample enough security in all con-
science for a policy of £5OOO, the present limit of the Society, and, when it is fully realised
that the City Mutual issues the most liberal policy in the world and gives the largest
bonuses of any Life Office on earth, the Society will no doubt get a fair share of the
best class of Life Assurance in Australia.

IN BANANA LAND
Yeates, who was defeated by Jim Page for
Maranoa, pushed into some out-of-the-way
places when electioneering in that vast area.
In a couple of the fastnesses they hadn’t
seen a political candidate for well over 10
years. This will make a lot of people sad.
For 10 years there has been a refuge for
the politically-weary; and the politically-
weary didn’t know it!
Joseph Daniels, of Swan Hill, missed* the
century by only 10 months. Born in Bristol
(Eng.) he migrated to Australia in ’54 and
set up in business in Newcastle. Then,
weary of town life, he plunged into farming
with his sons at Pittsworth in his 64th year.
The effects of a fall a few months ago did
what the awful climate of the North hadn’t
been able to accomplish.
“R.” ; “F. 8.” (B. 14/6/T7) makes the
statement that the Queensland Government
has bought stations on debentures and then
put the proceeds of cattle sales into the
general revenue. This would entitle any
Treasurer to six months hard. The facts,
however, are that trust -funds are debited
with purchases and credited with the results
of sales, and the consolidated revenue
doesn’t know anything about either station
or cattle.
Next Saturday at Albion Talk, Brisbane.
1000 women dressed in black, all of them
with relatives at the Front, will “line the
approaches to the racecourse and form a
perfectly silent guard of dishonor for race-
goers to pass between, while 20 drums will
beat a steady tattoo.” And on their
first day off the men who go to the
races will line up at the tea-shop and
the millinery counter and the fur depot, and
provide the same women, or a majority of
them, with the same kind of guard. These
are great times to “compound for sins we
are inclined to by damning those we have
no mind to.”
"8.J.5.”: Strawberry gets the champion
ribbon, and whenT go to the cattle sale I pay
through the nose for Strawberry’s calf. I
buy on the ribbon, but wake up to find that
a ribbon doesn’t produce butter-fat. 1 forget
to curse the Show Society for the Queens-
land system of judging, and blame the old
cow. Why does the Government not
jump with hobnailed boots on those subsi-
dised societies? No stallion which cannot
I'hoduce a certificate of soundness can be
awarded a prize, yet there are no restric-
tion's on dairy cattle. The Agricultural
Department, if it is not asleep, should cry a
halt to cocky. Every show should be com-
FROM A MERE TRADESMAN. TOO!
CECIL: “Note, Chumley, I call that personal!"
pelled to judge the dairy cattle not alone on
handsome looks but on ability at the
bucket and physical soundness. For the
male exhibits, the records of their progeny
could be produced to prove their worth, or
if they are too young the records of the
dam. Australia has got to splash itself with
a bit of scientific breeding, and not blunder
along the high road to national poverty. The
remedy is herd-testing, and until that is in-
stituted throughout the length and breadth
of the State we will go on producing stock
that will not pay for its feed. The business
must be tackled seriously. And that means
completely changing our methods. We’re the
greatest playing nation in the world. We
play at everything, from politics down to
running a hen ranch.
“8..T.5.” : “Bumble’s” explanation (B.
7/6/T.7) of Bananaland’s bad roads is not
complete. Many shires do not employ even
an overseer, the councillors reckoning it their
privilege and duty to control all works. When
the jobs are carried out by contract and
ratepayers are the contractors, the system
is at its worst. A ratepayer secures a con-
tract, say, to form and gravel part of a road.
When lie is finished the supervising coun-
cillor measures the work, and, on the prin-
ciple that because the job looks all right on
top everything must be O.K. underneath,
passes it. If the councillor is too particular
and makes critical remarks he can depend
on hearing about it next time the ballot-box
is brought into use.
Dead at 84, John Arthur Macartney, of
Newstead. Ilfracombe, a figure of long stand-
ing in tlie Queensland pastoral world. De-
ceased was the eldest son of the late Hussey
Burgh Macartney, first Dean of Melbourne.
Associate to Justice Barry for a few years,
he gave that up to run Waverley station,
cut from Rockhampton. Later, be ex-
plored virgin country all over the State, ex-
tending his travels westward to the Victoria
River, northward to the Arafura, and round
to the Gulf. All these pilgrimages he made
on horsebaek; and he frequently rode into
Rockhampton from Waverley, 129 miles, one
day. and back the next. From first to last
the tireless old man had been owner or part-
owner of 33 different stations.
The Historical Society of Queensland is
spending much time just now gazing on a
supposed portrait of John Kent, commis-
sariat officer at Moreton Bay when Brisbane
was in swaddling clothes. Kent is shown in
a swanking outfit supposed to be that of a
“Deputy-Assistant Commissary General,” a
job probably analogous to that of Govern-
ment Resident of Brisbane. Later, he edited
the North Australian (since defunct),
published in Ipswich.
“Belladonna” : Parson F. de M. Tubman,
now in France with Billjim. was preaching
in Mackay when he noticed a couple canood-
ling in a back pew. After finishing he
announced that “if the two people who had
that night been turning God’s Holy Ground
into a park” did not apologise to him after
service he would announce their names in
church next Sunday. Tubman then retired
to the vestry, and a dozen of his flock
poured in to apologise!
Come North!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the bleak south wind comes blowing from tlie
icefields at the Pole.
And the huddled streets of Hobart feel the blast;
When the sound of dead leaves falling easts a gloom
upon the soul,
Do not let yourself believe that summer’s past.
For on lowland and on highland,
From the Tweed to Thursday Island,
'ijhe.v are saying that good weather’s come at last.
When the Melbourne lights are hidden in a mist ot
chilly rain,
And the leaden Straits are whipped to wrack and
foam,
\\ hen overcoats and mufflers are in fashion once
again,
Remember you’ve a continent for home!
For the winter never reaches
To the white Caloundra beaches
Where the long Pacific rollers curve and comb.
When Sydney skies are leaden and winter tires
are lit,
And the Manly boats slink homeward through the
grey,
Suburban folk in Brisbane in their sunhy gardens
sit,
And the white-winged yachts skim over Moreton
Bay,
In the pleasant autumn weather
Spring and harvest go together, ■ . ''
And autumn lasts till summer-time, they say.
Tlie crowded boats crawl northward through the
sparkling tropic spas,
Where tlie phosphorescent fish like opals glow.
And tlie rover finds a playground where the water
will not freeze,
And the gales from tlie Antarctic never blow.
Dreaming on the wide verandah
Of the Barron at Kuranda
Or tlie beauty of tlie Atherton plateau.
The oranges are ripening in the mellow sun of .Time,
'Through the scented groves at night the fireflies
sail. '
And tlie shop-gills of Rockhampton shoot the breakers
at Yeppoon,
And they’re harvesting the cane at Innisfail.
The loaded trains go rushing
To the little mills for crushing,
And you cast away molasses by the pail.
There are some who love the winter-time and never
feel the cold,
And they’re happy when the sun has ceased to
shine,
So the walrus and the Polar bear by Arctic regions
hold,
And the Eskimo thinks furs and blubber fine.
Well, the cobbler to his leather,
But give me sunny weather
And a loaf beneath a mango-tree for mine.
QUEENSLAND.
VANCE PALMER.

The Brisbane Operatic Players, with
Leonard Francis threatening tlie orchestra
with Ills baton and Mrs. Arthur Iluntei
“producing,” gave “Paul Jones an an mg
on Friday night, and will keep it up for a

Miss Veness, eldest daughter of A. A.
Veness. of Glen Innes, has been booked by
the eldest son of the McDougalls, of Cooyar
station. The wedding is fixed for July 18.
Rita Martin, a popular Toowoomba girl,
wears the usual diamond ring. The man is
Major \V. G. Forde, of the A.A.M.C. He
hails from Cloyne, Ireland.
The Wattle Day committee has decided to
work with the French Red Cross people on
July IS, and have a day of its own later on.
Alderman and Mrs. Campbell (Mayor and
Mayoress of Hamilton) had a silver cradle
thrust on them during the week by the mem-
bers of the Council. A small new daughter
supplied the excuse.
“Rosna” throws a line from London: —
Mrs. Fenwick, wife of a one-time squatting
Queenslander, has gone into the wilds of Piccadilly
and borrowed a house for Lieut. Roo and Capt. Corn-
stalk. The residence had one of its rooms decorated
at a cost of £2OOO a yard or thereabouts; but the
quaint owner seems to imagine we are a civilised
people, for he has lent it to Mrs. Fenwick and the
boys free of charge. It is now a club for Austral-
asian officers, with fairy-godmother Fenwick to oil
the wheels,
A suggestion for the establishment of re-
search fellowships at Queensland Uni. is
being considered, and if the necessary £BOOO
is forthcoming three will be created. One
will be for the blowfly pest in sheep, another
for the cattle tick and redwater, and a third
for the banana borer. Later, when this State
becomes very affluent, a Chair .of Economic
Biology will be founded.
Lieut. Roy Berry is the third member of
the household of W. Iv. Berry, of Sherwood,
to pay the Big Price. His brother and a
near friend who resided with the family
“went West” some time ago.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.


Ileolin
Better than Leather
Xeolin Soles wear much longer than leather
soles. How much longer?
That depends somewhat on the sort of leather
Xeolin Soles are called upon to compete
with.
They will outwear the .very best of leather.
So scarce is all leather now, and so many are the demands
on the very best, that few of us get really good leather
soles.
That is one reason why Xeolin Soles have proven such a
godsend.
They give longer service than the best of leather soles—*
and they are always the same.
There is only one quality of Xeolin.
Wherever you see that word Xeolin stamped on the sole—and be sure that you
do see it —no matter whether the shoe be high or moderate in price, you re
getting the same high grade of sole.
A lower-priced shoe does not mean a lower quality of Xeolin. A higher-priced
shoe does not mean a higher quality of Xeolin.
You’ll find Xeolin Soles on shoes of several grades and many prices.
And the shoe is always a better shoe because of the Xeolin Soles.
Besides, Xeolin is waterproof, which the best of leather is not.
Shoes with Xeolin Soles do not need to be broken in—they are yielding and
flexible from the very first. And Xeolin Soles are good for weak foot
muscles—they strengthen, by exercising them.
For youngsters, Xeolin Soles are not merely desirable —they are indispensable.
The shoe bill lessens automatically the day that Xeolin Soles come into the
home.
1
I
• V
The public demand calls for a bigger and bigger quantity of Yeolin Soles every
day. The people in Australia who tried it first are beginning to talk—
and the proof of the sole is in its wonderful wearing capability.
Meanwhile, your shoe stores and repair shops have them —though every new
supply is quickly exhausted.
You need have no hesitancy in asking your retailer for Yeolin. Practically
every shoeman in the land gladly recommends it.
If lie is out of stock just now, you will invariably find that his later-coming
shoe styles will be Neolin equipped.

bears the Registered Name

“Fair Cow”: All the bulls don't come
from Ireland. A friend wlio has a bachelor
flat fronting a dusty city street is also boss
of a North of England house-steward. As
the" man was tidying up the other morning
the boss looked up at the open ventilator
and remarked: “By Jove, it's dusty here.”
"Aye.” said the North of England sagely,
following the boss’s eye, “if aught goes past
it c-ooms in.”
Walter Kiugsmill. Chairman of Commit-
tees of Groperland’s Leg. Council, has goite
to the Malay States to collect tropical olants
and animals for the Zoo. lie did good work
as honorary director of the Gardens while
Ernest I.e Soeuf was away on active service.
Kingsmill hails from South Aus., hut has
been in W.A. politics for donkey's years,
lie is a cheery, humorous soul, and one of
the best speakers in the State. In the old
days he was a prospector.
Sydney Telegraph calls upon every true
Fusionist to go before the Luxuries Board
and stop any potential interference with Sup-
ply and demand. Germany is trying to Witt
the war by stopping waste. The TelegrAPil
demands that Australia shall win by keeping
the leak wide open. How many people want
two guesses at what the end of such lunacy
would be? Already the importing mania has
brought Australia a long way closer to
economic ruin than the infrequent sahsage
has brought Germany. » »
Tasmania has its example, too. of the
curse of borrowlng*orgie«—the insanity
which drives Australian States to go out ort
a wild borrowing spree and then khoek Off
suddenly when the bubble bursts, leaving
railway lines half-finished and ending
nowhere. Two months’ freight receipts from
the Preolenna branch, which was to serve a
coal-mine but never got there* amount to
£2B —about £2 per mile !
“.T.G.”: tic the action of Schubert, the
New York th eatrieal manager, in shoving
ultra-effeminate males into the chorus of
musical comedy (B. 24/5/M7). This is no
new thing. Years ago a number of men
were in the chorus and ballets of U.S.
theatres. There were scores who for years
had posed as chorus girls, and hardly anyone
was the wiser. I’m told (but this I don’t
know to be dinkum) that more than one
sprightly chorus fairy over whom Aus-
tralian baldheads raved during the run of an
imported American show at one time wore
trousers.
Captain Guilfoyle, whose M.C. was pinned
on by the King at a special investiture in
Hyde Park lately, is the linest sapling
ever grown by his father, the late Curator
Guilfoyle, of Melbourne Botanical Gardens.
Young Guilfoyle’s stars were all won in the
field, and he got into the Flying Corps early
through a lucky meeting with one of the
Cecils. He married his pretty Melbourne
fiancee during a spell of leave.
“G.D.M.”: On this visit to the tJ.S.A. I
am more profoundly impressed. The Wealth
of the country overwhelms the imagination,
Vaults and trouser-pockets hold one-third
of the world’s stock of gold—nearly
£000.000.000. The national assets approxi-
mate forty thousand millions sterling; the
yearly income ten thousand million pounds,
while the public debt per head is only
£2 ss. lOd. These figures are taken from a
statement by the American Bankers’ Associ-
ation. To day this country has two-and-a-
quarter times as much wealth as Great
Britain and four times as much as France.
It is easily the richest nation in all history.
The public debt is a trifling £220 000.000;
that of Australia about £500.000.000. In 31
months the foreign trade balance has
attained the huge total of five and three-
quarter billion dollars. And yet,
give me two rooms in Anzacland, and they
can keep their billions.
A remarkable show was that got up by the
painters of London at Chelsea, the artistic
suburb, for a war charity. Ellen Terry, as
the local Spirit, introduced celebrities con-
nected with the neighborhood—all played by
carefully-made-up professional actors. The
old partnership of Swinburne, Meredith, Ros-
setti. Morris and Watts-Dunton had Carlyle
added to it, and, of all men, Hall Caine.
Australia’s old friend Eille Norwood played
Watts-Dunton. Another scene showed
Whistler painting, interrupted by Connie
Gilchrist with a skipping-rope dance. The
moderns came in with a skit on George
Moore, and a scene involving Sargent and
others, with a special song-and-dance in
honor of Augustus John.
“Braax": The saphead who has £lB to
spend on fur and doesn’t know the difference
between rabbit and seal shouldn’t go out
alone. Dozens of robberies which are less
apparent are the daily duty of the softgoods
salesman. Eiderdown quilts containing only
kapok are sold by almost every firm. Half-
cotton blankets are “beautiful all-wool
goods.” But the deadly swindle —the one
with pneumonia in it-—is the all-cotton vest
or singlet, known sweetly to the trade as
“Merino,” a name which naturally, misleads
90 per cent, of the shoppers. The meanest
one concerns good old Billjim’s socks. “Fin-
gering” was worth about 40s. a spindle until
somebody called for a hundred thousand
pairs of socks for the boys at the Front.
The last invoice I saw for the stuff was
625. a spindle.
“Rustler”: The Methodies at Moonee
Ponds (Vic.) are making the best of this
sinful world. In front of the old church in
busy Puc-kle-street they built a row of fine
two-storey shops, leaving a narrow passage
to the tabernacle at the rear. The rents
rolled in, and presently a new church was
built on another site away from the main
street, and the old building is used as a
hall. The combined rents from the hall
and shops now furnish a nice steady income
which keeps a full head of steam on the
church, praise the landlord !
“German Caps” : It always amuses me to
find a newspaper denouncing everything Ger-
man in its reading columns while proclaim-
ing its title in German text. That puzzling
type should now be scrapped, and legible
British block type substituted.

“Nerves”
Onr nerves are similar to an intri-
cate network of telegraph wires.
Controlled and nourished by a
portion of the brain —known as
the nerve centres —the delicate
threadlike nerves radiate in all
directions throughout the body.
So long as the nerve centres are
capable of continually supplying
nourishment to the nerves, the
nerves will remain strong and
healthy. But directly the nerve
centres become weakened by
overwork, worry or anxiety, they
are unable to transmit the
necessary nourishment, and the
nerves become worn out and
“on edge.”' Then it is that a sud-
den sound makes you “ jump ”
—you get irritable —you suffer
from neuralgia—you are rest-
less and depressed. In this con-
dition there is nothing to equal
dsf
The Wine of Life
Because, being a powerful nerve
food, ‘ Wincarnis ’ gets right to
the root of the trouble, and, by
creating a supply of new nerve
force, stimulates and revitalises
the whole nervous system. Try
‘ Wincarnis ’ for ‘ Nerves.’ It
is wonderful. Over 10,000
Doctors recommend it.

Every Inch a Clock
Big Ben stands
erect —feet plan-
ted firmly—hands
in place —case
gleaming brightly,
face clean, honest,
alert.
Big Ben obeys
orders. Set him for
seven he rings on
the minute.
Sturdy, strong, lit for
service. A dependable
aide—an example of the
several virtues. A trust-
worthy alarm, a precise
timekeeper. Substantial
handsome, mechanic-
ally right. He is good
through and through.
Big Ben is seven inches tall,
well built, handsome, punctual.
He has a big, easily-read dial—a cheer-
ful, deep-toned voice—large, comfortable
winding keys—a light running movement
with watchlike escapement—an alarm
that calls steadily for live minutes or
intermittently for ten. 15/- at all clock
dealers.

The Inky Way.
“Harrison O.”: There dwells a motley
horde of pests upon the Inky Way, and over-
worked sub-editors still meet them day by
day. They do not write with fountain-pens,
but stencils use instead, and so in their
obitu’ries no one is ever dead. Their vic-
tims mostly “pass away,” or else they “cross
the bar,” or “join the great majority.” The
girls they write of are not ever, say, “good-
looking,”' “pretty,” anything like that;
they’re always “prepossessing,” and “embon-
point” ’s never “fat.” And oft a “conflagra-
tion” they describe, but ne’er a “fire.” Of
“bibulants” and such-like words they seldom
seem to tire. But persons branded “bibu-
lants” are never drunk, please note; “inebri-
ated” is the word on which their authors
dote. And e’en though writing “specials” is
his temporary game, the copy of the bloke
who uses stencils is the same. If set to
write an “intro.” to the Cup he’s bound to
say, right at the start, that “All roads led
to Flemington to-day.” And if a football
struggle he reports he’ll sell his soul before
he’ll tell the public that somebody kicked a
goal. A man may “notch a sixer,” or a
“major point” may score, or, mayhap, “find
the opening.” The same dull stencil bore
will never once be guilty of referring to the
“ball” ; it’s “leather,” “sphere” or “pigskin”
if he mentions it at all. Oh! I could keep
on going thus till 1 wore out my nib- —or, as
the stencillers would say, “extend the list
ad lib”; but “pressure on our space pre-
cludes” me now from doing so, yet ere I
stop I’d like to say one other thing I know:
The stencillers without a doubt will«nention
with a hiss that I’m a poor, “disgruntled”
cow for w'l'iting stuff like this!
* * * *
“Gallery Hand” : In the desperate struggle
to make ends meet since the Federal Arbi-
tration Court laid down a more reasonable
wage for pressmen, all sorts of expedients—
some very contemptible—are being resorted
to by proprietors. Most of them will bring
their own reward, and I expect any day to
hear bitter wails, particularly from Brisbane.
There the pooling system has been put into
operation. One paper makes itself responsi-
ble for certain courts (say), another repre-
sents the syndicate at some other stock job
and proofs are swapped. This allows a man
or two to be “fired,” but it doesn’t always save
trouble or money, as the bunch found out the
other day when they all fell into the same
error. The pool system, whether worked by
the employers or the staff men, is a fool sys-
tem. There’s no check ; and a check on copy is
just as important as getting copy. Pooling
used to be extensively used in Sydney in
court work, mostly because it was impossible
for men to cover their engagements. To be
handed out five, six and even seven courts
was common, and while it was supposed to
be an office sin for a reporter to take from
any other man’s copy, the offices knew that
that was the only way the work could be
done. Some of them paid through the nose
for their acquiescence. If the Brisbane
papers escape trouble they know a lot more
than I credit'them tvitli.
* * * *
It all depends on the hand that is pulling
the public’s leg at the moment. When, the
other night, Verbrugghen, of the N.S.W. Con-
servatorium, talked about Schubert’s
“Trout” Quintet, the Sydney Telegraph’s
soulful musical reporter got on to it like
this : -
Tlie song mirrors the mind of the poet contemplat-
ing the lively trout in a clear, sparkling brook in
the sunny morning. Then comes the ensnaring
angler and the inevitable tragedy.
Down the same column a food-consuming
Goth got on to a successful cruise of the
trawler Gunundaal; and this is how he did
it: —-
One haul alone produced over 70001 b. weight of
fish. Another large haul, consisting mostly of dog-
fish, was lost when the net was at the surface, as
the weight of the dogfish broke the belly r of the net.

“Xim” : The Parkes (N.S.W.) Examiner
holds the record of being the only country
paper in the Commonwealth which refuses to
accept liquor ads. For over 20 years it has
never inserted an appeal to cumanavanother.
* * * *
When, 12 months ago, the Y.M.C.A.
wanted a man to organise a press service
through Australia primarily in the interests
of its army and navy department, and in-
directly for the benefit of the lads at the
Front, it made no search of suburban taber-
nacles for a professing Christian. It took the
applicant whose claims appeared to meet its
practical requirements, and trusted to Provi-
dence for the rest. Thus it acquired D. G.
Macdougall, a pressman with 22 years at the
game as a chief credential. A score of years
back Macdougall was a protege of F. W.
Ward —of the beard —on Brisbane Courier,
and, later, in the shadow of the same whisk-
ers, he sub-edited the Observer until he
struck the track for Melbourne.
Bullant Mclntyre, of The Southern
Cross Times (W.A.), is a mere gentle
zephyr these days. For instance:
It strikes me that the officials of our Government
departments are taking a mean advantage of me
because I am paralysed; but they had better beware
and not drive me to murder, for as sure as I live
I shall murder either the buyer of my assets or the
Minister of the Crown who sanctions my persecution.
The time is ripe for a revolution in this country,
and I shall be in it, for bitter experience tells me
that the curse of our paltry Parliaments can only be
cured by bloody deeds.

heard' from, was in the wilds of Arizona
bottling local color for a Yank novel. Mum
Dawson, Boss.” her Australian play, is to
be produced in Melbourne later in the year.
