The Vigil.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
rain is falling on the roof.
I And no sound else disturbs the wife,
Except the trees and winds at strife,
Now near at hand and now aloof;
But list’ning, leaning evermore,
She waits a knock upon the door.
Her hair is braided round her head;
Her eyes are large and fierce and bright,
Her shapely throat is soft and white ;
And on her mouth there burns the red
Of that rich, storied gem that shone
Upon the breast of Prester John.
Upon the couch her husband lies.
How is it that he lies so still ?
Why sleeps he there so pale and chill,
The lamplight on his lidded eyes?
Has she not fire and more than fire
To thrill his flesh with hot desire?
Anon she lifts her rounded arms
As though to joy that she is free;
And her large eyes exultantly
Light up, as when the dawn-glow charms
With roseate lights that gleam and glance
Twin lakes to sudden radiance.
The rain is falling on the roof,
Yet, though her ears are open wide,
There is no other sound outside —
No fall of foot, nor tramp of hoof.
And on his couch with lidded eyes
The husband, cold and pallid, lies.
The midnight sky is wild and black
And drenches earth with ceaseless tears;
And now it seems to her she hears
Hoof-strokes upon the sodden track;
And now she rises, sweet as sin,
To let the late night-strayer in.
The lamplight gleams upon his face,
And glistens on his reddened spur;
He stretches out his arms to her
And folds her in a rude embrace
How can it be the husband lies
So still, with heavy lidded eyes?
Perchance he neither sees nor hears,
And sleeps unmoved by chance or change,
And yet. .. .and yet, it seems so strange.
If he be dead there should be tears.
Not love nor smiles, nor midnight bliss,
Nor mbuths that marry in a kiss.
The loud winds thrust upon the door,
The raindrops drum upon the roof,
The trickles from a waterproof
Make little pools upon the floor;
But two in one—the world apart —
They stand, heart throbbing back to heart.
Anon she says: “He died this morn.
He did not die a whit too soon ;
Life’s day, alas, makes towards its noon.
He should have died when love was born.
He should have died long since. And now
Kiss me again—my mouth, my brow!”
The winds no more the woods arouse,
The rain has ceased, the night is still;
Her peaked chin resting on a hill,
The moon looks down upon the house.
Wherein, stiff-limbed, cold lies the dead
What time Love whispers by his bed.
Indifferent with marble brow
He lies while hour on hour goes by ;
He does not see the lovers nigh.
Nor hear the deathless love they vow.
Did he so heed perchance a smile
Would flit across his face the while.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Postponed Hour.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was five minutes to two on the
morning of New Year’s Day when
John Nisbet gingerly let himself into
the hall. He had fearfully noticed that the
light was still on in the bedroom. All the
way home from seeing the New Year in—
or two New Years in, John wasn’t
certain which—he had hoped that his wife
would be asleep. He had sworn off; he was
manfully, though in a rather wobbling way,
following the King; but this night he saw
so many tracks to folfow that he was slightly
confused. And now, as he hung up his
latch-key aud tried to insert his hat in the
keyhole, his last hope of a restful night
was gone. That light in the bedroom shone
with a baleful glare: it reminded him hor-
ribly of his wife’s accusing glance.
And then he dimly heard a curious sound.
It was not his wife snoring. It was a dull
noise, rather like the vague sound that pre-
cedes an earthquake. He braced himself
unsteadily; but there was no earthquake.
Then he" heard another curious sound, a
whizzing sound that reverberated through
the silence. It seemed to come from every-
where ; it filled the night with its eerie
whisper. He pulled out his watch; the
time was 2 a.m.
“Daylight Shaving Bill!” he muttered
thickly, and unsteadily switched the hour
hand round to 3. The faint noise that the
revolving hand made seemed like a ghostly
echo of the mysterious whisper in the air.
Then with nine explanations on his lips,
he carefully opened the bedroom door. The
room was in darkness! And when he
clumsily lit a match, he found his wife sound
asleep. , ,
/‘Shaved !” he thickly muttered. Shaved
by Daylight Shaving Bill!”
The same night Reggie Bithell was seeing
Lena Waistcoate home. It had been a jolly
dance, and Reggie had toasted the New
Year several times. So had Lena. During
that hilarious supper Reggie found himselt
contemplating Lena with a new interest.
He had always thought her a pretty girl,
nice and- cosy to flirt with. But Reggie
was a wise youth: he wasn’t going to tie
himself up in marriage for years yet; what
he was after was a good time. At the supper
everybody 7 had been intensely patriotic. Some
of the patriotism, or maybe it was some ot
the wine, had fired Reggie’s heart, and
during the extra “extras” that followed —
all of which Reggie had with Lena he said
things to her that might well have set the
brains of a clever little thing like Lena
Waistcoate thinking rosily about her future.
Yet when Reggie found that somehow it had
been arranged that he would walk home
with Lena, he felt a shudder of fear. In
that mood, and with such a nice girl as
Lena by his side, he would have to be caie-
ful, very careful, not to commit himself
The next thing, if he didn't take a pull
on himself, he would find himself proposing,
to her. So as they set out he summoned
all his resources to steer a straight course,
for safety. It was so late, too. As they
came to that lonely part of the road he
looked at his watch. It was a few minutes
to two. Then, suddenly, Lena stumbled
over a stone, and would have fallen if he
had not caught her in his arms. And while
he held her the Town Hall clock struck
two. Or was it three? In his muddled con-
dition he could not be sure.
The next thing that happened was a
whizzing sound in his ears, and then Lena
kissed him comprehensively on the mouth.
And, still with that buzzing noise in his
head —or was it in th§ stars? —he heard
Lena saving. “You’ve made me so happy,
Reggie, darling. I never knew you cared
for me.”
Strangely enough, he did care; and he
told her so. Ills kiss was even more com-
prehensive than hers. And with a strange
wonder in their hearts they went on. At
the door there was a prolonged farewell;
and Reggie as he turned away heard Lena s
mother sleepily saying:
“llow late you are, Lena! Why, it s
after three!”
* * * *
Constable John Chuck was on his beat
that same night. He had seen the hew
Year in without the assistance of any liquid.
The only happiness in his mind was the
thought that owing to all the clocks being
put on at 2 a.m. he would be relieved an
hour sooner. It was just a few minutes
before two that he saw a figure, bent beneath
a bulging sack, emerge unostentatiously
from a back gate and stroll casually down
the street. In an instant John Chuck had
started in pursuit, but the stranger evidently
preferred his own company, and began to
run.
The clock struck the hour, and with the
blood singing in his ears the policeman
stopped. His prey had vanished from his
sight. Curious! There was no doorway
into which he could have escaped. But there
was no sign of man or sack. “A clean
getaway!” he sighed, perplexed. He felt
dizzy and disgusted. Then his puzzled eyes
sought the face of a clock. “Past three!”
he muttered. “Be the saints, hev I been
chasin’ ’im fer an hour !” Then he laughed,
and taking out his watch, swung the hour
hand round to the new time.
That night, just on two o’clock, the young
wife of Herbert Smithers stirred in her
sleep. Her husband slept calmly on; but
her mother’s heart had heard her baby cry.
Half-asleep she leaned across to the dainty
cot beside her bed', to soothe her darling
and tuck the clothes about him. Then the
chime of the dining-room clock woke her
fvillv. Instinctively she counted the deep
bell-like strokes —one, two, three, And bend-
ing across her babe she found him peace-
fully. deeply asleep, with all the clothes
smooth and even. She paused an instant
in wonder. In her sleep she had distinctly
heard her baby cry—a lusty cry, a wide-
awake cry. But there was a curious buzz-
ing in her ears, and happily she laid her
head on the pillow again, with the thought
that she must have dreamed that her child
was awake.
* * * *
For all these persons, as for all Australia,
that hour had gone. Gone where? You
can’t snip an hour out of Time and expect
to find no hole left. True, the edges had
been evenly joined; everybody had put clocks
and watches on; but Time himself cannot
be deceived by such a childish .expedient
as that. Australia didn’t want that hour;
had deliberately refused to have that hour;
but even Australia couldn’t monkey with
such a slow and stately thing as Time..
“Very well,” said Time, hurt by this ill-
treatment of himself. “You think you can
get ahead of me by whizzing the hour hand
round ; but I won’t forget. Some day you’ll
want that hour back. Rll keep it somewhere
hidden from you ; I’ll wrap it up carefully
with moth-balls, and when you’re ready to
have it again I’ll hand it back just as it
was when you threw it out. And. remember,
it won’t be the hour you expect; but the
very hour you’ve refused to have. You pre-
tend that you haven’t missed that hour; but
you will. You think it comfortably mislaid
or conveniently destroyed; but you can’t
obliterate Time —not even so small a frac-
tion of it as GO seconds. That hour is safely
filed away; some day I’ll turn it up. It’s
not lost, merely postponed.”
John Nisbet was so grateful to the Day-
light Saving Bill for having let him off th<
wifely lecture on New Year morning that h(
swore off. His persistent sobriety amazec
his wife. After a couple of months of it it
began to annoy her. In the old year John’s
occasional jags had afforded her the oppor
tunity to go on a moral burst herself. She
got as drunk on moral indignation as John
had ever been on whisky. Every happily-
married woman needs such occasional emo-
tional outlets; arid, lacking the excuse for
wifely reproaches, married life becomes hor-
ribly decorous and horribly dull.
On the evening of March 24, John spent
a happy evening with his wife, discussing the
war. But at 3 a.m. he suddenly awoke.
There was a curious creaking noise in his
ears, as if somebody was winding a gigantic
clock backwards. He instantly remembered
that this was the extra hour provided in
order to switch back to solar time. Sleepily
he put his watch back to 2 a.m., comforting
himself with the reflection that he would have
an extra hour in bed. But the noise he
made —or was it the noise of a million
watches being similarly ill-treated? —woke his
wife.
“You’re drunk, John!” she indignantly
snorted. “Disgracefully drunk, again !”
“Nonshense, dearie,” John muttered
thickly. He was surprised to find that he

had some difficulty iu expressing himself.
Jushd sheein’ goo’ ole New Year in !”
‘‘New Year ! Why. it’s the end of March !”
his wife exclaimed.
Somehow she felt a thrill of satisfaction.
Now r she knew what she had been missing
these last few mouths, the sensation of moral
superiority that only comes to a wife when
her husband is drunk.
John felt, strangely confused. He at-
tempted to explain that he wasn’t drunk,
couldn’t possibly be drunk. Yet he knew
that he was drunk. Delightfully,
drunk.' Too drunk to care what his wife
said. So for an hour—that heaven-sent
"Xtra hour—-he lay there and was pleasantly
lulled by his wife’s moral monologue.
Next morning his wife was her charming
s, 'Jf again ; she had had her emotional spree.
And John, seeing that whether he drank or
not he was liable *to become intoxicated,
decided that he would not get drunk without
a cause. lie made up his mind to follow
<Jeorge the Fifth some distance behind; and
his wife, at seemly intervals, wept over his
lapses, and was emotionally comforted.
* * * *
Reggie Bithell and Lena Waistcoate had
quarrelled. Their brief engagement had been
broken ; they swore never to speak to each
other again. When Reggie woke the next
morning lie recognised that Lena’s action in
kissing him before he had kissed her, was
hardly modest. Of course, he loved her, and all
that, and apparently he was engaged to her,
though, curiously enough, he could not re-
member having actually proposed. That
cloud on his happiness slowly spread, over-
shadowing all." And when he hinted to Lena
that lie had forgotten the details of their
actual engagement she had had to confess,
too, that the memory of that precious hour
bad also been obliterated from her memory.
When he had almost angrily accused her of
having given the first kiss, Lena hotly con-
tradicted him, though at the back of her
brain slie could not recall anything that had
happened before that fatal embrace. So they
parted in anger.
But one night in March it fell to his lot
again to see her home from a dance. When
they found themselves thus thrust upon each
other’s company by a hostess who was
ignorant of their changed relations, each was
too proud to refuse, and in angry silence
they started to walk to her home. 'But just
as they reached the fatal spot of the botched
proposal, Lena again stumbled, and Reggie,
catching her once again in his arms, sud-
denly felt all his unjust suspicions disappear.
Eagerly he poured out his love for her, and,
after a solemn interval, Lena happily mur-
mured: “Reggie, darling, this is so'sudden!”
It wasn’t, as a matter of fact; it was
three months later. But neither knew that.
To them it was a reconciliation, a wonder-
ful hour of reconciliation. And though,
when three o’clock struck, Reggie remem-
bered that he would have to put his watch
back, it was Lena who lovingly did it for
him.
. “9 Reggie,”• She whispered, “wouldn’t it be
lovely it we could only have this heavenly
hour again?”
But they couldn't. There had been quite
enough monkeying with Time. He had to
get on.
* * * -*
About the same time a prisoner in a cell,
convicted of a number of burglaries, woke in
the darkness. He guessed it must be some-
where about three o’clock, and then remem-
bered that the Judge,, or God, or somebody,
had meanly added an hour to his already
long sentence. Yes, owing to that blanky
Daylight Saving arrangement, he was going
to get an extra hour —in quod. If he could
only have had that extra hour outside!
He did. The warder, looking into the cell,
discovered that his prisoner was missing.
The door was locked : there was no sign of
his escape except the obvious one that the
man wasn’t there. He roused the gaol
authorities, and a thorough search was made
of the premises, without the slightest clue
being discovered. After an hour’s investiga-
tion they gave it up; and the warder, re-
turning to the cell to have a final look round,
discovered his prisoner there, iu bed. And
there was a happy, reminiscent smile on his
face.
At the same hour ex-Constable John
Chuck, now I*te. Chuck, was mounting guard
in camp. The officer for the day, making
his night inspection, found one sentry miss-
ing. It was Pte. Chuck. Search failed to
disclose his whereabouts, which wasn’t at all
remarkable, since just as he put Ids wrist-
watch back the extra hour, he found himself,
in a blue uniform, chasing a suspicious-look-
ing person down a suburban street. It was
a hot chase, lasting nearly an hour, but the
man got away. However, Constable Chuck
had got near enough to the man to identify
him; and he comforted himself with the
thought that some day the Law would catch
the man for one of his many burglaries.
Then he resumed the pacing of his beat, but
had some difficulty in refraining from pre-
senting arms to a palpable drunk. As the
clock struck three, for the second time that
night, he found himself back on sentry-go in
camp, this time in khaki. Next morning he
got the proud distinction of being able to
write C.B. after his name.
* * » «
As the dining-room clock struck three, for
the first time that night the wife of Herbert
Smithers woke. She thought she had heard
her baby cry. She leant over to the cot, and,
only half-awake, soothed the restless child.
But the baby wouldn’t be pacified until she
had taken him into her arms, and, crooning
old nursery rhymes, so as not to wake her
husband, lulled the little thing to sleep.
Lying there, happy with the feel of her baby
against her breast, she fell at last to sleep.
In the morning she turned instinctively to
her baby’s cot. But, of course, there was
no cot there. It had been taken away when
her baby had died. Her husband, seeing the
instinctive movement of the mother bereaved,
gently put his arms about her. And she
woke —to the bleak realisation of her irre-
parable loss.
“Dear,” she whispered through her slow
tears, “I had a beautiful dream last night.
I dreamed that baby was sleeping in his little
cot. And he was restless, and to soothe him
I had to take him into our bed. and I sang
him the old nursery rhymes he loved, and at
last I fell asleep with him in my arms.”
There was nothing her husband could say.
“But it was so real, dear,” she sobbed.
“Do you think the little chap could have
come back to me—-just for an hour—because
he knows how dreadfully I miss him?”
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
As It Was.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ST. JOHN of the pearling fleet was At
Home. All the Island was aboard his
schooner. The men (who one and all
hated St. John) came because it was “busi-
ness,” and the women because there was no-
where else to go.
All went merry as a marriage bell. The
scarlet-striped awnings and long white line
of decks were cool and inviting. The Filipino
Jboys made quaint music on their mandolins;
and no one had ever questioned St. John’s
qualification in giving the finest of foods
and drinks.
Tony Savoran lent over the rail beside the
girl he liked best, and helped her count the
forty-seven sapphires in the Strait.
Tony liked her best because she was dif-
ferent, and had brown little flecks in the
innocence of her eyes. And she, Denise
Wayne, loved Tony for being himself and a
dear, and because he was big and straight
and strong.
“Say those verses about Torres,” she
demanded. And Tony, obedient, began:
Valdez del Torres sailed the main,
He was a great grandee of Spain,
and told how disaster and hunger and
dread ghosts harassed him, till the Southern
Cross came a-comforting.
“Three years this day we’ve sailed the main,
We pray you take us back to Spain,”
the crew besought. Then the mutiny and
the scurvy-sickness and lastly:
When they laid them down to die
The starried cross rose in the sky.
Tony waxed rhetorical. He almost swore
when Ah Mat, Malay, in a fine new batik
turban, curved towards them with a salver
of caged sunshine in slender-stemmed glass.
A dinghy put off from the little stone
jetty. A nice dinghy it was, white with a
red rim. A Iviwai boy was pulling. A
Japanese sat, very straight, astern. As he
passed the schooner he bowed, the stiff-
necked bow of the High Nobility.
“He’s the best-looking Jap I’ve seen,” said
Denise, ineonsequentl.v.
“Saruma, the diver,” averred Tony. And
just then St. John carried Denise off to show
her some pearls, and didn’t include Tony.
* * * *
Night, and a week later. A drab miasma
swathed the island and lay like a tide-wave
from Horn to Prince o’ Wales.
Tony and this Girl he Liked Best wefe
gar-fishing off the reef. Two hours had
they fished and caught next to nothing. Then
a shark’s fin rising so close that Denise
could have touched it told them why. No
one cares to be off the reef when the great
sharks are out. So Tony and the Girl
stowed their fishing-gear and made for the
Sidela boat-house that stands straight and
strong (like Tony himself, thought Denise)
on the sand-beach.
Monsoon season was three moons past,
yet a sudden squall screamed over the edge
of Green Fort, seized the dinghy, and flung
it like a walnut shell half across the strait,
leaving it wallowing beside a lugger that
pitched like a thing possessed. When it
steadied down Denise and Tony went
aboard, to escape the rain that struck into
(?ie sea like spears. Saruma the diver in-
vited them.
His was a well-composed cabin. A red
silk rug lay neatly across the bunk, and a
beautifully-done crayon showed the Mikado
taking a salute.
“You will drink tea?” Saruma asked.
Denise just loved the little handleless cups
of porcelain, with storks in dead black ; the
quaint squat teapot; the flat wee jug. And
the purple iris above the swinging lamp was
good to look upon.
Tony laughed that she was so young and
so happy, and drank two cups.
He smiled over the bushikan (orange peel,
sugared) and kept on smiling in a silly sort
of way (which wasn’t a bit like TonV). Then,
suddenly, his head fell forward. Denise was
smiling, too, in the same witless way, from
afar. And an anchor-chain creaked on its
windlass.
Then Tony waked. lie stood up—with
unseen hands dragging him down—and
faced the diver.
“You!” —he gasped. “You” !
The diver bowed.
“Once,” he said, “five year ago, you swore
me, and you kicked. I called to her. I
take”
“Tony!” Denise cried in little convulsive
gasps. “Tony—save me !”
But the world had gone velvet-black, and
Tony was falling through.
Their dinghy was found floating bottom
up; and the good Padre of the Roman
Catholic Church jumped at conclusions and
read prayers for our dear brother and sister
now departed. Also for Saruma, a Japan-
ese, of the Lone Star lugger,- and his
crew, lost in the same squall.
Tony’s watch came back in the belly of
a shark.
Three moons later word came that a white
girl had been seen aboard a pearling lugger
in the Philippines. Investigation showed
that this white woman was very, very mad.
All the time she was singing something
about:
“Three years this day we’ve sailed the main,
We pray you take us back to Spain.”
Which proved she was. Spanish, not Aus-
tralian. One night she fell overboard and
was drowned.
•Which is a rotten yarn, but true.
S. S. SEVERN.
